THE RED PAGE.
N.S.W.
Oh, to begin again 1
Not from the first, but now,
Letting the threads fall where
Tangles too bitter grow.
Oh, to begin, and look
Straight into everything 1
Seeing without the beam
Of old traditioning.
How would I see thee, Death ?
Mother with'melting eyes,
Waiting to waft her child
Into some Paradise,
Whether of rest or joy,
Laughter or solitude,
Just what would satisfy
Each child’s unspoken mood.
Mother with loving lips,
Calling, in tenderness,
All the worlds unto her,
Even the motherless.
How would I see thee, Love ?
Casting tradition’s beam
Out of my eyes. Ah, Love !
Swift little fiery gleam,
Faded before it breaks
Into the heart’s recess,
Where the black shadows link
In languid loneliness.
Faded and passed away,
Turning the shadows there,
Colder and deadlier
After that golden glare.
How would I see thee, Truth?
Fought for so patiently :
Ah, for thfi wounded Hands !
Ah, for the Agony !
Must I behold in thee
Only a sacrifice
jEons have perished for,
War-time and armistice.
Wonderful sacrifice !
Yes, and I come, I come,
Low to the altar steps,
Farewell to hope and home !
Seeing without the beam,
Of old traditionings.
. . . Brighten the altar fire !
Tighten the victim's strings !

The Disappearance of Car 8809.
it’s a sure thing this ain’t a fake ? ” asked
, Jimmy Horgan of the lawyer.
" “ Perfectly- sure,” was the smiling re-
sponse. “Your uncle left you the $5OOO
without restrictions of any kind, and it is in the.
bank awaiting your orders now.”
“Say,” said Jimmy, “I don’t seem to savvy
tliis thing somehow—could you let me have two
hundred so’s I can kind of feel it ? ”
“Certainly.” Think of that answer to such a
request! James gasped, and when a few minutes
later he walked down the steps with twenty crisp,
succulent, legal-tender ten-dollar bills in his pocket
he wondered who the man was that his legs were
carrying along so jauntily.
That was the beginning of the great mystery -
not the germ exactly, but the place where the
germ began : The Disappearance of Car Number
8809.
How so large and eminently practical a thing as
a trolley-car —a thing so blatantly modern and un-
imaginative and withal so hard and heavy—could
vanish from the face .of the earth and leave neither
track nor wrack behind, is a problem that has
caused silver threads to appear amid the gold-and-
bald spots of the-officers of the Suburban Trolley
Company.
With it went motorman and conductor ; gone,
vanished, dissipated into thin air. The whole
business was, and then it was n’t; that is all they
knew about it.
The facts in the case are these:
“ Say, Billy,” said James to his conductor, when
he arrived in the yard, “here’s Willy Waldy
Astor, and that’s me,” whereupon he poked him-
self in the chest with, a dramatic forefinger.
“ Huh ! ” sniffed the conductor. “ Somebody’s
grounded his wire again ! Where do you feel it
worst ? ”
“No, b’gosh! Pm givm’ it to you straight,”
said James, earnestly, and then he poured his tale
of sudden 'fortune into Billy’s ears, to a running
fire of “Ah, gwan !■” of wonder and admiration.
“Now,” he concluded, “we’ve got fifteen
minutes to skip over to Jimmy’s and celebrate,
before we pull out.”
That was the germ. Long before the fifteen
minutes had expired, they found that they were
sick of trolley-roads. Sick is a poor word ; they
were belligerently opposed to the trolley-road.
The germ sprouted.
“I’m dead tired of goin’ over the same old
streets,” said Jimmy. “Sometimes it seems to
me .that I’d bafter turn off som’ers or bust. Say,
if you ’ll go me on it, we ’ll take the old ambulance
clear to the end of everything this morning.
There’s more’n forty thousand switches we can
hit, and they can’t stop us. I ’ll back you if you
lose your job. What do you say ? ”
“- Shoot ! ” answered the conductor. “ I’ve got
you faded. 1 don’t know as I care if I
never work again, and as for jerkin’ fares for
these people, why” , and then followed a
rapid and vigorous description of the officers of the
Suburban Trolley Co., from Billy’s point of view.
In the meanwhile they started for the car.
“ Come, you fellers - ! ” yelled the starter. “Get
a move on. You ought,to be out now ! ”
“ W.e’re cornin’, Uncle,” said Jim.
“Give him the bell, Billy,” said the starter.
The conductor pulled the strap. “Adoo! Fare-
well ! Good-bye, Reddy ! ” he called. If we
don’t see you again, hello ! ”
The starter waved his hand and smiled per-
functorily. He was used to these small jokes, and
had no thought that the words were loaded with
meaning. .
8809 droned merrily along the track, until she
came to the first switch.
“Give us the Squarebush line, Jerry 1” said
James.
The melancholy man jabbed Ins iron into the
track. Squarebush, North Pole, or infernal
regions, it was all one to him. “ Come along,” he
growled. .
“Hey there! Hey!” cried an excited old
gentleman, as the car shot up the switch. I
thought this car went through Lethe-street ? ”
“ It use! io.” answered Billy, soothingly, “but
it’s got tired of it.
“ Tired of it l Tired ? ” said the old gentleman
blankly. “Here, let me out!” he concluded
with energy.
“A lunatic —no doubt of it,” he added to him-
self, as he watched the car depart. Then he
waited a weary while on the corner—in vain, for
no other car would be sent forth until 8809 passed
a certain turn-out which she had not the least idea
of even approaching this day—and that ruptured
the schedule.
A sour-faced young man with a fighting jaw
boarded the car a few blocks further on.
“Say, do youse go t’rough Scrabble Grass
Avenue ! ” he asked in a voice like a curse.
“ Now, that depends,” answered the blithe
William. “If we want to, we will; if we don’t,
we wont —d’ yer feel like makin’ it an object to
us ? ”
“Say, you’re a pretty fresh duck,” sneered the
sour-faced man. Then he donned his most fero-
cious look. “Now, I want to go to Scrabble
Grass Avenue,” said he, glowering, “and the
quicker I git there, the better ’t’ll be.”
“Sure!’ answered William, heartily. “Of
course!” and he stopped the car. “Now then,
you are right over there —get a move on you, too,
for I don’t like your face.”
Sometimes a fighting jaw merely implies a
fighting character, and does n’t insist upon it.
“D’yer mean 1 ’ve got ter walk?” asked the
sour-faced man in a milder tone.
“Sure thing—-unless you can slide it. Come,
get a waggle on you, or I’ll kick you half-way
there.” . .. . . .
“Say, what’s got into you this morning?”
gasped the stranger.
It was Billy’s turn to scoff. “ You ort to read
the papers,” said ne, “ then you would n’t act like
such a lobster. Tilings ain’t run like tney used to
be, my friend; me and my pardner has bought
this car, and we ’re runnin’ it around gettin’ cus-
tom where we can.”
“Ain’t there any more railroad company!”
“Nope,” was the prompt answer. “The hull
business is in the hands of private parties like us
—and we ’re losin’ money on you by the minute,
so step off, please.”
For several hours after this, 8819 had a peculiar
career. Slie stopped for passengers like a well-
ordered trolley car, but if William did not like the
looks of people as they drew near, she bounded
ahead like an antelope.
Then there was a rive mile trip on a branch line,
merely to accommodate a pallid little woman with
a big oa by.
Jim escorted her to her destination several
blocks from the trolly-line, leaving 8809 to block
the mils.
“! ! ! !! ! ! !! ! ” said the motorman of the
car that belonged there. “Go on !Goon ! What
d’ yer mean by sitting there like a toad in a rain-
storm, holdin’ us up, when we ’re twenty minutes
late a’ready ? ” "
William spread his hands in deprecation. “ It’s
orders -! can’t help it,” said he.
“Orders !! ! What orders? What are yer
doin’ in this part of the country, anyhow ? ”
“ Old man Rockyfellbr has come over to call on
an old woman that useter cook for him, and the
compny’s give him all rights on this car—my
mate's takin’ him around to the house own.
If he wants to come back with us you ’ll have to
wait —that’s all.”
The other leaned back in astonishment and fury.
“Well, wouldn’t that knock you?” said he.
“.Them rich old devils can have anything they
want, no matter what breaks, can’t they ? ”
“That ’s about the size of it-but here comes
Jim—we ’ll spin ahead and turn out for you up
above.”
“ Thankee, pardner,” said the mollified motor-
man. “I’m obliged to yer, but I kin tell yer
one thing. I’m goin’ to join the ancient and
honorable order of amalgamated Anarchists this
night. Aou bet. Call on his cook and block the
whole line ! Well, ”
But 8809 had gone on, rushing to her fate.
As she sped down Poolton Avenue a party of
young men with long hair rushed out of a cafe,
yelling wildly.
“ Pull up, .Jim ! ” said Billy. “ They look like
our land.”
“Where do you go?” asked the first panting
youth.
“ Any old place.”
“Hey?”
• “That’s what—we’ve broke loose and we’re
takin’ in the country to suit ourselves.”
“ C’m on, fellers ! ” snouted the young man.
“We’re strictly in it. Say, are you giving it to
me straight ?” ! . ,
“Read our signs,” answered William. The
young man guffawed. “ .Say, this is great! Have
a drink ? ”
William took the proffered flask and partook
freely. So also did J ames, coming back from the
platform to do so. Left to herself, 8809 rammed
an Italian’s banana cart, scattering the yellow
fruit to the winds.
“Got to pay for what you break,” said Jim,
and he heaved two silver dollars at the vender,
“You’re the right kind, pardner,” said the
young man. “ Have another drink with me—l
insist.”
“Oh ! you needn’t strain yourself,” answered
James; “ I can be coaxed.”
Amid the hilarity one young man began to ring
up fares.
“Hey, what cher doin’!” shouted‘Billy in the
grip of habit. Then he remembered. “Go
ahead ! ” said he. “No harm done.” The register
rang—the signal bell rang—both gongs rang. The
people stared, and the horses stood on their hind
legs.
The first young man was quite carried away
with excitement. He clambered over the seats to
the front platform. “Here, let me run this old
shebang,” said he. “I won’t kill nobody.”
“Well, we’re in the country now,” answered
Jim. “ I guess you won’t do much damage.” So
he gave him a few pointers and joined the revellers
in the seats.
Fifteen minutes sped by, when they were
startled by a yell from the young man, who at the
same moment began to grind frantically at the
brake, the huge muscles of his back nearly split-
ting the coat in his effort.
Jumping to their feet, the party saw ahead of
them a sharp incline ending in an unprotected
bridge.
“Jee-roo-saluin !” shouted Jim. “Wood’s
Bridge—the worst place in the hull country ! I
forgot it ! ”
At the same instant there was a snap and a
jingle of metal as the brake broke. The car,
which had slacked a little in its speed, leaped
ahead again.
“Turn off your power—reverse, I mean!”
yelled Jimmy. Then there was a thudding sound
on the car-roof. “ Oh, Lord !” he groaned, “ the
trolley’s jumped.”
Billy had spotted a long haystack not five feet
off.
“ Quick, boys !” he yelled. “ Get on the step
and jump ! ”
They were upon it in a twinkle, and every man
leapt.
Over and over they rolled, but they struggled to
heir feet to see the end.
t
8809 ran down the grade, bucking like a mus-
tang. She took the bridge _ with a rush, popped
high in the air, and, turning completely over,
went down into twenty feet of water —trucks up—
with a tremendous splash.
There was a moment of appalled silence.
“ She’s gone,” said James, solemnly.
“Everlastingly,” assented Billy.
“ And now I reckon we’d better go, too.”
“ Right you are ! ” said Billy. “ You ’ll excuse
us, friends, but we’ve got business away from
here.”
“Sure!’’said the college boys. “Good-bye.
We’ve had a great time.”
“ Good-bye ! ” shouted the trolley men as they
hustled through the fields.
And that was the last that anyone ever saw of
8809.

Our ’bus-driver had a jibber in t’ other day, and there
was the usual halt. After vain floggings, Tim, the driver,
fervently ejaculated—“ O you cow l ” A lively lady
inside remarked to her female friend —“ What a singular

the death of a prominent citizen the day previously.
An old Irishman, who had been silent all the time, rose to
go with the remark, “ Yis, sure, there’s men dying this
year that never died before.”

few minutes.” “ Well, I don't want it then. But if I
could n’t have got it I wanted it at once.”

“ Anarchist” Andrews as Inventor.
J. A. Andrews writes to The Bulletin
The journal printed in 1891 with movable type
cut on slips of cigar-box wood, of which some
rare copies are to-day kept as curiosities, was a
work of patience rather than ingenuity ;' however,
it is true that I have always been Somewhat of an
amateur inventor. When I was about 19 years
old my room was a complicated curiosity; I had
altered all the locks of the apartment and contents,
covered the door with a network of strange devices
for opening, closing, regulating;, &c., fitted the
Venetian blind with an automatic adjuster made
of the neck of a powder-horn, and covered the walls
with a multitude of weird designs in luminous
paint, upon which I could fix lights and shadows,
and from which evolve the colors of the rainbow,
by a process of photography which I think is still
my own secret. For the benefit of those who
may desire to produce Luminous Photographs
I may as well explain. To take a temporary
picture is a mere matter of casting light and shade
on a dark surface of the paint. The light develops
luminosity where it falls. This picture fades in a
couple of days if left in the dark (as is necessary to
prevent it from becoming a uniform mass of light)
and can only be renewed by taking afresh. Now,
to “fix” it, my plan was to expose as for photo-
graphic printing for several weeks together. The
parts made luminous by the action of light became
gradually exhausted, whilst those kept dark
remained fresh and capable of shining; so that
there was at length produced a picture capable for
a long time of being developed by merely taking
the plate into general light. Colour I produced
by veiling the paint and exposing the portion
intended to be red to red (strontium) light only,
and so on successively ; but the coloured luminosity
is relatively feeble, and endures only for a few
hours. I did not attempt “fixing ” colour effects,
but they might perhaps be “fixed” on luminous
paint by complementary exhaustion.
About this time I took compassion on the many
would-be poets and invented a Poetical Machine.
to consist of movable bars of rubber type, governed
by the very mechanism Which somebody has since
thought of for making the linotype replace its
matrices. All that the aspirant would have to do
would be to select his rhymes from a list exhibited
on the machine, place them in whatever order he
desired, and turn a handle, whereupon the
machine would print verses accordingly. Of
course the maker would have to stock the machine
with lines that could be read in any order and
always make some vague approach to sense, or to
the semblance of it. This—who knows’-some
enterprising manufacturer may yet put on the
market for the relief of millions of love-sick
swains, popular-song writers, and prominent poli-
-I<More likely to be welcomed by practical people
troubled with Jumping Gas-Light is the idea that
I hit upon when that nuisance interrupted my
writing. I took off the burner and put on a piece
of rubber tube, which I led into a basin of water
placed on the table. The burner I screwed into
the bottom of an old dipper -without a handle, and
filling this dipper with. water 1 put a sheet of
cardboard over the mouth and inverted it in the
basin, withdrawing the cardboard. Ihe gas rose
into the dipper ; the force of the spurts was spent
in raising the dipper, and the weight of the latter
then maintained the outflow of gas by the burner
in the intervals when less or none came through
the tube. The elevation and depression of the
dipper were not noticeable in amount, and the
burner emitted a steady flame perfectly free from
TEX Greatly-Improved Gaslight by merely
taking a common brass paper-fastener of the
round-headed kind, and attaching it by the legs
around the burner, making the head come over so
as to just catch and spread out from its curved
surface —-placed at about the angle of an italic
parenthesis mark [ ) ] —the escaping gas. This
gives a great increase of light practically free of
Here is a wrinkle which I frequently adopted
for Carrying Ink Without a Bottle, and which
may prove handy to a business man or clerk who
would be convenienced by being able to transfer
a small portion of copying-ink, for instance,
from the office to his abode. I used to gum
the inside of a common match-box, and the
outside of the part that the lid fits on to ; and
then having filled and closed the box, 1 gummed a
strip of paper round the seam. I have earned a
gallon of ink by matchboxfuls in my pocket in
this way without leakage, walking two miles.
In later times, my devices have been too closely
connected with my particular circumstances to be,
in the main, of practical interest to anyone else ;
but there are a few exceptions. It is just an off-
chance if a traveller in a railway carriage when the
lamp goes out (a familiar occurrence on the New
Soutn Wales lines) happens to be provided with a
candle ; but being so situated I once overwhelmed
a fellow-passenger with a most unreasonable
amount of astonishment by a very simple expedient
for not having to hold the candle in one’s hand. Of
course it could not be cemented down with its own
grease, on account of the jarring. What I did
was merely to cut two holes at opposite sides of a
common match-box, close to the bottom, pass a
small pocxet-Knife through, with a blade, open at
each end ; and then, setting the blades'at right
angles with the handle, I drove them under the
foot of the iron upright rising from the back of a
seat, between two semi-compartments; and there
was a socket made and fixed in a few moments. I
should not remember such. a. trifle but for the
ludicrous enthusiasm displayed by the fellow-
traveller in question, who on recovering his breath
swore that lie “ had travelled all through the
United States and never seen a, trick like that.”
Water from Fire is a possibility that,ought not
to be overlooked in a dry country like Vves-
tralia, where perhaps persons might travel with a
portable condenser and fail to find anything to
distil. I have produced a good deal of water
from flame. Any fuel that contains hydrogen—
tiiat is, practically, any but pure charcoal or
anthracite—will produce steam while burning. It
is a very common experience in winter to place a
billy of cold water on the fire, and for some time
afterwards to have drops pouring off the outside
at a rate which threatens the fire with extinction.
These drops are formed by the condensation,
partly of moisture evaporated from the fuel, but
principally of that newly formed by combustion of
Hydrocarbons. In case of necessity, then, water
could be obtained in sufficient quantity to
save life wherever the escape draught of a blaze
could be drawn off through a cooler. I used to
obtain water for moistening a postage stamp, etc.,
by holding a piece of massive brass momentarily
over the flame o,f a candle. _
If I ever have the means to bring it out I shall
put forward a machine which can be manufactured
to sell uuder £5, for casting and setting up type,
one letter at a time. It would not be nearly as
quick as the linotype, etc., but it would be a good
thing for the small printers. Probably before the
chance comes nay way, somebody else will invent
it independently.
Meanwhile, I am gravely meditating a machine
for writing penny dreadfuls. A profound study
of Frank Beades, JDeadwood Dicks, and luck
Carters, has convinced me that it is possible to
turn out these indispensable adjuncts of civilisa-
tion wholly by machinery. The author need only
be employed to write one key work, so to speak,
and the talented machine will produce from that
one hundred and forty-four distinct and different
MSS., all superior to the original.
I have signed here, using a pen in the ordinary
way, and afterwards smudged the signature over
with my thumb dipped in ink, to make the writing
plain. The signature is white; in fact, the unaltered
color of the paper. If you would like to sign your
letters in white that way, try it. It is easy, and does
not require a visit to. the chemist’s. The paper show-
ing up in the signature is bare as before the signa-
ture was written. Well, if you want to puzzle
your friends, sign with melted candle-grease, and,
after laying on the smudge, take out the paraffin
or stearine by heat. That’s all.

Red Wine, My Love.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
This is the story : the woman, his wife,
Grew tired of his arms and threw him over;
Love is the salt of a woman’s life,
And her world lacks taste without a lover.
He married beneath him, the story ran,
His friends applauding with stifled wishes;
Love is like meat to the heart of man,
Though his banquet-board bears other dishes.
She liked him a little, his gold meant much,
A carriage and pair, the latest dresses,
A sphere above her (now out of touch),
A place of stars, and stars in her tresses.
An overlord of his heart was she,
And various voices spoke her praises,
Till last the moon, the stars, and the sea
Had less delicious lights and phases.
One would think that so fair a prize
Had need of a careful ward and warning,
But, no, his faith was of such a size
As met these thoughts with an idle scorning.
She wore his jewels, his gold, his name,
But suffered him with a sleeping passion,
Unlit as yet, but alert for flame,
As soul and heart grew tired of fashion.
The fruit hung high in the eye of the sun
Till one beheld it who knew it mellow,
And thus was a beautiful woman won—
Won and worn by a worthless fellow.
A touch on her arm—her arms were bare—
They stood where the dance-whirl swept no
longer,
A strong light blazed from a gem in her hair,
But the blaze in her great, dark eyes was
stronger.
’Twas afterwards, ’neath a creamy sky,
When the earth seemed spelled in a dream of
rapture,
That she tripped on a kiss —but where kisses lie
There runs the road to a certain capture.
The rest was easy—no need to tell—
As easy the grade from hill to hollow ;
Enough for the moment—the woman fell,
Nor cared a trifle for what might follow.
This to its credit —the sin did good—
(The good belonged to its unnamed features)
It ripened and filled her womanhood,
And made her one of earth’s near creatures.
The man, her husband, he heard it first
With scoff and scorn and a spice of humour,
Then hands to his ears, shut out the worst,
The word on the wind that was more than
rumour.
When guilt stood proven he made a feast,
Was it peace he sought in the merry medley ?
He laughed the loudest and grieved the least,
But signal-lights in his eyes burned redly.
The woman sat at her lover’s right,
And right of her sat her husband darkling ;
Two wine-full glasses—red wine and white—
Before each guest on the board stood sparkling.
The husband arose. “ A toast! ”he said,
“ To the good, scarce coin of God’s own mintage,
To all true wives,” and he bowed his head,
“And drink it, I pray you, in pure white
vintage.”
His wife’s heart shuddered, though not with sighs,
And her colour paled to a faint suggestion,
She searched for a word in her lover’s eyes,
And he bowed low in reply to her question.
Sin has its heroes ; they stirred and stood,
And one could have heard the fall of a feather.
The red wine—red as their love, their blood—
They drained in the face of the world together.
She looked a queen in her royal charms,
But, white and agasp, a moment after
She went to sleep in her lover’s arms,
With a kiss and a curse on her way to waft her.
The wine was poisoned—’twas tragic, sad
In such a manner to end such beauty,
But what would you have ? the man went mad
When his truant wife played false to duty.
For the rest, this moral is scrolled above,
For the heart to read at each life’s portal :
“ ’Tis venial only to sin for love,
But to give one’s self for gold is mortal.”
RODERIC QUINN.
Wonders will never cease. At recent
Beechworth (Yic.) municipal election it curiously
happened that each of the two candidates polled
160 votes. The returning-officer then gave his
casting vote to the teetotal candidate, although
the other man is a publican, and the R.O. himself
a brewer.
Dear Bulletin. —Amongst the things the
heathen can give us points about is letter-address-
ing. The Jap’s method :
SYDNEY,
214 GEORGE STREET,
BULLETIN OFFICE,
THE EDITOR.
Here the P.O. people have the main destination
as it should be, first, and the other particulars in
their natural order, instead of, as now, having to
wade through half-a-dozen lines to find to where
the epistle has to go before delivery.— Amb. G.
[But in letter-sorting, as it is, the bottom-lino is
taken first, so that the one method is just as con-
venient as the other.]

SATURDAY, MARCH 31, 1900.
A Broken-Down Municipal System.
So far, the plague in Sydney is a very small
affair. It isn’t a patch on the daily, hourly
typhoid as a means of slaughtering the
public, and so far it has proved about as safe
as football, and much safer than it was a
few weeks ago .to have doubts concerning
the absolute justice of the war in South
Africa. Compared with influenza, the situa-
tion is about this—where 4000 people took
influenza and, perhaps, 100 died, 20 people
take the bubonic disease, and 18 die. The
mortality isn’t nearly so great, and the
bubonic disease progresses with a snail-like
crawl compared to the joyous velocity of the
other complaint. The total mortality from
the disease which has filled Sydney with
panic isn’t large enough, so far, to make any
noticeable increase in the total death-rate.
The plague doesn’t even begin to com-
pare, so far, as a destructive agent with
diphtheria, measles, cancer, consumption,
pneumonia, bronchitis, convulsions, apo-
plexy, Bright’s disease, epilepsy, dysentery,
rheumatism, whooping-cough, and a dozen
or two dozen more maladies. It isn’t even
in the same street with premature birth,
malformation of the spine, old age, or that
peculiarly fatal disease known as suicide, and
it isn’t far ahead of that cause of death
described in the Registrar-General’s returns
as “ privation through being lost in the
bush.” The main points about it are that it
is a new and mysterious disease, it is very
dangerous when it starts, though it very
seldom gets started, and it is liable to bolt
and run amuck under favorable circum-
stances.
* * m m
Still, The Bulletin at first viewed the
panic with satisfaction. All the chronic
heavy mortality from typhoid, diphtheria
and other dirt-diseases failed to move the
authorities to a visible knowledge of the fact
that Sydney was a lamentably ill-regulated
city, and if a small, cheap sudden plague
could arouse them to a terror-stricken con-
viction of the fact there were great possi-
bilities about it. But there is much panic,
a mad rush to the Blue Mountains, a desire
to have a violent, temporary washing-up, a
yearning to quarantine people, and isolate
people, and inoculate people, and a general
inclination to leave the system whereby
Sydney is so much in need of cleaning, just
where it was. And if that is done Sydney,
after this plague leaves it and the scare is
over, will settle down to accumulate more
dirt and to get ready for another small epi-
demic and another cheap spasm.
* # * *
The question of the Moore Park ‘‘ tip ”
came up at an early stage of the trouble.
In any decently-civilised city garbage isn’t
“ tipped,” nor buried, nor thrown into a
river or harbor, nor even dropped into the
sea so that a great part of it may wash up
again on the shores along 100 miles of coast.
It is utterly destroyed by fire. But the area
ruled by Sydney Corporation is small and
closely built upon. There is no spot in it
where a destructor ought to be placed. If
Sydney tried to put its destructor in any
other municipality where a more convenient
site was obtainable it would almost certainly
receive a flat refusal. If it didn’t receive a
flat refusal there would assuredly be trouble
with intervening municipalities about the
constant procession of Sydney’s garbage
through their streets. And in this respect
Redfern, Darlington, Glebe, and several
other places are in the same situation as
Sydney. Then, supposing Sydney does
reform, and abolish its tip, and put up a
destructor, there isn’t so very much gained
if the one comparatively clean municipality is
still surrounded by 40 dirty ones. There is no
central authority which can compel the erec-
tion of four or five large up-to-date destruc-
tors around Sydney to consume the garbage
of the entire 41 metropolitan municipalities.
There seems no possibility of agreement
among them for joint action. If they all re-
form and put up 41 destructors—one apiece
—it will be a needless and useless expense,
for 41 small destructors will cost about six
times as much as four or five large ones.
Moreover, Sydney and its environs would
then be pretty nearly all garbage-destructor.
No respectable city of (say) 450,000 popula-
tion should have 41 garbage destructors.
The excitement is too great.
* • * •
So the idiotic municipal system stands in
the way. In fact every proposal for per-
manent reform bumps up hard against the
system somewhere. The system is a hog by
nature and a cul-de-sac by education. More
money is wanted if Sydney and its sur-
roundings are to be decently clean, and a
different kind of alderman is wanted. The
expense of keeping up 41 separate councils
and sets of officials, and the burden of all
these separate debts (for the credit of the
much-divided city is much worse than that
of a united city would be, and its debt is
needlessly expensive) has something to do
with the scarcity of money. Moreover, the
system, being a natural and acquired ass,
doesn’t compel the councils to raise enough
rates to meet their outgoings, so they are
getting more and more embarrassed, and as
they get more embarrassed the city grows
dirtier. It is impossible to get rid of the
slums where the plague naturally locates
itself, for the system gives councils practi-
cally no power to resume property for im-
provement purposes. Over a very large part
of the N.S. W. metropolis there is no Build-
ing Act —the system is too primitive for
that; so the manufacture of new slums and
new plague-haunts goes on apace. The 41
municipalities created by the awful system
of sub-division can’t agree about the need for
a Building Act. If the Sydney municipality
did acquire special powers to reconstruct the
worst parts of the city, and used these powers
wisely, still it could only do a little ; it is
necessary to give the same power and to
instil the same wisdom into forty other
municipal authorities before any general
good could result. And under the system
it is impossible to get aldermen with suffi-
cient brains and originality to make good
use of the powers they have, and make a
united demand for the new powers they re-
quire. The brilliant man doesn’t care to join
an institution which has little power, no good
repute, and where he will be over-ruled by
eleven vulgar dullards. If the community
does elect a better man than the average he
is presently petrified by his surroundings.
The surroundings can’t be got rid of because
the people aren’t allowed to make a clean
sweep of the old crowd and elect a wholly
new one, and partly for this reason the
people take little interest in the busi-
ness and make no special effort to get
good men. The other reasons why the
people take little interest is that only a
few of them have votes ; there is little
excitement in electing a man to be, perhaps,
one-twelfth part of the power which, within
certain very narrow limits, helps to boss one-
fortieth part of a second-rate city ; and they
know they can’t get first-rate men if they
try, for first-rate men aren’t going into so
small and mean a business. The system is
the same old cul-de-sac everywhere.
* * * *
The moral of all this is that Sydney doesn’t
want a mere spasmodic cleaning. It wants
to abolish the old system which made it
dirty, and find a new one which may make it
cleaner, and certainly can’t make it more
filthy. If Premier Lyne is content when
he has struggled round with a whitewash-
brush in one hand and a bottle of insecticide
in the other, he doesn’t comprehend the
situation. A much more essential proceed-
ing would be to call Parliament together
while the scare is on, and take advantage of it
to establish a wholly new order of things.
What is wanted most is one central authority
in place of 41 ; the mayor and aldermen to
be elected in a body every three years by
one-adult-one-vote ; the new Council to
have practically unlimited powers in the way
of enforcing cleanliness, ordering the
destruction, alteration or repair of undesir-
able buildings, preventing the erection of
undesirable buildings, making new streets,
improving old ones, resuming property
for public purposes, controlling streets,
wharves, parks, water supply, sewers, etc.
Then if Sydney wanted to be clean it could
be clean ; whereas at present it can’t be
clean even if it wants to. The only real
difficulties in the way are that the 41 sets of
aldermen don’t want union because it might
diminish their importance—and that can be
got over by not asking the permission of the
41 sets of aldermen. Also there is the ques-
tion of the debts. The latter is a real diffi-
culty but not a serious one. An independ-
ent tribunal could decide the actual present
value of the assets which each municipality
brought into the partnership, and by
deducting them from its debt find
the net liability which each brought
in. Then the new Council could levy uni-
form general rates through all its territory,
and a variable rate in the territory of each
of the old municipalities to pay interest on
its old debts and gradually pay off the princi-
pal. The rate could be so graded that all
the old debts would be extinguished about
the same time, and thereafter the new
Great Sydney would start fair with uniform
taxation all round.
* • • *
It is commonly alleged that people can’t
be made moral by Act of Parliament, though
there is no certain evidence that they can’t
—in fact, there is abundant evidence that
they can, to some extent at least. It is also
alleged that they can’t be made clean by Act
of Parliament. This latter point is also
more than doubtful. Certainly the existing
municipal system of Sydney is a remarkable
proof that they can be made unclean by Act
of Parliament. It is a remarkable com-
mentary on the existing order of things that
the instant a really serious difficulty
arose the municipal authority, which should
surely be doing something, should practically
drop out of sight. The Council is somewhere
in the background, where its component parts
are violently calling each other bad names
over the old, old proposal to erect a garbage-
destructor, and adjourning in great fury
without doing anything in particular. Evi-
dently nobody expects anything of the pre-
sent N. S. W. municipal system at a time of
real emergency.
Five lives Lost.
The coroner’s jury on the Sydney Harbor Colliery
accident followed the coroner like sheep. Accord-
ing to the reports of the inquest, the coroner
appears to have preferred to stroke things down
and smooth things over. He seems to have given
the jury their verdict like a bolus to swallow; and
the jury swallowed it obediently. The verdict
was Accidental Death —and thanks to the Colliery
Co. for the care it took ! But then the jury was a
Sydney jury of publicans, grocers, and photo-
graphers. What could they know of mining—
beyond what they were told? A jury of miners,
independent of the Harbor Collieries Co., would
no doubt have given a very different verdict.
* * * »
The Colliery Co. is sinking for coal on the shore
of Sydney harbor. Its shaft is 1815 feet deep, and
its expects to strike coal in another 1100 feet. The
shaft is 18ft. wide, and bricked for some distance ;
but the diameter is reduced by ironbark timbers
let into the shaft on two sides —to hold the guides
for the cages, when there are cages. At present
the men go down in an open bucket, each with a
leg over the side, and a hand upon the handle _ or
upon the wire rope. The bucket is 3ft. sin. wide
at the top, and 2ft. llin. at the bottom. Between
the men’s legs and the projecting timbers there is
4ft. 2in. space. The bucket is sent 1815 ft. in a
little over 2min. with men; in about lmin. with-
out. As time is money, naturally no time is
wasted.
• * * •
On March 17 six men were sent down in the
bucket at the usual speed. About 500 ft. from the
bottom the bucket swayed and struck a projecting
timber; overturned; threw five men out and
killed them. The sixth man held on till the
bucket was stopped a little lower down, and later
reached the bottom safely.
* * * *
Tne accepted theory was that the men moved in
the bucket and made it sway. But there was
evidence that the bucket had swayed previously
when its speed was checked in descending, and it
is obvious that any such loose weight at the end
of 1300 ft. of wire rope must be liable to sway dan-
gerously.
» * * *
What the jury of saddlers, and jewellers, and
ironmongers should have been told is not the fact
that the Colliery Co. is taking due precautions,
but that it is exercising great care in applying a
most dangerous method. To send men down a
shaft in a swaying bucket, without guides, at ten
miles an hour or more, may be safe enough when
the shaft is only a few hundred feet deep ; but
when it is more than one-third of a mile deep the
method risks life at every trip. The Sydney
Colliery shaft should long ago have been provided
with a proper safety cage, so that there would be
no risk from swaying, and if a man fell sick his
mates would still be safe.
* * * *
The distinction between a sinking shaft (where
fewer precautions are used) and a working shaft
(where more are compelled) is nonsense after a
certain point. The risk to life should be the
criterion of safety regulations ; and the risk is at
least as great in a sinking shaft as in a working
shaft. There is nobody directly responsible for
the accident. Safety cages—or bucket bearers
—cost money and delay work; and the Colliery
Co. naturally wants to get its work done
as quickly and as cheaply as possible.
The Collieries Inspector should have seen that
work at 1800 ft. required greater precautions than
at 500 ft.; and the conditions should have been
altered accordingly. But permanent officials get
into a coroner’s-way of letting sleeping dogs lie
and making as little trouble as possible. In this
community, where nearly every politician
can be “pulled,” and nearly everybody has a
“pull,” the official who makes trouble is cold-
shouldered. And the Collieries Inspector was
guilty of no fault under his Act or regulations.
* « • *
The only thing to do is to tie the regulations
tighter and then a cry would come (supported
by miners in search of employment) that industry
was hampered, and the Department would grant a
special permit nullifying the tighter regulations.
All Acts and regulations depend in the last resort
on the brains and hearts and consciences of the
men who administer them; and the chance of
getting administrators with brains and hearts and
consciences is small indeed in N.S. Wales. Look
at the Parliament! elected by the people !
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Curse of Foreign Capital.
Melbourne Age (17/3/’00) takes up the parable
which this paper took up years ago—that the
“ influx of British capital ” is a curse and a delu-
sion and a blight. Apropos of W.A. mining it
says :
It is evident that the evils we are suffering from are
traceable to the fact that Australia no longer controls her
own share markets, the commanding influence having
passed away to London. That is the price we are paying
for taking the British capitalist into partnership, from
which such great things were expected. The actual ex-
perience of the free investment of English money in some
Australian mines is thus proving the very reverse of the
great blessing anticipated. . . . The evil effects will live
long afterwards. For the unwieldy, over-capitalised com-
panies will remain, equipped with a costly management
and a system of control that must, if unreformed,
spell destruction eventually to the mining industry. ...
In the case of the mines managed from London the diffi-
culty is sought to be met by burdening the property with
a large and terribly expensive staff of mine superinten-
dents, engineers, managers, and sub-managers, and advi-
sory geological, chemical, mechanical, metallurgical and
other experts. Victorians, who are practically acquainted
with the simple methods in vogue here, may well laugh
over such extravagances. [Yes, when they see the rich
and clever mining magnates of Bendigo attending board-
meetings and doing their duty as directors conscientiously
at ss. per time. —Ed. B ] But these fantastic excres-
cences help to make the London system of management
possible for a time. The small army of high-salaried
officials knows no master but the London board.
The mine is kept closed to visitors, and its secrets
divulged only to the London directorate. . . Ever
on the scent for new sources of wealth, and having
exploited all old world possibilities, the company pro-
moter has found the English investor a ready and willing
follower on to the, to him, new fields of mining enter-
prise. Actual mining is, however, little benefited by
these operations. From this quarter we first heard of
the evil system of paid-up and contributing shareholders.
It is the paid up shareholder who represents the spoils of
mining. His sole contribution to the industry is his name
on the prospectus. The other fellow —the contributing
shareholder —has to find the money for everyone to play
with. The issue of paid-up shares is a most ingenious device,
whereby certain influential personages are induced to put
their names to prospectuses, and have their scruples
overcome about sitting on the board of directors. For a
certain number of paid-up shares these personages also
induce their auriferously inclined friends to go in for “ a
good thing.” Of the subscribed capital, the major part
also finds its way into the capacious pockets of the pro-
moters, vendors or underwriters, who are often identical,
but operate under different names. Perhaps the greatest
evil of all is the presence of these same personages on the
London board, drawing immense fees, in some cases £IOOO
each a year, as “ managing ” directors. Thus they are all
the time in the swim : always posted with the earliest
information, and all the time playing the part of the spoilt
child of fortune, who “ tosses ” with a double-headed coin,
or on the principle of “heads I win, tails you lose.”
[The Age omits to add that on top of their colossal fees,
these managing directors often scoop in, for absolutely no
valid consideration whatsoever, huge percentages of the
companies’ profits—book-profits, mark you, not actually-
divided profits, such percentages amounting in some
cases to many thousands annually.]
As one way of spoiling the ‘ ‘ first information ”
swindle how about an Act of Parliament declaring
that on, say, one day every week any shareholder
shall have power, on production of scrip to show
that he is a shareholder, to fully inspect the mine,
and that the results of each cleaning-up shal 1 be
posted conspicuously in some place accessible to
the public, within an hour after they are ascer-
tained ?
Cooling Down.
On March 19, Sydney Daily Telegraph started,
in a feeble sort of way, to hedge on the Contingent
question and its consequences. It began by re-
marking that the war had “ consummated for pood
or evil the military federation of the British
empire.” The mere hint that the Contingent-
fury might pan out for ultimate evil is in itself a
lamentable falling away from the pinnacle of
“loyal” frenzy on which the Australian dailies
have tottered and whooped in recent times. Then
it went on to state that “it is impossible for the
colonies, after the attitude they have just assumed
in Imperial affairs, to remain content with their
present voiceless position regarding the manage-
ment of them.” The Bulletin said the same
thing long ago. If Australia is going to waste its
men and money as a regular thing in British wars,
it must sooner or later have some voice in making
these wars, or else its soldiers are mere mer-
cenaries, fighting for pay in another country’s
quarrels. And the only way it can have
a voice is by having representatives in an Im-
perial Parliament or Council. And that means
Imperial Federation, and Imperial Federation
means permanently humping our share of the
Imperial war burden —our share being about
£4,000,000 a year. The Telegraph, however,
held that it would be no use for Australia to have
representatives in the Imperial Parliament,
because there wouldn’t be enough of them to have
any appreciable authority. So it suggested in-
stead the formation of an Imperial Advisory
Council, which would have no authority at all—
it would merely give advice just as Australia can
do now by cablegram, and the Imperial Govern-
ment would take no notice of the advice unless it
felt so inclined, just as it does now. And Aus-
tralia would form so small a part of the Advisory
Council that its share of the advice would be prac-
tically nil. The Telegraph alleged that Australia
would thus avoid “ raising the financial question.”
By having no control over British war policy it
would avoid paying its share of the British war
expenditure. And the fact that the out-
lying provinces of Greater Britain didn’t
contribute towards Britain’s regular war
expenditure would further justify Britain in
taking no notice of their advice. The only differ-
ence would be that, instead of Australia sending
occasional advice by cablegram, it would pay cer-
tain broken-down party hacks to sit round per-
manently, and be ready to obtrude their sugges-
tions and views at any moment. And Australia
would be as “voiceless” as it is now, for it has
always given advice—the trouble is that no one
takes any notice of it. What does this country


want with a regular Advisory Board when the
A.N.A., and the Mayor of Geelong, and Bard
Fitzgibbon, and Henry Copeland, and G. Pile s
dog are always ready to advise at the shortest
notice ?
The Gall of Agent-General Reeves.
Australia has had to put up with a lot of things
during the past ten years, but sundry new pro-
ceedings on the part of Agent-General Reeves, of
Maoriland, are about the last drop in its cup of
bitterness. The five federating provinces have
five delegates in London to help in the good work
of pushing the Commonwealth Bill through in its
entirety. The number looked needlessly large at
first, but now it begins to appear as if they would
all be wanted. The five provinces have agreed to
unite on certain conditions, and if they are content
with the conditions, and the British Government
is content, it seems reasonable to assume that
there is no more to be said. Adjacent provinces
which, by their own act, have kept out of the
Commonwealth, have no more to do with the
internal concerns of the five provinces united than
they had with those of the five provinces when
disunited. Yet the Federal delegates found Par-
ker, of Westralia, in London demanding that the
proposed internal government of the five provinces
which have agreed to federate should be altered
on certain lines laid down by an outside party
named John Forrest—a party who is outside by
his own deliberate act. And later on, Agent-
General Reeves, of Maoriland, demanded that
further alterations should be made to suit Maori-
land, which has never been in the movement at
all. What he desires is that the British Govern-
ment shall compel the insertion of new clauses,
providing that Maoriland may come into the
league, when it feels so inclined, on the same basis
as the Original States, and also that, if it doesn’t
feel inclined to come in altogether, it may come
in so far as to have the protection of the Federal
defence forces and to make commercial treaties
with the Commonwealth. Both Westralia and
Maoriland would probably have got all they
now ask, if they had presented their requests
to the Federal Convention, or later to the
Conference of Premiers, but they were too
tired, or too haughty, or too undecided to
take any steps then. Maoriland wasn’t even
represented on either occasion, and didn’t recognise
the existence of the Convention. But when, after
tremendous labor and expense, the five provinces
which stood together have arrived at an agree-
ment, and carried it through five troublesome
Parliaments, and secured the popular assent at
five separate Referendums, after months of toil
during which hundreds of speakers and organisers
stumped a territory of about a thousand million
acres —after all this the W. A. and Maoriland dele-
gates propose that the whole stupendous structure
should be pulled down so as to insert their particu-
lar half-brick in the foundations at the particular
moment when they choose to want their half-brick
inserted. Agent-General Reeves, in particular,
gravely states, according to the cable, that the in-
sertion of his particular half-brick wouldn’t in-
volve a delay of quite a year. He doesn’t
know exactly why another year of political
storm should matter much. It would onlv
mean carrying another amended Bill through
five Parliaments, and possibly five more
Referendums, with the possibility that the amend-
ments would cause the rejection of the measure by
one of the provinces and the Commonwealth
which took so much building might fall to pieces
again. Why Maoriland hadn’t the common cour-
tesy to ask for what it wanted while the Common-
wealth Bill was in preparation Reeves doesn’t
explain. Neither does Parker as regards Wes-
tralia. Reeves doesn’t promise that if all this
enormous labor is done over again to let Maori-
land come in that Maoriland ever will come in.
He doesn’t even allege that Maoriland has decided
that it wants to come in. Parker, of W.A., is in
much the same situation. Neither explains why
it wouldn’t do as well to get the union of five pro-
vinces definitely established, and then let the
Federal Parliament arrange for the entrance
of Maoriland and Westralia, instead of run-
ning the risk of wrecking the whole struc-
ture by tinkering with the Constitution before
the Commonwealth is really established. They
merely say that they have taken a sudden
desire to have the moon and must get it imme-
diately. If the federating provinces sent a depu-
tation to England to demand that the British
Government should withhold its consent to some
Maoriland Act of Parliament, which related
solely to Maoriland’s internal affairs, the remark-
able nature of the situation would probably dawn
on Agent-General Reeves. The cases are exactly
similar. Maoriland stood aloof from the Federal
movement though it was invited to join. _ It made
the Federal movement a purely Australian affair.
And it has no earthly right to interfere with
purely Australian affairs.

Oom John’s Defence.
When the W.A. Separation party got up its
famous petition to the British Govern-
ment demanding severance from the territory
bossed by that aggravated Boer, Oom John
Forrest, it set forth, among other things, that
the Goldfields had been unjustly treated in the
distribution of expenditure. They contributed
much, it was alleged, and received little. Where-
upon Boer John instructed his Government
Statistician to get up a set of figures showing that
the Goldfields really got more expenditure than
they should have, and that Oom John had been
to them a liberal parent and a lavish uncle. He
didn’t appoint any independent financial authori-
ties to investigate the matter—they might have
examined into it and reported that the Goldfields
complaint was fully justified, and he couldn’t sack
them for doing so. On the other hand, he
could sack the Statistician if that trodden-on
official didn’t report that the Goldfields com-
plaint was all a lie—which made all the dif-
ference. The Statistician struggled manfully to
make out a good case for his boss and the old
bloated regime. For the period 1896 to 1899 he
credited the proposed new province with 33 per
cent, of the postal and telegraph revenue, and
charged it with 60 per cent, of the cost of working
the post and telegraphs, and 80 per cent, of the
cost of telegraph extensions. Out of an item of
revenue described as “Reimbursements” he
credited it with nothing at all. He credited the
proposed new province with 20 per cent, of the
railway revenue and charged it 25 per cent, of the
railway working expenses. Previous to 30th June,
1896, he alleged that the goldfields supplied none
—absolutely none—of the railway revenue, though
fully three-fourths of the receipts in these times
arose from the tremendous goldfields traffic in the
great boom-time. Of miscellaneous receipts and
interest on bank balances, &c., he credited nothing
to the goldfields—the Bunbury end of the pro-
vince absorbed the lot. He charged the proposed
new province with one-fifth of the cost of Parlia-
ment in the years when it had only about one-
twentieth of the representation, and two-fifths of
the cost (40 per cent.) when it had only 11 per
cent, of the representation. By these and other
silly and outrageous fakes he arrived at this
result: —
Revenue derived from proposed new province, Q43
1895-1899 .. •• •* ” 2' 965,447
Expenditure on it out of revenue .. • • ’
,, . . , , . £345,596
Goldfields defrauded of •• •• Q ’ ,
This meant, roughly expended
on°Kbout £600,000 more than its fair share—that
it got the benefit of all own revenue and the
£345,596 stolen from the Goldfields, and that the
£240,000 or so which formed the deficit at 30th
June, 1899, had also been expended on the older
portion of the Groper country. y •*
Government Statistician’s own showing the.older
portion of Westralia—the Perth and Bunbury
portion, where the Forrest interest lies-have
badly swindled and defrauded the section which
now demands separation. This is the best that he
can do after practising weird dodges with the
figures, of which any sober and respectable pawn-
broker’s clerk should be utterly ashamed. Leav-
ing out the dodges, the real fraud probably
amounts to a million and a half. A wiser man
than Oom John would have dropped this docu-
ment in the sea with a brick tied to it, and
remarked haughtily that it was beneath his
tremendous dignity to notice the Separation
party’s manifesto. When a man s enemy calls
him a great political swindler it is bad ; when he
opens his mouth and calls himself even a small
one it is a great deal worse. And the man who
can’t fully acquit himself after a private trial
before himself and his own servant is in a pretty
bad way.
Concerning the Lyne and See Life
Assurance Co.
When the Citizens’ Life Assurance Co. held its
recent annual meeting in Sydney Manager Garvan
got this noble cube of wisdom off his chest :—
Last year I referred to the necessity of legislation in
connection with financial institutions. The way matters
stand now, companies may be formed to transact business
under the guise of life business, and by offering a contract
altogether impossible of fulfilment, attract some business.
In fact, the greatest nostrums, until found out, meet with
some success. Legislation such as I advocate would at
least compel an institution to transact its business with
sufficient premium rates.
Not only has Manager Garvan here struck a
great want—he has also struck what should, be the
best possible time and opportunity to fill that
want. In all the Australasian provinces life-
assurance legislation is shamefully and disgrace-
fully loose. Anybody may start an assurance office,
with or without capital. He may make his con-
cern look gloriously solvent by leasing a miserable
little office at a few shillings a week and calling it
“leasehold property, £15,000,” or anything like
that. He can put down in his balance-sheet
all manner of assets that don’t exist at all. He
may estimate that his assets will earn in the
future 5 or 6 or 7 per cent, interest; he mhy
attract business by quoting impossibly low rates
and he may do almost anything else that occurs to
him. There is practically no law on the matter,
no legal standard of solvency, no State audit or
valuation of securities —nothing ! The Federal
Parliament is supposed to deal with this subject
when it starts, but it won’t start for some time,
and it will have an awful lot to do when it does
start, and it may prove as tired and indifferent as
the State Parliaments have done, and the best
thing that can happen is for one of the State Par-
liaments to take up the matter and pass drastic
legislation now, and thereby force the hand of the
Central Government. The Federal Parliament
must make the life-assurance law uniform As
things are, it can do it by letting things slide all
over Australia, but, if one province has a good
and stringent life-assurance statute, the Federal
Parliament must either repeal it —which it will
never dare do — or extend it all over Aus-
tralia. What is wanted is a rigid annual
State audit, a regular State valuation of secu-
rities, and a law providing that future inte-
rest earnings shall be estimated at not over
3J per cent., and prohibiting insolvent offices
from doing any new business until the shortage in
the funds is made good. The Lyne Government
should take this matter up, if only in its own private
interest and to further embastion its own private
speculation. Premier Lyne and Colonial-Secretary
See are both directors of the Citizens’ Life Office,
and their manager and colleague says that he
wants more stringent assurance, legislation. They
should let him have it promptly and liberally. It
would prove that their own office can stand the
most rigid inspection, which would be a very good
thing for them and for the office and its clients ;
and it might drive out of the field some shaky
and shoddy institutions which are interfering
with their business, which would be good for
them and still better for the public. Of course if
they pass stringent legislation it will be said that
they did it to wipe out some rising competitors,
and that their motives were mean and sordid.
But, on the other hand, if they don’t do it, these
same competitors will wink at possible clients in
secret places, and say that Messrs. Lyne and See
would have legislated in the matter only they
couldn’t afford to have their own institution too
rigidly overhauled. They are bound to be
calumniated either way, so they might as> well
take the way that is best for themselves and for
the public interest.
Dr. Ashburton Thompson, of N.S.W. Board of
Health, on a part of Sydney’s plague-infested
area
I say deliberately that I know of no place worse than
this—no, not even in the London slums, of which I have
had large experience. It may not look as bad as a London
slum, perhaps ; but this is merely because of our more
liberal sunlight and clearer atmosphere. This collection
of filthy brick huts —I cannot call them houses —and all
other such places as are discovered, will be presented by
the medical officer of health for the metropolitan district
to the local authority as places unfit for human habita-
tion. They are simply ghastly.
It is just as well that all tlie public should know —
the thing can’t possibly be yelled out too often
—that the Central Board of Health has abso-
lutely no power to compel any landlord or tenant
or inhabitant of a house to clean his filthy
premises or have his pestilence-spreading cheap
sewerage remedied; the Board only has jurisdic-
tion over buildings in course of erection. And all
it can do in the case of the typical rotten old
plague-factory is to report the circumstances to
the City Council (which in its turn has no
power to have the necessary work done at the
owner’s or tenant’s expense, but can only pro-
secute him and have him fined mildly over and
over again until he remedies the evil). But
the Council so far has never got anybody
fined ; it has been elected by and mostly composed
of landlords, and lias been there all along merely
for the purpose of conserving the interests of the
landlords’ pockets. Some of the most hideously
dangerous houses in all Sydney—certainly the
most dangerous houses existent in any “respect-
able” quarter—were the property of a landlord
who was a member of the City Council and at the
same time a member of the Leg. Assembly. In
his aldermanic capacity he for years steadily and
effectually resisted all efforts to make him spend
money in repairing his typhoid factories; and
when health-law amendment which might affect
his purse was introduced into the Legislature he
would lobby against it day and night. And for
seventy or eighty years Sydney has been governed
by such hogs as that alderman-M.P., and such
hogs will govern it till it gets municipal one-man-
one-vote.
N.S.W. Minister O’Sullivan’s idea that the
State should guarantee municipal loans, thereby
enabling municipalities which now pay a ruinous
5 or 6 per cent, to get money at 3 or 3), is a great
idea if the money is raised locally instead of being
borrowed in England, and if any means can be
devised to make the municipalities pay up more
regularly than they pay their other State obliga-
tions. The average N.S.W. municipality pays
its outside creditors regularly, but when it owes
money to the State it never pays at all if it
can help it. If the State is going to guarantee
municipal loans, there should be a separate
fixed rate to cover interest and sinking fund,
it should be agreed upon when the loan
is contracted, the aldermen should have no
power to alter or abolish it, and the town-clerk or
other functionary should be a State official for the
purpose of collecting it and paying it direct to the
State, while he would remain a municipal official
for other purposes. His State and his municipal
bosses should I we equal right to sack him if he
failed in his duty, and inasmuch as he would be
partly a State official part of his salary should be
paid by the State out of the money collected on its
behalf —or, better still, the State should pay him
a small percentage on collections. On that basis
it would be an excellent and workable scheme,
but it doesn’t seem workable on any other basis.

Isabella Downey, midwife, was sentenced to
death at Melb. Criminal Court, last week, for the
crime of attempting to get Ellen Butler “ out of
trouble,” after the young spinster had herself
made sundry reckless attempts to effect the same
unholy purpose. Isabella Downey’s case seems
to deserve a little more sympathy than the down-
fall of the average abortionist, insofar that there
was no evidence of her having been a habitual or
avaricious offender. Ellen Butler doubtless
persuaded this woman against her better judg-
ment to do the criminal deed for a fee of
40s., promising gratefully that she, the said
Ellen, would take all possible care of herself
after the event. And because she didn’t take care
of herself—her father gave evidence that he had
protested against her stupidity in shifting her bed
from one room to another, and getting up to cook
the meals— Ellen Butler died. And before she
paid the penalty of her foolishness, ungrateful
Ellen “split on” the woman who had put her
under an obligation. That’s the story of how
Isabella Downey came to be sentenced to death,
the same old story that often, to use the prisoner’s
words, “comes of doing some people a kind
turn.”
For cool originality Big John Forrest —the great
Federal-Anti-Federal wobbler —deserves a leather
medal frequently. Oom John suggests that the
Australian Governments should contribute some
£20,000 or so to the Indian famine-fund, and
Melb. Argus and Age, amongst sundry other
dailies, cordially back up the proposal. It is in
accordance with the eternal fitness of things that
the most untrustworthy wobbler among all the
provincial politicians—the man who has done
most to wreck the Federal cause in his own pro-
vince—should thus be first and most eager to pro-
pose Federal action. But let that pass. The
argument of the Argus is that the existing local
demands, on top of the Patriotic Fund contribu-
tions, will tax the energies and resources of the
charitable to the utmost, therefore the Govern-
ments should each vote a sum, according to popu-
lation, out of revenue. But when the provinces
are invited to provide for their own starving and
old and poor, the Conservative press rises in a
great hurry to observe that there are no special
funds available, and that it would be a heinous
crime to further tax the thrifty Fat-man, who is
already so heavily burdened. This overpowering
desire on the part of Big John and other Tories to
assist —with other people’s money —a number of
hapless niggers a long way off is a touching cir-
cumstance, and stands in fine contrast with their
attitude towards their own poor and suffering.
The Argus lays it down that it is Australia’s
plain duty to contribute. Which is a cheeky
assertion to come from a paper that has so long
and so enthusiastically approved of the selfish,
mercenary neglect of the local wreck. Victoria,
which provides only a gaol for its own sick
poor, and gives the starving and the sick and
the paralytic six months’ hard labor in order that
they may be provided for, is a curious place to
find sympathy with the starving Hindoo. There
appear to be two ways out of the difficulty so far
as Victoria is concerned. One is to cable the
Indian authorities—“ Why don’t you put the
famished inhabitants in gaol as we do, and if you
haven’t enough gaols to put them in why haven’t
you ? ” The other is to provide some other home
than a gaol for its own sick and aged and home-
less, and then send along its Indian Famine con-
tribution if it has any money left.
The British Secretary of State for War offers 114
commissions to Australian officers. The selection
of the men to be made by the “ Governors ” of the
provinces, “after consultation with Imperial
officers commanding local forces.” The Bulletin
is much mistaken if this does not mean that Aus-
tralia is to be practically stripped of and
that the men who have been educated and
trained at considerable expense by Australia
for Australia’s defence, are to be drawn off to
fill the gaps made in the Imperial army
by Boer bullets. Seeing what we have seen,
it is not reasonable to believe that consideration
for Australian interests will prevail in the minds
of Australian “statesmen” against the express
wishes of their present tin divinity, J. Chamber-
lain. Australia can perhaps spare all the men she
has sent to Africa better than 114 officers, since
the loss of the latter would leavh us with no one to
knock recruits into shape. It would be interest-
ing to know before this order is filled exactly what
114 officers represent in hard cash to our bleeding
country.
The Queensland Government is playing a truly
artful game. Recent events have shown only too
clearly that the languid Bananalander was becom-
ing somewhat weary of the Continuous Boodlers,
with their chronic crop of public frauds, petty
swindles, and malodorous jobs of all kinds. Last
session of Parliament saw the Ministerial gang
a-tottering ; subsequent events in the election-line
haven’t improved matters. But this Transvaal
business, with its disloyalty corollaries, has been a
perfect godsend to Philp and co. With the
utmost cunning they have contrived to cast sus-
picion on the “ loyalty ” of Labor representatives,
whose one democratic sin is that they believe in
the wretched Transvaal job just as fervently as
their Capitalistic opponents. The stigma of pro-
Boer has been craftily affixed to the Parliamentary
Opposition, and the Philpites trust that, under
the present Imperialistic mania masquerading as
eatnotism, the Q.N.B. scandals, the disgraceful
hillagoe job, the Seaforth Purchase business (to
say nothing of a dozen minor iniquities), will be
clean forgotten, and that Labor will be downed
that the Empire (supposed to be on the verge of
ruin) may be saved. In fact, had the present war-
like fit seized Bananaland on the eve of a general
election, the chances are Democracy would have
gone to the dogs entirely. Unfortunately for the
Ministerialists, the war-fever soon cools, and the
people sit down again to reflect, and the stink of
the old jobs rises up again—loathsome and damn-
able as ever!

Me. W. Rodier writes to The Bulletin from
Tambua, Cobar: —
Editor Bulletin. —You say (B., March 10) that the
squatter is responsible for the deterioration of the Wes-
tern country by overstocking'. The person who wrote
that evidently knows very little about things. Take the
rainfall for the last four years over the west: you will
find it very much below the average, varying up to 70 per
cent, in places. Also, take the number of stock : they
are very much below what is considered a fair thing in an
average year. There is also the rabbit question to be
considered ; and in this our landlord, the State, has
systematically shirked his fair responsibility, at least for
the last 12 years. What is wanted is for the State to
find out the proper means of coping with the pest; then
it will discover that the squatters themselves will very
soon set to work and clear the country. At present
every one is either using his own plan or doing nothing
at all; and as the drought has ruined nearly all owners
no one is inclined to spend any money in trying to solve
a question that in common justice and fairness should be
solved by our landlord.
To which The Bulletin remarks :—
This country had droughts in the past as bad as this,
probably worse ; yet it was not ruined, as many squatters
now say it is. Why ? Simply because it was not then
heavily stocked, or stocked at all. It is naturally drought-
resisting country ; the scrub, &c., is built expressly for
the climate. But it cannot stand drought and over-
stocking. When drought came along the squatters ought
to have nursed their land ; they ought to have stocked
some of it lightly and much of it not at all {vide Mr.
Peter Waite, boss mulga-and-salt-bush squatter). In-
stead they tried to keep as many stock as ever on it—
often, poor beggars, because, as previously said, they had
to pay as much interest out of wool at 6d. as they did
out of Is. wool. Also, on general principles, if Pro-
vidence and the squatter are jointly blameable this
paper prefers to leave Providence out of the indictment.
The only reason for blaming anybody is to get things
improved ; and this paper regrets that it has so little
influence with Providence that it seems a waste of good
blame to direct it at that quarter.

Says General Roberts in his proclamation to the
Free Staters:—
Orders have been issued by me prohibiting soldiers
from entering private houses or molesting the civil popu-
lation an any pretext whatever, and every precaution has
been taken against injury to property on the part of any
person belonging to, or connected with the army.
From the comments of the Australian daily
press one would suppose that this is a new
departure in the direction of humanity; General
Roberts has been lauded to the skies for it
—yet it is only the substance of a standing
order that has been in force in the British army
for over a century. It is to be found repeated
over and over again in the “Wellington Des-
Eatches. ” It was in the standing orders of the
light Division in the Peninsula with an injunction
that anyone who infringed it was to be “tried on
the drum-head and if found guilty hanged.”
Punishment was swift in those days. Humanity
has nothing to do with it. It is in the
interest of the army and of discipline. If
an army plunders, the population of the country
is made hostile. If soldiers are allowed to leave
the ranks to maraud they are apt to be murdered
by the angry people. Besides, pillage means
waste. Men destroy more than they consume.
If it is necessary to live on the country, it is
better to get what is wanted by means of requisi-
tions and payment. If supplies are taken without
payment, the people will hide what they have.
If payment is offered, they come in of their own
accord. Even in a country so hostile as Afghan-
istan there is no difficulty in getting supplies for
payment.
Ikey Mo has just been awarded a first-class cer-
tificate in S.A. by “his Honor.” Two men had a
fancy for Antarctic in the Brown Hill Handicap at
Vic. Park, a local course—the only city course, in
fact. They weren’t going out themselves, so they
got Cowley, who keeps a shop, to undertake to
put on two half-crowns for them. Which, being
an A.M. Beggar, he didn’t do. According to the
evidence—
Plaintiff Frankel saw Cowley at the latter’s shop, when
defendant said, “ Well, old man, you’ve had a bit of bad
luck. I had it on with the books.” Frankel replied,
“ Books be blowed ; I could have had it on that way any-
where in town.” “ To tell you the truth, I forgot all
about it. But I’ll pay you the bookies’ limit, 10 to 1 ;
that’ll be 50s.”
But as the tote-div. on a 5s. investment was
£32 4s., Frankel “ wasn’t taking any absent-
mindedness”; and neither was the court. Ver-
dict accordingly. What the “ books ” would have
offered if Cowley had rushed them with the two
half-crowns Heaven only kows. Also, Heaven
only knows what the books would have offered if
there hadn’t been the tote-competition to squeeze
them into moderate liberality. Still, despite all
the squeezing, the man who went to Isaac got
only; 50s., while the people’s own price, mathe-
matically ascertained, was 644s. Which seems to
mean that, if he did all the business, Isaac’s end
of the purse would be 594s., and his successful
client’s 50s. ! And yet there are some presum-
ably honest people in Australia who are fight-
ing the tote with all their limbs, while Isaac and
Moses and Reuben smile—likewise Gad and
Manasseii.
A return furnished by the Victorian Neglected
Children’s Department for ’98—the most recent
issue—sets forth some startling facts. In that
year there were no fewer than 4588 youngsters in
the care of the Dept.; and of these no less than
1020 had been made wards of State during the
year. The Australian juvenile is contracting a
confirmed habit of regarding the State as his
father and mother, also his uncle and aunt and
nurse and general relation. No less than 251 of
this 1020 had been deserted by their fathers, and
51 didn’t seem to have gone to the trouble i f
getting a father at all —at least, that necessary
individual was returned as unknown. Presumabl y
he existed in a cursory, negligent sort of way, an l
then departed casually. The Dept, had ap-
parently grown so accustomed to this evanescent
progenitor that it didn’t trouble to enquire into the
perfectly usual circumstance, but just accepted
the infants as part of its daily duty. As
inquiry wouldn’t have diminished the casual-
ness of the parent, this was probably the wisest
course. The Dept.’s plan of boarding the grow-
ing youngsters out with farmers, fruit-growers,
etc., works well on the whole. Of the 4588, 350
were in the schools ; and of these 350 no less than
67 had absconded in the one year. Nothing hap-
pened. They simply walked out in an accidental
sort of way, and were no more heard of ; and the
Dept, was probably only too glad to get rid of
them. Yet despite the 1020 children absorbed by
the State in one year, the streets of Melbourne,
