Alexandra “Hamlet,” March 30 (last night
of the season).
Miss Nance O’Neil has probably made her
greatest Sydney success up to date in “ Peg
Woffington.” Certainly her rather scratch com-
pany has shown to better advantage in it than in
either of the previous productions. “Woffing-
ton ” is described as a comedy, and it is certainly
supposed to have been regarded as such in the
days of our cheerless forefathers, who are now
dead, and are probably happier and more frisky
that way than when they were alive and
having comedies. Its heroine wastes her
heart on a very ordinary lout who is
already married, and when she discovers the fact
she puts a good face on things, and tries to forget
that the one man she ever loved is a lout, and was
always a lout, and will be a lout to the end of his
days. She endeavors to make him a better lout
than he was, and to make his young wife think him
a better lout than he ever will be, and then she
goes back to the paint, and the footlights, and the
gentlemen who ask her to be their unmarried
friend for a consideration. The other characters
include a young wife with a shattered illusion—
a wife whose lout has fallen like Dagon from
his pedestal, so that he will never be quite the
same moral boor again. Also a starving dramatist
puts his ghostly face in at the theatre back-doors
to have his play returned to him with scorn,
whereupon he goes home to his hungry wife and
his two hungry children who had a slice of bread
yesterday. A good part of the audience wept
mto its handkerchief over the hungry home and
the two pathetic children—yet a bygone race of
undertakers really and truly regarded this moan-
ing mass of pathos as a comedy. “Peg. W.” in
its lighter moments depends on sundry gentlemen
of fashion whose language towards each other is
cheerfully insulting. There are two ladies in the
piece, and the most fashionable gentleman offers
one, by letter, £3OO a year if she will “ fall.” He
does this on a reasonably lengthy acquaintance, so
his conduct is excusable. In these days it was the
square thing to ask a lady to fall after a reasonably
lengthy acquaintance. But when he requests the
other lady to fall at their second meeting, and
without even offering her £3OO a year, he doesn’t
seem so wildly comic as he might be. It was an
age when things that now wear clothes went
around in the Altogether, or wore q very disreput-
able kilt at most.
* * * *
In the tragic part of Peg Woffington
Miss Nance O’Neil appears to better advantage
than she did as Leah. She is blighted in both
characters with exactly the same blight. Leah
and Margaret Woffington both lightly turned
their young woman’s fancy in the spring to
thoughts of lout—and were left. But Leah took
it cursing, with her sombre clothes dis-
hevelled, and her arms uplifted, and her long
hair flying to the winds that blow in the
halls of heaven, while Margaret Woffington took
it quietly, in her best frock, and with her hair
done up in a tidy knob at the back. Leah be-
longed to a primitive age when a deceived woman
might curse on the housetop if the roof was strong
enough to support her, and Mrs. Woffington
didn’t. Miss O’Neil has a stately figure to hang
good clothes on, and in various ways she is
a great success as the broken-hearted actress,
while she was only a moderate one as
the broken-hearted Jewess, but it is the same
tragedy each time. Clay Clement, after being
one dead failure and one deader one, makes a very
fine Charles Pomander. Barton Hill bears up
the character of Triplet—the crushed writer whose
children had something to eat yesterday—in a
way that leaves little to be desired. And Miss
Inez Bensusan, as the trusting wife of the lout,
only wants to be a stranger from America, with a
huge advertisement, to be recognised as a very
fine actress ; but, being a local product, she
will probably have to be content with being
regarded as a nice girl with a pleasing ribbon,
in her hair. The writer had some idea of saying
severe things about the lout, but decided to leave
that person to be gnawed by the worm of silent
disapproval.
* • • *
This is Miss O’Neil’s last week at Sydney
Royal, though she re-appears at Her Majesty’s on
14th April—bubonic plague permitting—com-
mencing with “Camille.” On Monday Robert
Brough takes up the thread of comedy at the
Royal with the “Happy Life.” He was an-
nounced for Saturday, but the steamer which bears
Robert and his fortunes from Maoriland has been
delayed. Brough is supposed to come for nine
1 weeks, but if he oan see his way to make it ulna
i months it will be so much the better.
** * *
“ Youth ” is going with a reasonable
amount of support and a loud bang at Her
Majesty’s. There is more banging in this version
than in any previous one, and the Doppers’
terrific attack on Kimberley can be heard all
down Pitt-street. Peaceful persons half-a-mile
off drop their beer in affright when Oom Kruge ';
scanty legions rush the fortifications ; and dogs,
and girls with perambulators, and Greeks with
barrows of 16 specked bananas for 3d., light out in
terror for parts unknown. Yet there are appar-
ently only ten Boers altogether in the force which
bursts headlong over the British fortifications,
and the courage of these ten brown-clad men with
slouch hats ought to be better appreciated. To
keep up the idea with which the play was written
the British force should be much smaller, and
the landscape outside should be brown with
the old cheap weatherbeaten hats of the
enemy. Despite the uproar, and the lavish
scenery, and the fine company, and the new
patriotic tail that has been pinned on to the old
drama, and Brownlow’s melody, it is obvious that
the war-fever has cooled down somewhat. There
isn’t enough of it left to make “ Youth ” more
than a respectable success. Probably when the
troops come back and are kissed things may
brighten up somewhat.
** * *
Up to time of writing C. Holloway had
announced no change in the bill at Sydney
Lyceum. The play with the unjustly incinerated
bishop in it is now in its fourth week, and possibly
the loud local demand for a destructor to take the
place of the Moore Park tip may lend it enough
extra support to keep it going a while longer.
The complete obliteration of the bishop by fire
appeals to the feelings of Sydney at the present
moment. Just now the obliteration of anything
by fire strikes Sydney as appropriate and tending
to the public good.

Sydney Liedertafel proposes to have one
of its occasional breakings-out into opera at the
Palace Theatre on Saturday. The basis and
foundation of the proceedings is to be “Lady
Dolly.” The literary part of this effort is supplied
by Margery Browne —the advertisement gives no
further information, not even a prefix ; and the
music by Alfred Hill. Sundry influential persons
of great political or liquid capacity are mentioned
as patrons.
* * * at
In another portion of this issue appears
a portrait of “ Nat ” Douglas, a one-time favorite
actor, who died in Dunedin (M.L.) early this
month. He was born in Dublin, but spent most
of his life on the Australian stage. “Nat ” and
the late Maggie Oliver and “ Bill ” Holloway were
amateurs together in the very long ago, and used
to indulge in lurid sixpenny shockers in an ancient
bam that stood somewhere about Queen’s Place,
Sydney. At one of these performances, in 1865,
Nat attracted the attention of someone who even-
tually got him a start in the Victoria Theatre
under Tolano. From that time forward he be-
came a stock actor of great renown, and played
many parts well, and some exceedingly well, and
none very badly ; and earned distinction as a man
who had absolutely no use for the prompter. He
was strong as Laertes, lago (the gentleman whom
a historic back-blox critic once described as
a “ perfect cow ”), Oassio, Bassanio, Richmond,
and many other characters. Mr. Douglas leaves a
widow, at one time known in the theatrical world
as Miss Docy Mainwaring. Sad to say, his
last days were embittered by poverty and profes-
sional obscurity. His health failed years ago,
necessitating a long retirement from the stage,
and when he recovered, his former reputation was
forgotten, and he was too old to start building a
new one. So the good old veteran had mostly to
go back to the cheap shocker and the barn again,
m his declining years.

At Sydney Criterion a pleasant-looking
young man named Ernest Brinkmann is making
his first appearance and sings a respectable,
ordinary song in a respectable baritone voice.
Two more new arrivals are the Mintons, who do
some good work in the rather old song-and-dance
and banjo line of business. The Amann boom is
now a subdued roar which becomes aggressive
when Sir ’Enery looks round on the audience, or
Baden-Powell flings up his hat, or Buller waves his
sword towards Ladysmith. It breaks out with its
old-time bellow when a stout German-looking lady
bows distantly to the cheering mass of humanity,
but all the same Ludwig has been at the Cri. a
good while now.
** * *
“ When the Lamps are Lighted ” came
along to Melbourne Royal on Saturday night with
the air and swagger of a melodrama that meant
business. G. R. Sims wrote this four-act work of
fiction, or, rather, he was part author and sole
editor, consequently it possesses commercial
merits, and makes many orthodox appeals to pub-
lic sympathy. All the old reliable characters,
sentiments and situations that have done duty in
a dozen previous dramas from the Sims factory
are crowded into these four acts. The unjustly-
convicted father who drowns his sorrow in gin is
there all the time, together with his wife who made
herself over to a conscience-stricken capitalist for
her daughter’s sake, and the tall, noble young
man who refuses to “ chuck ” the gilded daughter
when he learns her mothah’s Past, and the murder
in the private study where the safe gets burgled,
and the wicked trap-door, likewise the villain
in the garret, and the burglar’s penitent para-
mour, and innumerable other items. The capitalist,
by the way, gets knifed in his bad conscience in
the second act; but he is such a strangely unpic-
turesque person that nobody feels sorry to see
him expire, moreover his decease tends to thicken
the plot and shake up the survivors. If it were
possible to kill him again in the third act the pre-
sent scribe would not attempt to stay the assassin’s
hand.
• * • *
Outside of these domestic troubles, John
Sheridan and Gracie Whiteford, assisted by a
most intelligent kiddie and an unkempt hireling
gal called Semolina, run a caravan show in a
breezy little world of their own. Sometimes
the caravan makes a comic entry into the
domain of grief and wickedness, and for
a while Sheridan is an Irish detective of
the Grattan Riggs type engaged in tracking
the villain to his dingy lair. But he wears a light
heart under his disguises, and he circumvents the
blackest schemes as though he didn’t take them
seriously. Even when he gets rolled down the
trap-hole there doesn’t seem to be any cause for
alarm. The belief that all is well with Sheridan
proves to be quite correct. He turns up again in
the coat of the man who rolled him to his watery
grave, or wherever they went together, and in the
pocket of this garment he discovers the dear old
proof of the inebriated father’s innocence.
Meanwhile the drunken parent aforesaid has also
plunged into the river, which cures him of the
drink habit and reconciles him to his unhappy
wife, who decides that she will take up the broken
thread of respectable matrimony, instead of going
forth to nurse poor and suffering citizens at large.
“ When the Lamps are Lighted ” is a prettily-
mounted drama; also, the stage-management is par-
ticularly effective from time to time. The acting
of the people who undertake serious or sinful
characters might be stronger in most cases, but,
take them altogether, they are a capable co. The
little part of a bent-legged Hebrew toddling up
and down the “ den ” in search of rubies is brightly
played by Frank Weathersby. Leon Roche is a
satisfactory burglar ; Fred Sinclair gives a stiff,
natural impersonation of an upright young man ;
Henry Willard, as the father, humps a heavy
burden of woe to the best of his ability ; and Miss
Maud Denny is hard and sweet, like a ribston
pippin. Miss Maud Gwynne, as the emotional
mother, promises at the outset to score heavily,
but her acting powers are limited, and she relies
MANDARIN KONG YEW WAI, late Chinese
Minister for Labor, who will shortly visit Australia
in the interests of Chinese Reform.
almost entirely on an impressive voice, which has
much pathos in it, reminding one of Miss Beatrice
Lamb’s beautiful monotone.

** * m
Dampier’s profitable season at the Alex-
andra ends next Friday night (30th) with Shake-
speare’s goriest tragedy. “ Hamlet ” has been re-
garded for the last two or three centuries as the
most money-making of the Bard’s productions, but
no thoughtful student has ever suggested a practi-
cal committee of enquiry into the secret of its
success. To tell the truth, the milk, figuratively
speaking, in this great classical cocoanut is
berlud—pure berlud. “ Romeo and Juliet ”
is an entertainment which always commands the
patronage of the Butchers’ Union, but for real,
sanguinary interest the Italian love-story can’t
hold a corpse-candle to “Hamlet.” The one
humorous individual in all Denmark, so far as this
story shows, is the grave-digger. Yorick, in his
time, was a fellow of infinite jest, no doubt, and
the grave-digger seems to have become addicted
to conundrums when he buried the jocular Yorick,
thirty years previous to Ophelia’s decease. There
is no evidence of frivolity on the part of anyone
save Yonok and the working man who disturbs
his bones. The grave-digger is, indeed, the only
character permitted to smile aloud during the
evening, also he is the only live person of import-
ance when the curtain falls. Everybody else
worth mentioning has come to a violent end, and
we are to suppose that after the digger has
put the whole crowd of them underground the
original ghost comes out and gloats over the whole
cemetery. Two nights of “East Lynne” will
sadden the way for “ Hamlet.” On Saturday the
Dampier Co. will depart from Melbourne with
Sydney in view, and other places in between.
The Sydney season opens on 14th April.

The Commercial Bank of Tasmania announces a
profit, for the half-year ended 28th Feb., 1900, of £6611,
which is the best record since the end of 1892. The
recent profits of the Oommereial have been :
June, 1892 .. £13,393 December, 1896 £3833

The profit is equal to £5 9s. 6d. per cent, per annum on
shareholders’ funds (capital, £141,493 ; reserve fund,
£100,000). A dividend at 6 per cent, per annum absorbs
£4245 ; dividend tax thereon is £212 ; bank premises are
written down by-£ISOO ; and the remaining £654, added
to previous balance of £9BO, leaves £1634 to carry for-
ward. Six months ago the Commercial of Tasmania wrole
£90,000 off its reserve-fund to cover losses and deprecia-
tion, and the fact that it. is already found necessary to
provide another £ISOO out of earnings to write down the
paper-value of premises suggests that this provision, large
as it was, was none too large—in fact, not quite large
enough. It also, last year, raised the dividend from sto
6 per cent., which was a tolerably solemn thing to do on
the very day when it admitted to £90,000 of losses, and
wrote off nearly half the reserve it had accumulated in 68
years of existence. Whether the largely-increased earn-
ings this half are due in any great degree to amounts re-
covered out of the losses written off six months ago, or
whether they are all ordinary trade profits, doesn't
appear. At all events, the Commercial is paying a quite
large enough dividend at present, and it is to be hoped
something vigorous will be done in the way of rebuilding
the reserves before there is any further increase.
The Tasmanian Commercial has a good steady
business. During the half-year its note circulation has in-
creased slightly and deposits have expanded by over
£lso,ooo—from £1,318,405 to £1,472,102. It holds £272.969
cash and £158,019 in Government securities—equal to
nearly 30 per cent, on its liabilities to the public. The
reserve of State securities is especially solid con.idaring
the size of the institution, being equal to £16,000 more
than the paid-up capital. Altogether the bank looks
better than it has done for a long wh'le.

A few days ago the Bank of Victoria and
National Bank of Australasia paid off the final instalment
of the reconstructed deposits of 1893. They were the
last of the reconstructed banks which paid the full 4i per
cent, on their old deposits. The institutions which still
remain in a reconstructed state have all, in some way or
other, made new and easier terms with their depositors.
There were 13 reconstructs originally, but only seven re-
main in that state ; in fact, one is so nearly free that the
number may be called six--the Commercial of Australia,
Colonial, London, English Scottish and Australian, A,J.S.,
and Queensland National. The City of Melbourne Bank
was the only one which died of the reconstruction process,
which is the more remarkable as it hung out longer in the
panic time than any of its rivals. Not reckoning the
Federal Bank, which was a death, not a reconstruction
(the date on its tombstone is 29th January, 1893), the Com-
mercial of Australia, which closed sth April, 1893, was the
first to go, and the two last were the Queensland Royal
and the City of Melbourne on May 17. The plague lasted
just 42 days.
* * V •
The Ocean Accident and Guarantee Corporation,
of London, a concern which does a large business in Aus-
tralasia, issues a balance-sheet for 1899 which is calculated
to make many rival institutions green with envy. The
net premium income is £718,746 (in 1897 net premiums
were only £334,102, or less than half). After making pro-
vision for liability under unexpired policies the profits are
£129,635, equal to over 94 per cent, per annum on the
paid-up capital of £137,493. During the year the Ocean
00. bought up the business of a rival institution, and the
sum paid for the goodwill thereof is wholly provided for
out of this year’s revenue. If it had been written off by
instalments, as is the custom, the year’s profits would be
still larger. A dividend and bonus at the rate of
20 per cent, per annum absorb something over
£27,000 ; an addition of no less than £IOO,OOO is made
to reserve fund, which increases it to £350,000; and a
small balance is carried forward. These are magnificent
figures, yet large as the profits are they would he still
larger'but for the painful accuracy with which provision
is made for various kinds of contingency. The Ocean 00.
publishes, along with its balance-sheet, a list of the
securities in which its cash is invested—and the fact that
it can get a statement of the particulars of over £1,000,000
worth of investments on to one sheet of paper may pos-
sibly convince some Australian companies that they
could be a great deal more explicit than'they are on this
important point without ruining themselves in stationery.

The Great Boulder Co., Kalgoorlie, have just
E laced an order with the Austral Otis Engineering Co.,
td., South Melbourne, to build a pair of Compound Con-
densing Engines of 700 h.p. to drive their extensive mil-
ling plant. These engines will be of the latest type, with
Proell valve gear; the flywheel is 20ft. in diameter,
grooved for 16 ropes, and weighs upwards of 22 tons ; the
total weight of engine will be 85 tons. The engines are to
be completed by the end of the year, and will be the finest
and largest plant on the field. Three engines under con-
struction at the Otis Works for the Kalgoorlie Goldfields
have an aggregate of 1800 horse power.*
Gilfillan and M'Creery, Mining and Metallurgi-
cal Engineers, 434 Collins-street, Melbourne, have a
vacancy for a pupil in their ore-testing works. Therough
tuition in assaying, metallurgical work, ore testing, etc.*

Messrs. W. & J. Lempriere, Mining Machinery
Merchants, 506 Little Collins-street, Melbourne, report
that the Luhrig system of concentration is gradually es-
tablishing itself in the principal mills of Australia. The
latest Luhrig vanner has a large capacity, and owing to
important improvements the life of the belt, &c., is greatly
increased. Vanners and spare parts are stocked in Melb.
and prompt delivery is thus assured. A small testing
plant is available near Melbourne. Upon receipt of
necessary data, estimates for complete plants, including
batteries, rolls, trommels, jigs, classifiers, vanners, &c.,
will be proceeded with by competent engineers.*

Mr. Mephan Ferguson, Engineer, Ironfounder,
&c., of Melbourne, Adelaide, and Perth, reports that the
great demand for spiral riveted pipes has necessitated the
increase of manufacturing facilities at his Footscray
works, and he is now in a position to supply orders at
very short notice, for all sizes of pipes from six to twenty
inches in diameter, with either patent cast-iron bolted
joints, flanged joints or the ordinary spigot and faucet
lead joints. Mr. Ferguson also manufactures boilers,
pumps, fluming, patent Nelyambo earth-scoops or tank-
cleaners, rabbit exterminators, refrigerators, ice-making
machinery, bolts, nuts, forgings, and all sorts of iron-work.*

Bendigo’s recent spurt is over; the develop-
ments that at one time promised to keep it going have
not come up to expectations. Most of the deepest-level
stone is poor, or very poor ; New Chum Railway’s 3200ft.
formation is at present hardly inside the pay-limit. As
for the middle levels, about the best stone in quantity is
being got from the Shenandoah—oz. stuff. In the less
fashionable quarters the Prince of Wales group is look-
ing up most; but that in the present depression doesn’t
mean much. Meantime this field’s awful sacrifice of
miners continues.
« « * *
Old Bendigo hands have often wondered why
some New Chum co. didn’t cut at a depth for the Black
Leader—a vein a few hundred feet from the main reef,
narrow at surface but very rich in some of the places
where it has been pot-holed. No co. has, however, ven-
tured to risk swamping its deep workings with water;
but it is now likely that the N.O. United will face it.

The balance-sheet of the Sydney Permanent
Freehold Land and Building 00. is more than usually
interesting, because this is a rare kind of co. which pays
no interest at all on its deposits. In 1895 the depositors
agreed to accept reduced interest at the rate of 2) per
cent per annum, and next year it was resolved that on
and kfter 25th Sept., 1896, deposit interest should leave
©ff entirely. As it was really being paid out of assets,
which was altogether too like the depositors getting their
interest out of their own principal, this was undoubtedly
a wise proceeding. The co. devoted itself to clearing off
the mortgages on its property and the bank overdraft,
and when these burdens are lifted it is understood the
position of the depositors wifi be reconsidered. During

In 1897 the Sydney Permanent Freehold Land and Build-
ing Cos. set aside £58,000 as a reserve for depreciation, and
losses on sales, &c., have so far eaten away this provision
that only £34,371 remains available to provide for future

This is a curious result. The Sydney Perma-
nent Freehold Land and Building Cos. claims to have
£44,878 of its capital intact, after making provision for all
losses. Out of £IOO,OOO of borrowed money it gets
the use of £88,413 absolutely free. It has £145,303 worth
of assets, to earn working expenses and interest on only
£11,243 of interest-bearing liabilities. And for the last
two years it hasn’t managed to do even that. Its gross
earnings last year amounted to only £1 2s. per cent, per
annum on the alleged bedrock value of its assets. The
trouble seems to lie in the fact that the assets consist
very largely of bare lands (country and suburban) which
are very difficult of sale, and on which some occasional
cow grazes gratis. And until there is more activity in
the property-market the co. will apparently do nothing
save exist in a sort of way. In fact, if the income keeps
falling as it has done, and the property-market doesn’t
rise, the co. will soon only exist in a very bad sort of way,
for there is a point beyoud which economy in working ex-
penses can’t go.

The Civil Service Co-operative Society of
N.S.W. completed, on 31st January, 1900, the most profit-
able half-year in its history. Profits of late have been :
January, 1896 ..£3273 July, 1898 ..£4184
July, 1896 .. 3275 January, 1899 .. 4968
January, 1897 .. 3785 July, 1899 .. 4801
July, 1597 .. 3962 January, 1900 .. 5495
January, IS9B .. 4250
A dividend at the rate of TO per cent, per annum absorbs
£350, and 10 per cent, bonus to shareholders accounts for
another £4588. The remaining £557, added to previous
balance, leaves £930 to carry forward. Under these cir-
cumstances the society ought soon to be able to make
some further additions to its reserve fund which has been
stationary at £11,500 for a long time. Also, with the in-
crease of earnings, there begins to be hope that the over-
draft will gradually disappear. Since the society moved
into larger premises and commenced to keep a much
larger stock the overdraft has been chronic.

At July, 1899, the Mutual Store took £2OOO from its scanty
reserve of £7200 (reducing it to £5200) to pay for sewer-
age connections, law costs, &c., and, as these things were
fairly chargeable to revenue, it really made no profit that
half-year. After paying its present dividend, the profit-
balance carried forward is £1552, and, lest the manage-
ment should again be led away from the safe though
obscure paths of £2 4s. sd. per cent, to the dazzling
pinnacle of £3 6s. Bd., it would be well-advised to put
£ISOO of that to reserve straight away. What with losses,
depreciation, sewerage ex.’s, law costs, writing-off, bad
trade, and the £66,116 overdraft, the Mutual Store hasn’t
been doing well, and the increase of its overdraft by £1754
during the half-year is a bad sign. Also, the reserves are
£9OOO less than they were at January, 1893, and the half-
year’s sales are £38,000 less.
Reported that B. H. Junction North began
March with “ the best output made by the co. so far—l2oo
tons sulphides, giving 222 tons concentrates, worth 62 per
cent, lead, 30oz. silver, and 7 per cent, zinc.” And the
market sprang. But it isn’t much of a “ best output,”
anyhow. This co. issues only an annual report; last was
to July, ’99. In the preceding 12 months 46,989 tons ore
had been milled, producing 8459 tons concentrates, worth
31oz. silver, 60.) per cent, lead and 6'9 per cent. zinc.
That is, taking the official figures, 903 tons of ore was
averaged weekly, which produced 162) tons concentrates,
worth practically the same as this last week’s product.
A third more ore (less 4 tons) was milled than in the
average week of last year, and a third more concentrates
(and 5 tons besides) were produced—which, considering
the additional plant provided, is not an improvement to
get excited about. Still, it is a fact that the mine looks a
good deal better underground than it did a while back.
Last 12 months the concentrates produced were set down
at £52,195, and expenses (including £3350 depreciation)
amounted to £53,017. But metal prices have moved in
favor of the co. since then.
Concerning the price of metals, one of the most
noticeable features of the past 18 months, during which
most of them have jumped anyhow, has been the steadi-
ness of silver, usually the most unsteady of them all.
For two-thirds of the whole time it has been 2s. 3d. and a
fraction, and it has not been a penny either above or
below that level for more than a fortnight.

The Broken Hill Water Supply Cos. finding
itself in a terribly tight place owing to the threatened
competition of B.H. Proprietary Cos., seeks to block by
legal technicalities the B.H.P. dam-site application.
« * * *
B.H. Water Supply is in more trouble. Govt.
Analyst Hamlet has reported so unfavorably on samples
of water submitted that N.S.W. Health Dept, has sent
him up to determine to what extent the use of the water
damages the public health. When the. co. refused the
local municipal council’s handsome offer of £300,000 for its
works it threw away a splendid chance to get in out of
the rain.
* * » *
Most of the Broken Hill stocks are at boom
price have been so for months. The slump in
many of the big Westralian mines favored B.H. scrip, and
the increasing output of the latter is in marked contrast
with the decreasing yields of many of the former.
* * * *
B.H. Prop, directors have just got the sanction
of shareholders to sell any of the co.’s outside blocks
which they may see a chance to get rid of. In the dull
days, when it wasn’t certain whether even the main-lode
mines could pay their way on sulphides, practically all
the outside shows were allowed to lapse ; and the B.H.
Prop., on the plea of wanting water, took them up one
after another, until now it holds 32 leases in that district;
and many of these leases were formerly appraised by the
market at £IOO,OOO or so each. Apropos the many dis-
cussions of the life of the B.H. Prop, leases, just as well
to put it down here that the principal leases held by the
co. date from Sept. 3, ’BS.
* * * *
Sulphide Corporation shares, in the beginning
held almost exclusively in Europe, have been, contrary to
the general order of things, drifting so steadily out from
the t’otherside to Australia that an Australian register
has been opened. Shares sprang lately, but it was more
through this register than from any development at the
mine. The mine is big, and the plants are doing good
work, and the movements in the metal markets favor the
co. ; but things were like that when the last annual re-
port was issued and the big dividend declared. This year,
though, there will be an interim div.
* * * *
South Broken Hills have finned up considerably
since the squabbling as to who was to manage the con-
cern has been settled by the rejection of a prominent
Adelaide man, who long sat on the Board without holding
a qualification in his own right.
The reason for the strong demand lately for
South Broken Hill shares is partly attributable to
developments at the mine, and partly to an offer
having been received by the directors from a Melbourne
broker to float two of the co.’s blocks into a separate co.
The co. was offered £IO,OOO in cash and one-fourth in-
terest in paid-up shares in a proposed new co. to take
over the two leases. The directors have declined this,
and the broker now offers one-third interest in fully paid-
up shares. This would be accepted if promoter would
put £50,000 to the credit for working capital, but his idea
is only £IO,OOO. The directors are now considering a
proposal to float off four blocks (they have six leases
altogether) into a separate co. amongst present share-
holders, without asking outside assistance. These four
blocks are quite separate from the two which the co. is
now mining, and include the lease once held by the old
B.H. Block 5 00., which has a shaft and machinery on it.

• * * *
Always look for something new from White
Cliffs opal fields about this time of year. The field’s
suburbs consist of several thousand sq. miles of N.S.W.’s
most awful wilderness, dotted with the victims of a five
years’ drought; and in the absence of a decent water
supply and other accessories of civilisation, the acute
gentlemen who buy opals quit the field in a body
about the beginning of November, and stay away till
March. In those months there is no market to speak of ;
most of the sales are made to absentees’ “buttoners”—
the gentlemen who hang round the pubs, and “ do in ”
the “ cronk stuff,” which is the stuff that t-ributors get
and don’t propose to say anything about. Therefore if
anybody who isn’t desperately hard-up finds stone during
the summer he sits on it till the legitimate buyers are
about due ; and then the public hears of the “ new ” finds.
■This year’s crop has been a shade better than usual,
chiefly because through the English co.’s wholesale
“ sacking ” of hands there has been more prospecting.
Also gems have been found in formations where they were
never suspected ; but opals, like horses, run in all shapes.

except to the brokers. It has been as tricky a stringer

mines were merely Brokers’ Joys.

Tin dredging at Stanthorpe. The new co. which

portion of the claims. The co. seems to be under the

Dredging 00., of many dredges and leases on the Shoal-
haven and elsewhere.

The following reaches The Bulletin from a
reliable man who thoroughly knows what he is talking
about“ A few lines from Woodlark Island may interest
you. This is certainly a most extraordinary formation to
bear gold. There is nothing in the Australasian group
carrying that metal like it. The gold-hearing country on
which the Koolomodan formation lies is about 2) miles
long by about 1 mile wide. I have followed it to where
the coral overlies the primary rock, east, west, north and
south. Everywhere there is the color of gold ; the tops
of the highest hills have an alluvial formation on the sur-
face in which gold can be got, but nothing payable.
Also in some of the coral creeks, with coral bottom, gold
has been found and worked. Such gold, no doubt, has
been derived from the higher alluvial and not from any
reefs or lodes now in existence. Excepting outside bound-
ariesof the ground of the Sydney and Charters Towersco.’s
the lode seems to dip, both at the north and south end.
To the south is all taken up by the aforementioned
co’s to where the formation seems to end ; I think it will
be expensive work to find anything payable northward
outside the co’s boundaries. The difficulties in the way
of developing ground here are great indeed. Native
carriers are very scarce, and their food crops, such as
taro and yams, have been a failure this season, so the
carriers have to be fed on rice and the usual fanned dog.
In travelling through the island one comes across large
creeks running through coral fissures, visible for about
half a mile, each heading out of an. enormous cave and
emptying into another —nothing being known either of
source or outlet. Also, round holes are to be met with,
about 3ft. in diam., with no bottom visible, and opening
out below to an enormous size. If any unfortunate
travelling by himself should fall into one, he would never
get out alive. These holes are most treacherous, being
covered up with vegetable matter. Concerning the pro-
perties at work, I do not think the year 1900 will see any
divs. I hear from Sydney that shares have dropped, and I
am sure that ere a crushing takes place on the ground they
will be very much lower still. For some unknown reason
the Ivanhoe has been temporarily detained here. She has
very rich ore on board, while the Victory got away with
an inferior sample. The result of the Victory's shipment
will be known before this reaches you, and if it has not
caused a slump I shall be much mistaken. There is a lot
of sickness on the Island. It rains in torrents nearly
every day, and when a man is not drenched with ram ha
is saturated with perspiration.”
* * * *
Woodlark Island: The last consignment went
under an oz. to the ton; of course it will not pay to bring
ore so far for so little. One paper asks why the publio
should be alarmed because one parcel of ore had not
realised expectation. There is every reason to be alarmed.
Is history going to repeat itself ? Many ounces to the ton
before the float and only a few dwt. afterwards. With
ore such as this surely it would be easy to at least roughly
judge its value before it was consigned; whoever was re-
sponsible for sending ore under an ounce to the ton from
Woodlark Island to N.S.W. should not be given an oppor-
tunity to repeat the mistake.
* * • •
The Sydney Sunday tipster says that Great
Boulders are now dirt cheap and should be bought as they
are bound to see £3 or £4. Also he remarks that the
Boulder has on hand a million tons of tailings which will
go an oz. per ton. Well, if the Boulder had a million tons
of tailings worth £4 a ton gross it would have a lot more
gold in its tailings heap than ever has been produced
otherwise by the mine. For a good while the Boulder
crushed with 10-head only ; now it has 60-head going. It
would take 60-head of stamps over 20 years; steady run
day and night to turn out a million tons of tailings. Yon
can’t get two or three tons of tailings from one ton of
stone.

The London director again. Under article 86
of the rules of association of Lake View Consols Cos. the
four directors took for the year ended August 31, fees and
percentages amounting to £6300. There are now five
directors, and the accrued percentages alone for the past
five months amount to £7OOO. Monstrous Robbery 1
Unspeakable Grab ! Shameless Reach-out 1 And the
beauty of the average London director is that, so far_ as
he can manage it, not a single item of valuable information
is imparted to his Australian shareholder until the Gang
at headquarters has worked it for all it is worth.

* * * *
A correspondent tells The Bulletin that the
Balia Balia (near Roebourne, W.A.) copper-shows are, so
far, not a glittering success. Thirty men have been at
work for a year, and under 200 tons ore has been shipped ;
this has been got by extensive picking. The writer avers
that the formation consists of a surface deposit only—no
lode. One mine in the district, the Evelyn, 40 miles from
Balia Balia, promises well, he says, and threatens to
develop into a really good property.

Peak Hill Goldfields, Limited, for which Gov.

an interim div. of 2s. Co.’s share capital, £230,000; divs.
to date, £99,666.
* * * *
Princess Royal will shortly help Norseman out
of the dust and the ashes in which it has been having a
bad time lately. As the result of 12 months’ work the co.
now has a 30-head battery, a cyanide plant (1800 tons
capacity), and a mine opened up sufficiently to keep the
works moving. From April the output should be doubled.
* * * *
Venture Corporation : The last meeting of this
crowd in London showed its experience in Westralia,
where it has lost to date £420,000. The only thing
it got of any value was the Boulder Main Reef, which
since it passed to a subsidiary co. has paid £120,000 in
divs. The co. engaged the very highest-paid experts to
examine for it. Of the Golden Cement Claim, White
Feather, the boss expert said it was worth in gold
£2,774,280. The directors then employed a high-toned
London firm of engineers to condemn or confirm these
figures. The firm’s report valued the property at £2,286,291.
Since then the property has been absolutely worked out,
and the gold realised £2290 2s. 6d. No explanation has
been given as to what has become of the balance
of £2,771,990. One shareholder innocently asked the
chairman if it were not possible to get a refund
of fees from the examiners, as they were men of great
eminence in their profession ; to which he replied that
the Board would willingly give a very good commission
if it could be shown how it was to be done. The
strength of this organisation is shown by the fact that,
despite enormous losses in Westralia, it has still
£900,000 cash in hand, and is doing mining and other
business in other parts of the world. It was for this co.
that T. A. Rickard, the State Geologist of Colorado,
travelled through Westralia to see what it had got for
its money, and it wag on his advice that it dropped
all its remaining options and pulled out of the country
altogether.
• * * •
The pioneer of the Westralian public batteries,
after lying idle for some months, is now to move on.
Originally intended for the Magnet, it was passed on to
Yalgoo ; and now, Yalgoo having duffered, East Magnet is
to get it. One-third of the public batteries are now
clearly not wanted where they are. Districts applying
for batteries in these later days are asked for certified
statements of ore at grass; one of them, Siberia, has
already responded, and goes on to show a return of £6075
in the first six months, charging only 15s. per ton. Really
it is a wonder good things like this aren’t rushed.
• • • *
Westralia’s first copper-smelting furnace, at
Murrin Murrin, outside Menzies, is, along with the mine,
owned by one man, Charles Taylor. Before putting up
the works, parcels, amounting to nearly 300 tons, were
sent away ; and the returns ran up to 25 per cent. The
plant is small, of course, but fairly complete, even to
electric-light for nightshift. The show was originally
taken for gold ; the ore so far has been quarried, but
shaft-sinking will be necessary.
• * * *
The expectation (first ventilated in these
columns some months ago) that the South German Reef,
Maldon, would strike a floor at 1700ft. level, has been
realised. They have now sunk about 20ft. through blue-
stone, and, by all appearances, the shaft will go right
through to the bottom of the 1750ft. level in the same
country. Local shareholders are now getting anxious as
to when the reef is likely to make again and where, as
where it broke off it was close to the boundary of the
adjoining property. They have had to draw on their
reserve to pay part of the late divs.

SOCIETY
Already the old official families of N.S.W.
are reaching out for the fat billets under Federa-
tion, to which they consider they have a pre-
emptive title. Now, the cause of Australian
unity had no more strenuous or dangerous enemies
than the old official families. Almost to a man
and woman, they intrigued and plotted against
the aspirations of the people ; and now they are
intriguing and plotting to “ officer ” the people in
the important Federal departments. Will the
people let them ?
* » • •
The plague-scare has many advantages
over the war-fever. Kipling hasn’t made any
poem about it. It doesn’t move any great crowd
of idiots to sing patriotic songs every 51,- minutes.
It leads to cleanliness whereas the other only led
to dirt and neglect—women used to abandon the
usual Saturday cleaning-up to go and see con-
tingents depart. And the man who has the plague
doesn’t give himself the appalling airs of the man
with the other plague, nor does he insist on
wearing a sword and a busby and a pair of big gilt
spurs while his disease is on him. In its own
small way the bubonic affair has a lot to re-
commend it. And yet among all the thousands
who cheered for the war there isn’t one to give the
plague a well-deserved whoop-up.
* • * •
Some months ago N.S.W. Govt. Bacterio-
logist asked the Minister in authority for a loca-
tion in which to cultivate bubonic serum for
prophylactic purposes. The Minister refused, on
the ground that it would introduce bubonic germs
into the country. Then another Minister came
into power ; same request, same answer. Bubonic
plague is here now, rapping at the door of all
sorts and conditions of people. The health-office
has run out of serum, and three to four weeks is
the time allotted after cabling to get a supply
from India. To put it mildly, the undue caution
of Australia in this matter is hardly to be de-
scribed as an evidence of over-civilisation.
* * * *
S.A. seems to have had only three cases
of bubonic plague so far, and each was discovered
at Adelaide Hospital. The latest is enveloped in
the same mystery as its predecessors, for what
was certified by an outside doctor as typhoid was
declared by the Hospital staff to be plague.
Moreover, the body was buried without any other
than the Hospital doctors being consulted. The
general public have a fixed idea that S. A.’s plague-
cases originated at the Hospital, and the resident
staff have done nothing to allay suspicion. The
only medical man absolutely “in the know ” is
Coroner Ramsay-Smith, formerly senior physician
at the institution, and Premier Holder has persist-
ently prevented independent inquiry.
• * * * •
Uncleanly Sydney has a new trouble. The
fish in some parts of the harbor are dying, and the
water-police are employed in catching their
decaying remains in nets when floating on the
water or gathering them when cast on the beaches.
So bad is the smell that some of the waterside

■■uburbs are almost uninhabitable; but the
hentific authorities are contemptuously indiffer-

The Fisheries Commission, having caught a few
dead fish, sent them to the Museum. The Museum
could not find anything wrong with them but
thought it might be the dirty water. Some more
were sent to the Board of Health which was too
busy with rats to attend to them. Therefore the
uninstructed outsider can exercise his wits on the
question, and he does. One hypothesis is that
there is an annual fish-epidemic in Port Jackson
every March, another that the fish are dying of
disinfectants and rat-poison, a third that the soap-
suds from the Autumn house-cleaning are to blame.
To make matters worse, fish—at any rate pi-awns
—from the harbor bays were allowed to be sold for
food at the Corporation Fish Market. Among
them were prawns from Johnstone’s Bay, and
Johnstone’s Bay is apparently one of the most
virulently affected plague areas. But it is re-
assuring to note that, in the opinion of inspectors
from the Fisheries Dept, and the Town Hall,
they were good and healthy, and quite fit for food.
Of course all the inspectors know about the matter
is that the prawns look fresh and don’t stink.
* • » •
So fearful of the plague have Sydneyites
become, and so great is the scare caused by the
recent epidemic among the fish in Port Jackson,
that the leading city fishmonger, Chinnery, ad-
vertises that he sells no fish caught in the harbor.
The plague-scare has given the fish-trade a severe
knock ; and yet another blow has been adminis-
tered by Card. Moran’s notification that the faith-
ful may eat meat during Lent. Imagine the
pleasure given by this announcement to a heretic
who had expressly imported from Yankeeland
£2OOO worth of salmon-in-brine for the use of the
faithful during the aforesaid sacred season !

Bowes’ Tattersall’s Club in Melbourne has

ally occupied by the Young Men’s Christian
Association, and subsequently let (by the mort-
agee) to the League of Yic. Wheelmen.
A Bulletin correspondent:—
In quarantining Sussex and Erskine-streets, Sydney,
the intentions of the Government are doubtless strictly
honorable, hut its logic is deplorable. The Balmain,
Leichhardt and Annandale ferry boats were sent round to
Circular Quay; but the Lane Cove folks were evidently con-
sidered immune, though their steamboats occupy one
side of wharf, the other side of which, separated by a
paling fence only, is ranked as “ infected.” On Saturday
morning the L.C. passengers, on arrival at Sydney, were
treated to a series of cheering sights, such as* many
“ bubonic” rats and a long-deceased dog floating opposite
the wharf; a short distance away a couple of “ Dagoes ”
were busily netting prawns. On 'landing the passengers
were confronted by a bobby and a fair imitation
of a dog-leg fence, which prevented entrance into
Erskine-street, the consequence being that everyone
had to pass through a fruit-shop and out of a large win-
dow about Bft. above the lane, by means of a shaky ladder.
This was one of the few occasions where the fat "man did
not come out on top, but the male lean kine who got
down first were treated to a display of ankle and hose the
like of which has not been seen in Lane Cove district
for quite a long while. The dangerous passage through
50ft.-wide Erskine-street was avoided by the simple
(Govt.) expedient of sending some 500 people through a
2ft. right-of-way between the fences of the unspeakable
back-yards of Sussex-street, into which thoroughfare the
right-of-way opened not far from where one of the latest
fatal cases occurred.
♦ * » *
The amazingly up-to-date N.S. W. Minister
O’Sullivan wants to go back to the devices of the
Great Plague of London days (240 years ago) and
burn barrels of tar in the streets to purify the
atmosphere. He says the germs could all be des-
troyed by burning £3OO worth of tar. Why
doesn’t he go a little further back and propose
putting up burnt offerings to Baal? Since
Charles ll.’s day chemists have found out how to
extract and intensify the disinfectant principles of
tar and many other substances ; and you can buy
them in bottles, and after enormously diluting
them, pour the contents over suspected places.
Since the marvellous discoveries made in con-
nection with coal-tar and the myriad pre-
parations thereof you can carry more effective
disinfectant in a little waistcoat-pocket phial than
could be put into a 400-gallon tank in the days of
which Mr. O’Sullivan has been reading in the
Parliamentary library. Going back to the stench
of the primitive burning tar-barrel is abolishing
the railway and taking to the bullock-dray. The
next thing will be a naked O’Sullivan with a
burning cresset doing the part of Solomon Eagle
along Sussex-street. Solomon Eagle was another
great disinfectant of the old days.
As the writer meandered down to the
office the other day to earn his daily beer he
observed a shambling phalanx of boys and men
lurching up Bridge St. Sydney, and wondered
what it could mean. When he went out for his
10 o’clock refresher he observed more squadrons
going the same way. At his 11 o’clock ‘ ‘ Tooth’s
draught” they were still going, and when he went
forth at noon for some tanglefoot they were hurry-
ing past thicker than ever. At 1 o’clock when he
stepped up the street for something to drink the
drove was still going strong. Then he asked a
small man with an umbrella who had just fallen
over a dog what it meant, and learned that the
crowd was rushing to be inoculated against the
bubonic rat. A panic had set in, and the people
from the infected area were hurrying to take ad-
vantage of the safeguard, whatever it might be
worth. Up at the Board of Health the crush was
dreadful and the crowd struggled and fought to
get in. When they got in the inoculation
went on merrily. The surgeon approached with a
hypodermic syringe charged with serum, and
drove it about an inch under the skin before in-
jecting the serum. The skin rose up like a little
bulb, and the surgeon kept his finger over it till
the blood had carried the injected fluid away into
the recesses of the system. As the needle was
driven in the men shouted and swore, and when
they got loose they charged out like mad bulls, and
requested that lurid things might happen to them
if ever they were caught m that place again. The
women bore the incision with a martyr-like ex-
pression. They didn’t scream. When it was
over they drifted outside wearily, and hung about
on one another’s necks, and wept softly, and stood
under the fig-trees showing each other their arms
and wondering if they were going to die. Alto-
gether, inoculation is not a cheerful experience,
but, judging by all accounts, it is a lot better than
the plague.

Daily paper platitude (any daily paper,
nearly any day) : “The training which our men
will have in South Africa will be invaluable.”
From the letter home of a Bourke trooper: “This
game is like bushranging. . . . It’s all right —not
too slow at all.” And yet when we had several
experienced men in the same line of business in
Australia we spent quite a lot to get rid of them.
* * * *
A clerical gentleman who recently vanished
from Australian ken is credited with the latest
idea in divorce dodges. While playing some ex-
traordinary capers with the heads of local religious
bodies, he engaged himself to marry a local girl.
And the wife in London ? To her was sent a long
and detailed account of an imaginary divorce alleged
to have been granted in camera by a local Judge
—and the letter closed with the intimation that
both parties were now free to marry. The cleric’s
wife must have smelt rats and things, for she
queried the Judge to know if the account was cor-
rect ; and of course His Honor handed the letter
to the police, “ who are making enquiries.”

THE STOCKYARD RUINS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
By the stockyard old and falling
Golden butter-cups are smiling,
And the bell-bird sounds a prelude
To a chorus of delight—
Such a twit t’ring and a calling
In a harmony beguiling —
As the Spring displays her splendours
To intoxicate my sight.
By the stockyard old and falling
Is my Love, alone, reclining,
As the heavens blend their colors
In the mirrors of her eyes ;
And my name her lips are calling,
To my love she is inclining —
Now, will some one kindly teil me,
Is this Earth or Paradise ?
Narranghi Boori.
The building where, in brighter days,
Young saints who walk in godly ways
Were won’t to meet for prayer and praise
And healthy conversation,
Became the haunt of biking boys
Who smoked and spat and made a noise,
Nor ever sought to know the joys
Pertaining to salvation.
’Twas bad, but Fate was more unkind.
For, nowadays, what do we find !
The place where simple souls designed
Kerristian deeds —O Crikey !
Is peopled now by men of sin
Who talk about the lamb they skin,
And jugginses who hope to win
Mere earthly crowns from Ikey.

* * * *
After all, there was one outburst of true
Hibernian feeling on St. Patrick’s Day at Melb.
Exhibition. How it evolved is unknown but it
had a powerful atmosphere of whisky. The
shrieks of a man from Galway attracted a rush,
and the malcontent was seen in the middle of a
ring, bouncing hysterically, yelling in wild Irish,
and threatening death to a conciliatory constable.
Authorities broke through, clutched the disaffected
gentleman, and soothed him with soft answers
and smcial pleading. Sure, they were all friends
and they were Britons all, and they were out for
pleasant, peaceful, and brotherly “enjymint.”
Was it Hooligan that “ ud dishturb and schkatter
the swate flowers of amity,” and mangle the
beatitudes “when the Quane, Godblesser, had
mide them all aquail wid puttin’ the shamrocks in
the soldiers’ coats?” At this the offender gave
the agonised cry of a man who sees all his ideals
shattered at a blow. “Glorybe!” he yelled;
“has the time come whin a dacent mahn can’t
aven bate a polaceman ? ”
A little Sydney girl draws a military pic-
ture for The Bulletin :
Travelling recently into Sydney I became suddenly
aware of a gorgeous vision entering the tram. , It was an
apparently millingtary person attired in a gorgeous coat
of glowing scarlet extensively emblazoned with tin-foil
and silver braid, provided with tremendous silver buttons,
and further adorned with curious black braid trappings
suspended from the neck. Over the left shoulder hung
an equally gorgeous red-silk pleated sash, and on the
great creature’s head, notwithstanding the heat of the
day, was ludicrously perched an absurd little busby,
ornamented with a stiff shaving-brush affair and some
more black trapping, and with- -passing round the wearer’s
fp.ee—a heavy silver chain, which after consideration I
decided was intended to keep his jawbone in position and
possibly to help his “mo.” to maintain that fiercely-
horizontal twist which, with his bold, black eyes, gave
him such a footlight ferocity of aspect. In his hand the
haughty one carried an immense sword and seemed to be
well aware that he was just too immense for anything,
for no sooner had he seated himself than he betook him
to the window as a looking-glass and anxiously surveyed
his ensemble to make sure that all his finery was right.
Satisfied on this score, he glanced proudly round the car
until he espied in a corner a very small piece of femininity
indulging in a very large smile, evidently at his expense.
Then over his martial countenance stole a look of haughty
dignity. He gave that insignificant atom one withering
glance which said plainly enough—“ May the Lord forgive
you, for I never can ! ” And with the twentieth (I
counted them) look in the glass and a final re-arrange-
ment of his plumage, he stood up and strode forth upon
his lofty way.

Perhaps when Vic. Premier M‘Lean has
quite finished eating dirt over the Davey case he
will give the world his candid opinion of himself
as a strictly moral military administrator. The
wonder is that Davey has been content with so
little, considering his opportunities. Perhaps
before they have time to ship him off to S’africa he
will change his mind and demand to be made a
captain, or colonel, or something as a salve to his
wounded honor. If he does, it will be hard to see
how M‘Lean can refuse.

A Melb. pickpocket, caught with his hands
in the folds of a woman’s dress in the St. Patrick’s
Day crowd, scorned the usual plea about being
hard-up or “ first attempt ” or mistaken identity
or anything of that sort. His sole complaint was
embodied in the disgusted observation that he had
explored 20 pockets that day and hadn’t got a 3d.
bit out of the lot. And he went to gaol swearing
violently.

latest escapee
from Parramatta
(N. S. W.) gaol,
took his unobtru-
sive departure in
a way that has
made the au-
thorities boil with
indignation. Ru-
mour, at all
events, has it
that he shifted
one or two bars
of his cell win-
dow in a leisurely
way, replacing
them with bogus

so that he had
everything ready
to step out when
the psychological
moment arrived.
Then one night

and vanished.
Presumably a

and a horse which
disappeared from

and was found
wandering in

apparently had
something to do
with the matter
This is the sort

makes a gaol look
ridiculous, and
the gaol that be-
comes ridiculous
is lost. When
the premises cease to retain the unwilling respect
of the inmates it is time to turn them into a.

Two Melbourne youths, who had a
“derry” on their boarding-house, recently paid
off old scores upon leaving. They occupied the
front balcony room, and when the landlady made
inspection after they left, she found everythin g
apparently in order. While she was in the kit-
chen not long after, however, she heard a stranr e
noise in the street, and then a large piece of rork
came crashing through the hall-fanlight. Next
minute her husband burst into the hall, pursued
by several infuriated persons, who commenced
to smash up every article within reach, wHle
outside a mob brandished their fists ar.d
threatened to pull the house down. The explana-
tion was that before leaving the late lodgers had
hung along the balcony a calico sign—“ Local
office of the Pro-Boer Fund. Subscriptions re-
ceived within in aid of the poor Boers.” Beneath
this were two stuffed figures labelled “ Oom Paul 5 *
and “Chamberlain,” respectively, the former
dashing a huge fist into Chamberlain’s eye. The
whole concern was promptly pitched into the
street, and the crowd compromised by dancing
the stuffing out of the Kruger effigy.

* * * *
An interesting recent case in Melb. In-
solvency Court was that of a high-toned clerk, who
owed, among other things, £25 to a servant-girl
for wages. Judge Molesworth remarked that in-
solvent had not denied himself wines, spirits or
beer what time the weary handmaiden was
whistling for her money, neither was he so stone-
broke but that he could employ a barrister and a
solicitor (on cash-terms) to put him through the
Court. The Judge, strange to say, seemed to
regard the domestic servant as a more deserving
object than the alligator and crocodile. However,
there were two excuses for the defaulter. In the
first place, he bears the Christian name of Cecil,
a lofty appellation that naturally tends to make a
man live above his means. Also, it transpired
that the debts which Cecil has accumulated since
1896 are partly due to his wife having been ill for
the last seven weeks. In a less degree they result,
probably, from an, attack of measles when Cecil
was cutting his teeth.
* * * *
There’s a general growl among Melb.
lawyers at the stagnation in the courts. Practic-
ally all the cases which gave promise of decent
spoils at the beginning of the year have petered
out, and the frost is so heavy that no County
Court case has yet been taken before a jury this
term.
* * * *
Things are still not what they used to be
with the Barrier labor unions. Of 15 men killed
in the Broken Hill mines last year, only one
belonged to the Miners’ Association.
* * * *
About two months ago a Yatala (S. A.)
Labor Prison convict threw down his tools and
quietly absconded in broad daylight. He has
been at large ever since, and the police have the
mortification of receiving a note from him every
few days giving information of his last address,
and occasionally he breaks into warehouses right
under the eyes of the law. Police-chief Madley
is now in fear lest he should carry out his latest
proposal to stay at the chief’s private house from
a Saturday to Monday “whenever convenient.”
It was this escapee who headed a gang which
baffled the Melbourne police for over four years.
* ♦ * *
In the Hutchison libel-action v. Singleton
Aegus, Mr. Reid, Q.C., wanted Police Magistrate
Macfarlane to repeat certain evidence given before
him in the police-court. Chief Justice Darley
ruled that no Judge or P.M. should be asked to
say what occurs in a court over which he presides,
and instanced his own refusal to repeat such evi-
dence before a Royal Commission. No statute
exists conferring this immunity on Judges and
magistrates, nor does there exist any general
agreement among Judges that such an immunity
should be conferred. The Chief Justice pleads
public policy in support. But public policy does
not appear to exert itself much in the matter.
Besides, in hundreds of N.S.W. cases magistrates
have reported the proceedings of their courts
before higher tribunals, and neither public policy
nor the presiding Judges showed shock.
Again has the farce of sentencing a woman
to death for killing another by an “ illegal opera-
tion ” of the usual sort been gone through at
Melbourne. Everyone perfectly well knows that
the prisoner will not be hanged ; all the same the
public is bombarded with accounts of the “ piti-
able scene in court,” the “ agony and confusion,”
the “piteous cries of several females,” “the
shrieks and moans and bitter sobs ” —how the
prisoner “drew a deep breath and fell fainting
into the dock seat,” &c. It helps the sale of the
evening papers.

Writes “ Veteran ” :
A cottage in a Sydney suburb, containing two small
rooms and a kitchen, is occupied by a woman with eight
children of her own, for whose support she gets a regular
weekly allowance from the State Children’s Protection
Board ; one foster child, for whom she is paid by its
mother ; and two adult lodgers—three adults and nine
children ! One of the Board’s inspectors regularly visits
the place. Interesting' to know whether its overcrowded
state is known to the Board, and what they think of it.

From time to time the yachting Brassey
cables Melbourne announcing that he has just
arrived at somewhere en route to dear old Eng-
land. Nobody cares twopence—-anyhow, they
don’t care the price of a cable message—for new;
of rubicund TJncle Tom’s whereabouts, so it is
supposed that this unnatural expenditure of
pounds must be a solemn duty. Lord 8., if The
Bulletin remembers rightly, has not yet ceased
to represent Victoria as regards the gubernatorial
salary. He is at present retiring from the billet
on six months’ leave of absence and he commands
two-fifths of the “screw,” leaving the other three-
fifths for Lieut. Gov. Madden. Therefore he
notifies Victoria of the fact that he is still alive
and quite strong enough to draw his share. By
the way, the residue of the awful wines, spirits
and cigars with which Brassey used to mortify his
Guv’ment House guests was sold by a Melb.
auctioneer the other day.
* • * *
A petition for the reprieve of Jones, con-
victed of the cruel Broadford child murder, was
presented to “ Acting ”-Gov. Madden but a few
days before the day appointed for execution, when
it naturally received comparatively little attention.
Jones was one of the sufferers by the war. The
public are too busy and too bloodthirsty at
present to bother about the fate of any retail
murderer, and when nearly everybody has some
reputable acquaintance or relation killed or
wounded in South Africa, the life of a man
supposed to be guilty of a particularly
fiendish crime can excite no sympathy. Archer
was convicted on evidence ten times as strong as
that alleged against Jones, vet half Melbourne
was roaring out that an innocent man was being
sent to the gallows before the criminal confessed
his guilt to Rev. Elliott. And really there is a
reasonable doubt in the case of Jones.
* * « *
The disgraced matriculant of Maoriland
University (he surreptitiously obtained exam,
papers before date of sitting and passed, of course,
with ease) is said to differ from several of the
matriculated in Melbourne and Sydney only in
being found out. Printed University examination
papers easily lend themselves to the designs of the
artful. The same remark applies to examinations
of schoolmasters under Education Boards, and the
cure or partial cure long ago commended is to lay
more stress on the oral than on the written section
of the test questions.

Von Kotze in reply :
“ Constance Clyde,” on the subject of sweated
females, argues as if the continuation or otherwise
of prostitution depended on the economical status
of woman. Nothing of the kind. If every, female
on the planet were to receive a minimum wage of .£5 a
day, a lot of the misery surrounding the life of the
“hunted huntress” would disappear, but she herself
would flourish just as übiquitously as ever, or nearly so.
It takes a female writer to continually introduce the
great fallen woman topic into her copy, even if said copy
should treat on the differential calculus or Polar explora-
tion. Sort of morbid King Charles’s head, I suppose.

The Bathurst Gaol prisoner who broke
from the ranks while at drill the other day and
seriously injured warder Tuckett helps to maintain
the reputation of a penal establishment notorious
for such outbreaks. For years at Bathurst revolts
of prisoners followed one another at short intervals.
Seventy years ago Bathurst was the scene of what
may be called a convict rebellion. About eighty
men defied the authorities and for days the issue
was doubtful. One man and five horses were'
killed. But as the prisoners of Bathurst seem to
be more than ordinarily ferocious so their gaolers
appear above the average in the savagery of their
measures of repression. The revolt alluded to
ended in the hanging of ten of the convicts.
Bathurst’s old gallows was kept in full work. The
new one has not been worked so hard, but has
turned off some gruesome cases.
