A WOMAN'S LETTER.
Sydney, March 27, 1900.
My Dear Myee,—
Prince Alfred. Park, the Exhibition Building.
By one’s watch it is 10 o’clock. All the doors are
surrounded. Impossible for a woman to get near.
The papers, said we would be let in at 10. But the
doors remain barred.
Near me stands an old woman, white and weak,
cleaner of a bank near the infected area. She has
come to protect her life. All day yesterday she
stood in the crowd, thousands of men and boys
around her. This morning she was here at seven.
She waits quite patiently. In her dim old eyes
is the fixed determination to save her life—of 80
years!
Past 10 now. The men are increasing by hun-
dreds. How am I going to get through that
heaving, hysteric, greasy, grimy mass, flattened
up against the doors, flowing back over the foot-
paths, away towards the gates ?
A policeman solves the problem. Why do the
heathen rage against policemen ? I think it must
be because the heathen doesn’t call them “ Con-
stable.” Anyway, I’m inside. The odour of old
hats and daily occupation gives way to the disin-
fectant that sweeps from end to end of the Exhi-
bition Building. The place is nearly empty. But
at the far end is a door, and round that door is a
little crowd. And tnrough that door are the
serum, the doctor, the assistant, the whole gamut,
from plague to life.
“Bare your left arm,” a man cries in a loud
voice.
lam through the guarded door now. They are
getting ready rapidly. The doctor, all in white,
is thanking some young medical students for the
help they gave him yesterday. On a long stool
assistants are spreading Government towels, and
down on the towels they dump little piles of
sticking-plaster, bowls of disinfectant, clumps of
wadding, and little jelly-glasses full of a dull
yellowy-brown liquid, with a faint white froth on
the top—the precious stuff itself.
Behind the form, up against the wall, stand
bottles and bottles of serum, white-colored and
innocent-looking as a gargle. “Let in fifty
women,” says Dr. Armstrong.
“ Bare your left arm,” cries the man at the door,
and one by one the women file through. Women
of all ages, all sizes, all occupations, down to little
things of six.
And now comes the problem. How to bare the
arm! Some of the “factories” make no bones
about it—ah, unfortunate figure of speech ! They
come straight out of the whole bodice, and stand
levealed in all their thinness and their hard cotton
under-bodices or broken corsets. Some of them
struggle desperately to put up a shrunk-tight
washing sleeve, that sticks at the elbow, and
won’t go any further. Halfway between the
elbow and the shoulder it must go. So off with
the frock, and more revealing. Little girls slip
off their dresses made in one, and move about in
their petticoats.
And one—there is always one bolder, wiser,
more dauntless than all the rest. One comes in
with a slap-dash air, says “ ’Ere, do so,” and holds
out her left arm clad in black cashmere, but with
a long, calm, deliberate tear from the shoulder to
the elbow.
I looked to see if she had a cape—but no ! She
pinned the tear loosely together afterwards, and
went out as dauntless as she had come in.
A fit of panic seizes a little group to the
left. “Oh, Mary Ann, I ain’t game!” “Oh,
mummy, mummy, take me ’ome ! ” They burst
into tears. They fall into each other’s arms, and
lean against the walls. But in the end they are
all “done.” They are all so thin, so suggestive of
their hard lives, that one’s heart aches to think of
these poor attenuated arms being exposed even to
the kind pin-prick of the serum-needle.
But the arms are bare, the assistants are ready,
the doctors wait. A dab on the arm with a piece
of wadding soaked in disinfectant—that is the
preliminary step. Sometimes the wadding goes
Sale cream to the arm, and comes back deeply,
arkly, unbeautifully brown. “I do hope I have
a clean piece of wadding.”
The medical student in the white suit smiled,
and said it was only with the men that a piece of
wadding ever did duty twice. Of course the
wadding is disinfected, but then — They screw
up their mouths, they screw up their eyes, they
back a little. An assistant urges them on. The
doctor takes hold of the arm. They turn their
heads away, and a look of mortal anguish comes
over their faces. But before it can settle there,
before a real living look of pain can seize upon the
face, the asssistant dabs a bit of sticking-plaster,
the doctor drops the arm, the deed is done. And
then—oh brave, oh happy, and proud, and com-
placent factory—you are inoculated, you bear on
your left the blister of a blameless, plague-less life,
and between you and the other factories is a great
gulf fixed.
The girls who are “done” are immediately
rushed. They are implored to describe their feel-
ings. They show that blister proudly.
“ ’Urt 1 ” The voice that was full of tears a
minute ago is full of scorn. But they carry the
left arm stiffly away from them, and they go out
into the street, gazing, gazing down on that sacred
sleeve, and in the tram a twinge begins to make
itself vaguely felt.
One hour and the twinge has decided to become
a pain. It creeps down the arm, slowly, dully.
But somehow, although it is pain, there’s an in-
terestingness about it that prevents you from
moaning. Also it has a far-off feeling, as though
it were in your arm, and jet not in your arm. Or
as if underneath the cocaine, a bad toothache was
smothered, but not put out.
Ah, but wait! one hour, two hours, three, four,
five hours go by gaily. Even six, perhaps. But
after six, the deluge. The head begins to ache, a
fevered, irritable feeling gets strong hold of jou.
And the arm, —at bedtime you cannot raise it to
your hairpins. For a day and a night you feel as
if you were at the tag end of an illness without
ever having been ill.
More than anything else, Mr. Valentine
Heaton’s death has driven the plague scare among
the moneyed classes. Until then, rich people
were not mortal. Only poor men could get
plague. But suddenly the Black Death seizes a
moneyed young man who lived at Randwick in
the heart of moneyed people—Mr. Heaton, of
Heaton Brothers, big produce merchants, one of
the guests at a big society wedding last week— a
cousin of the bride, in fact—among hundreds of
other society people.
Rich people are now making for the Mountains
in great numbers, for Plague germs are said not to
flourish at over 3000 ft. Katoomba particularly is
being wildly rushed, especially with families. In
fact, there’s no joy in staying even in the best
hotels up there just now. The gardens, halls, and
balconies swarm with perambulators and nurse-
maids, and a “ quiet week in the Mountains ” has
a very sardonic sound about it.
I notice, by the way, that children seem to get
over Plague. These three flea-bitten little Dovey
children who lived on the dirt-tip at Moore Park,
and the young boy, Strafford, are all convalescing.
News has come of the death of our Go-
vernor’s brother, the Hon. Edward Lygon.
For the third time in succession Govern-
ment House is in mourning. The Duffs, the
Hampdens, and now Lord Beauchamp ended their
vice-regal regimes sorrowfully, shadowed by
death.
The Hon. Ernest was twenty-seven years old,
and had been six years in the army. He
was shot through the heart, near Bloemfon-
tein, while riding about carelessly with three
other officers, who were all wounded. Every-
one feels deeply sorry for Lord Beauchamp,
who seemed to have some premonition of coming
grief, and has been worrying himself a good deal
over the question of how to tone down amuse-
ments for the coming winter, expressing himself
as loath to start festivities when any moment
might bring bad news from the Transvaal.
Sydney heard of the Governor’s bereavement on
Sunday, at the opening of the Home for the Aged
Poor at Randwick. These new buildings, by the
way, are a boon. But why so inexpressibly un-
beautiful? Why are homes for old people always
made ugly ? Or as plain as possible ? Charitable
people still cling to the old fiction that plainness
is next to godliness. It is just about time to give
up that fiction now, when the aesthetic is as cheap
as the hideous.
In “Peg Woffington,” on Saturday night, we
saw Nance O’Neil in comedy for the first time.
Her tragedy woman, by the way, is distinctly one
of the new-woman kind, bursting into tears, then
breaking into a high, gay laugh. The old-woman
always kept a good space between her crying and
her laughing. It was a physical—and moral—
impossibility for her to laugh within five minutes
after tears, and besides—a woman with a pink
nose and red eyes roulading—they knew better,
those wise old women. No, when they wept they
did it into their handkerchiefs, seriously, and to
the point, taking a good half-hour over it, or even,
if the game was big enough, a whole day, with the
shutters drawn and a smell of scent and exhaustion
in the air.
To return to Peg, the frocking is nowhere para-
lysing. “The Woffington” herself only wears
two frocks. She lets slip her third chance—Mrs.
Bernard Beere used to take it, and come into
Triplet’s house in something radiantly elaborate—
and merely put on her third frock again for the third
act. In fact, there is only one dress to remember
affectionately in the whole play—Nance O’Neil’s
yellow symphony in the second act. This looks
like the softest primrose brocade polonaise and
panniers, over clinging dead-white gauze and
yellow lace. An immense yellow rose in Nance’s
yellow hair is audacious, but fetching. The dress
is less exaggerated at the back and side than the
first-act frock, a flowered chine, ever so bunched
and puffed and horse-haired at the back. In this
dress you can see that Miss O’Neil has some secret
theory about turning her back, for she turns it
just as much as ever, indifferent to the fact that
she is turning on the audience a fashion-freak
that is very near a monstrosity.
For the first time I liked Clay Clements. His
sardonic grin is justified as Sir Charles Pomander,
whereas it was a blot on the face of the good
pastor in “ Magda,” who would have died young
if the play hadn’t wanted him. He wears some
gorgeous suits and a black wig, and throws side-
lights on his American reputation of being a
favorite with women. Mr. Canfield has given up
looking like Dante Gabriel Rossetti—l always cut
Rossetti’s portrait out of his poems because I like
the poems—and emerges as something green-
looking, with a lot of butter on his Yankee
accent. Triplet has no clothes, of course, nor
Mrs. Triplet, but the two smart little Triplets—
where did one of them get that bran new red-
brown suit from ? Mrs. Braey’s frocks are
flowered and a little chaotic-looking from the
circle. Miss Edith Berry puts a red head round
the door once, and says two words out of some
green and brown rags. She does not come on the
stage at all, so there was no opportunity of seeing
whether she had given up that tripping walk of
hers, that showed all the soles of her feet: every
bit of them.
Next Monday night will be annoyingly full
with the dear Broughs back, at the Royal, with
“The Happy Life,” and Alfred Hill’s new
romance-opera, opening at the Palace for a three
nights’ run.
In new music, there is Victor Daley’s tender
song, “In the Woods at Dandenong,” with sweet
music by Ernest Truman (Paling and Co.). Words
and music are both so high above the average local
music-mangle’s output that nobody need consider
the printed fact on the cover that the song was
“ Composed and Published at the Request of His
Excellency.”
Speaking of His Ex.’s requests: he is always
doing kind little things to someone or another,
mostly—always, in fact —on the quiet. The latest
was to give gifted Miss Thea Proctor, a local girl
artist, an order for a bookplate.
“ Litus ” writes from Armidale:—
Armidale completely lost its head over Gov. Beauchamp
last week. The Earl came up to the New England capital
to be entertained, and the leading locals made a ludicrous
bungle of the business. At the Town Hall reception His
Ex. asked the committee to introduce him to some of the
people, and the committee responded by leading up four
clergymen and two lay cits., the hall being at the time
chockful of the public men of the locality. On the show-
ground a leading lady invited H.E. to afternoon-tea, at
which were assembled a number of prominent persons.
The invitation was promptly accepted, and here again the
introduction muddle was perpetrated. The Governor was
honored by being asked to meet two of the gathering, the
remainder, for some reason or other, being overlooked. At
the ball (from which, by the way, many of the Armidale aris-
tocracy were absent) everything was in a ridiculous state
of topsy-turvy. The vice-regal set had not been arranged
beforehand, the consequence being that partners had to
be selected in the room, the Mayor having actually to be
introduced to his fair partner a couple of minutes prior to
the commencement of the set. The Governor was not
introduced to a solitary lady in the hall, the result being
that he was compelled to gaze on and watch the company
whirl around. At supper, several guests quitted the
room before the Governor and the Mayoress had vacated
their seats, and, to complete the dismal record of
bungling, the reception committee allowed the Governor
andhisA.D.O. to drive to the State car at the railway
station unattended.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ I have been talking with one of the jurymen
in the Fraser case. The verdict is yet another in-
stance of the absolute necessity of dividing crimes
into degrees, as in America and France. The
local dailies are wild in their indignation that
Mrs. Fraser should have been acquitted, and in
one journal there are sinister hints about the jury.
But the jury did its best according to its reason,
and, of course, its sympathies. The fact that
turee death-sentences were pronounced a fortnight
before the Fraser trial, and a fourth only the day
previous, has escaped the notice of the wise men
of press and public, who are howling so loudly
against the acquittal. But it did not escape the
jury.
“The jurymen were asked to judge whether
Mrs. Fraser deliberately shot her husband or not.
The ridiculous alternative of finding her guilty of
unlawfully wounding was also offered, and they
knew that in one case the woman might be
hanged, and in the other, get 14 years in gaol.
She was a fetching woman—a golden-haired blonde
with the most lovely complexion and a bewitch-
ing bust —and the jury gazed upon this beauteous
form for two solid days. They knew that it was
unlikely she would ever be hanged, but that it was
extremely probable she might get a sentence of 14
years in the event of a verdict of guilty either way.
“They had the partially-borne-out allegations
of neglect and insult on the part of the husband.
They had the husband’s own statement that his
wife was “ off her head ” when she shot him, and,
not being desirous of obtaining for her a lengthy
sentence of imprisonment, they were confronted
with the option of finding her insane, and com-
mitting her to a lunatic asylum, or letting her go
altogether.
“ They accordingly acquitted her, as any other
average jury, unbiassed by public opinion or a
‘bluffing’ Judge, would have done, and, in the
absence of any tangible reason, and not having the
courage to refuse to give any reason at all—which
would have been the wisest course, the 12 had to fall
back on the flimsy tarradiddle that they believed
the shooting was all an accident. Had the jury
been able to limit the penalty to two or three years
in gaol, all her pearly teeth and heaving bosom
would probably not have sufficed to save her
from the penalty of the law, but the fear of
the Judge’s wrath did its work. All the same, the
acquittal of Mrs. Fraser stands as an awful con-
trast to the case of Cecile Anderson, who remains
a prisoner to this day, after serving many years,
for the impulsive slaying of a man who had cruelly
robbed and ruined her.
“ The few who gained admittance to Port Melb.
pier just prior to the embarkation of the Vic.
Bushmen’s Contingent were paralyzed to see one
f allant officer kissing all the male acquaintances
e could catch, and letting maudlin tears trickle
down his nose on to the wharf. It is only fair to
say that the same hardy Clancy, who gained his
bush experience on the Collins-st. Block, was over
two sheets in the wind.
“ Judging by the packed houses at the Princess’s
the pantomime is a great success, goodness knows
why. The soldier-girl on parade is rather fetch-
ing; also, the red and yellow ballet is delightful,
but the rest is weariness, and one worries over
such small details as the insecurity of Gulliver’s
placket - hole, and the furriness of a certain
charmer’s bare legs. Also, one wonders if most
pantomimes are written in lunatic asylums, and
if not, why ?
“Thanks to her realistic socks, Dorothy Vane,
as Red Riding Hood, comes as something of a
shock to fawnlike observers. Not that a grown-
up in baby hose is a complete novelty. Up
North more than one warm-blooded belle scouts
stockings as summer-wear, and occasionally en-
raptures beholders by a random glimpse of shapely
pedestals blushing above pink silk socks. But
somehow she-Bananalanders who happen to be
undecorative of nether-limb seem spared the
‘ rush of heat to the shins ’ complained of by trim-
ankled compatriotes.
“Lady Clarke saw the pantomime in one of
her pale-lilac frocks, with a cluster of dark
violets on the corsage and a spangle of dia-
monds in her ebon coiffure. Lady Janet’s
second daughter, Vera, has returned from her
travels with unwaved dark locks and simple white
frocks, as of yore. Ornamental school-girls with
fluffy hair and picturesque plumage are not
prevalent among the Naicest permeated with
English notions. The Australian little Miss is at
her prettiest just before she turns up her curls,
but at ’ome the Spring Chicken is purposely kept
in the ugly-duckling stage till the time comes to
develop into a swan.
“ The popular kharki gown goes well with a rosy
wearer. But for pale people, its dead-grass hue
wants brightening up, and colors pretty enough
to mend matters mar the military effect. All the
tabbiest women in town sport scarlet facings to
their Kharki and Lancer hats. I guess a con-
tingent of these stout people wou T 1 soon bounce
Oom Paul into suing for peace.
“ Fact is stranger than fiction. It is recorded
as a capital joke in ‘ Innocents Abroad ’ that
when, at a board-ship lantern display, the pro-
gramme said ‘ Our destination,’ the amateur show-
man flashed forth by mistake a convincing view
of a famous burying ground. On Wednesday
(March 21} Kew (Melb.) held its joint citizens’
picnic and state-school outing. The procession
was advertised to start, and did so, from Booron-
dara Cemetery, thence making its way with flags
flying and bands playing to the grounds of Kew
Lunatic Asylum, where the revels proceeded
under drizzling rain.
“ Nowadays theatrical shows rub off their first
freshness in the Harbor City, and altogether
Sydney scores off Melbourne. Northerners are
looting our marriage-market, I notice. In April
Miss Stanbridge, an important local heiress, is to
bestow her hand on a N.S.W. man, and in the
same month Dr. W. Hope, a moneyed, popular,
able young practitioner of Western Vic., is to
wed a Sydney girl.
“ One innovation the Harbor citizens are wel-
come to, the Plague—though they apparently
resent our backwardness in this respect. At
present, Melbourne folk in Sydney are quizzed
and laughed at for their ‘ quaint fussiness,’ and
so many jokes are (or have been) cut at the ex-
pense of * old Granny Gresswell,’ as they call our
Bubonic shoo-er, and his namby-pamby precau-
tions, that the poor thing’s left ear must be burn-
ing permanently.
“Re ‘Merchant of Venice’ at the Alexandra,
all sympathy is monopolised by Shylock, though
Dampier seems a shade too abject and crushed in
the end. Surely such an intense old patriarch
would have retained enough fire to the last for a
backward glance of hatred when no one was
looking. Even a phlegmatic eight-year-old
covertly sticks out his tongue at dad, ” though
the latter flatters himself that for once he’s
spanked the devil out of the rebel. Lily
Dampier’s Portia is a handsome, portly personage
in white, brocaded with brown, a shimmering
bodice and up-to-date touch of black in the shape
of a corsage rosette. Nerissa (Katherine Russell),
when I saw her, had white-washed the tip of her
nose “something frightful.” She was at her best
when she threw a white cloak over her hard
purple frock. The men, too, affected crude
magentas and greens, &c., and gold fringes like
Freemasons. Antonio looked a fat, sulky little
Scotchman. One thought of him as “ man Tonv”
f looming in his cups. Rose Dampier, as the
ew’s fair .daughter, wore Paisley-mixture with
magenta trimmings, and later on white.
“The cables recording his whereabouts, which
ex-Gov. Brassey is so constantly sending to Mel-
bourne, are accounted for by the theory that the
vain and prosy old gentleman still labors under
the delusion that he has some sort of a chance of
securing the Governor-Generalship. Hence he
even spends pounds to keep himself in evidence.
“ Lawyer M’lntyre, now languishing in gaol for
the embezzlement of his brother-in-law’s estate,
was positively struck off the rolls last week. The
plea that he had not been in a fit state of health to
prove his innocence at the trial was advanced
with all legal seriousness, but, after considering
the matter for some hours, Judge Holroyd decided
that the prisoner had qualified himself for dis-
missal from the learned brotherhood. Alexander
Grant M’lntyre, who once figured in Melbourne as
‘a pretty boy,’ did a good many foolish things
before he landed in gaol. Many years ago he was
one of the many jugginses who lost their hearts to
a fascinating theatrical ‘star ’ and manageress, and
when the charmer wanted coin to carry on the
show young Mac loomed large behind the scenes.
She took what she could borrow from the ardent
lawyer and gave him discreet smiles for his money.
At last the lady came along with a pretty big bill
for her dear Mac. to endorse, and when his name
was inscribed on the bit of paper she wisely con-
cluded that she had no further use for the young
man. Therefore, when he went round to the
theatre in the evening swelling with adoration
and importance, he found an usher waiting to tell
him that his presence behind the scenes would be
resented as an unwarranted intrusion. The pretty
manageress had struck his name off her roll!
Y ours affectionately,
GOULI-GOULI.
In Victoria the boy with 12 years behind
him and a cigarette in his teeth has a free run of
the smoking-carriages, grown-ups having often to
stand while he sits, sucks and spits. In answer
to grown-up grumbling, Vic. traffic-manager Fitz-
patrick admits that the department is helpless,
and states that though N.S.W. keeps out
youngsters with half or three-quarter tickets, such
a regulation could not be legally sustained.

GOOD HEALTH WITHOUT DRUGS
4.—Mothers and Children.
Mothers who would keep their children in good
health should give them, morning and evening,
Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa made with hot milk. Deli-
cate men and women who have weak lungs, to be
hale, robust and healthy, should use Dr. Tibbies’
Vi-Cocoa morning and evening, and all men who
have to be exposed to the bleak uncertainty of our
trying climate should fortify themselves before
they face their daily toil with Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-
Cocoa, and they can then brave the fury of the
elements with equanimity. The writer speaks
from personal experience and from observation of
beneficial effects on others. Tea opens the pores
and temporarily excites, coffee stimulates the
action of the heart, whilst Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa
gives strength, stamina, and builds up and
strengthens the lung tissues. It is indeed a won-
derful food beverage. Nothing has ever been dis-
covered that can approach it in giving lightness of
heart, joy of life, fleetness of foot, and that
general feeling of comfort which only comes from
a full capacity to enjoy every pleasure, moral,
intellectual and physical.
Dr. Tibbies’ vi-Cocoa can be obtained from all
chemists, grocers and stores or from Dr. Tibbies’
Vi-Cocoa, Limited, 269 George-street, Sydney.
Merit, and merit alone, is what we claim for
Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa, and we are prepared to
send to any reader (a postcard will do) who names
The Bulletin a dainty sample tin of Dr. Tibbies’
Vi-Coooa. *

PERSONAL ITEMS.
A Sydney suburban Wosleyan clergyman
who appears to be the chief mouthpiece of the
Orange fraternity had a son born to him on St.
Patrick’s Day!
N.S.W. has five police-magistrates over 70
years old. One is 77, and one 72; also, there are
on the list two P.M’s of 68 years, one of 67, two of
66, one of 65, one of 63, and four of 60.
Energetic W. P. Reeves, Agent-General
for Maoriland, has only been 13 years in politics.
Previously he played at the Bar and worked for
the Press. He was at one time, through his
fattier, chief owner of Lyttelton Times.
Colonel Lassetter’s trip to England with
the N.S.W. Lancers cost him £lO,OOO. The
Colonel’s wife was Miss Antill, sister of Captain
and Major Antill, who are in Africa with the
Contingent. The Bulletin would like to see a
few Sydney men who whoop so loudly about their
“ patriotism ” fork up in the military interest as
gamely as the Lassetter circle. Jingo skite is
the cheapest form of patriotism.
The Argus did not publish the text of the
cable it received from its correspondent MacDonald
in Ladysmith. The message was communicated
privately to a few of his friends and relatives, but
for some reason not very intelligible was not given
to the public. “Mac.” wired, “Too ill for field
work,” indicating that the long starvation had
served him badly notwithstanding his fine physique
and sound constitution. Probably the doctor sent
the message.
F. B. Freehill jumps up Major, by ex-
amination. He is solicitor, politician, President
of the Irish National League, Consul for Spain in
N.S.W., eleven (or was it twelve ?) times an un-
successful candidate for Parliament, notary public,
and now Major of the Irish Rifles. As to the
notary-public distinction, Mr. Freehill had that
conferred on him by the Archbishop of Canter-
bury, and was, it is said, the first R.C. in Aus-
tralia to receive the honor.
SHE DID.
HE: “Now, Miss Frivol, nobody seems to have
guessed what character you represent this
evening, so you’ll have to give us all a little
surprise.”
LADY IN WHITE: “Do you mean to tell me you
can’t really guess what book I represent — why,
I’m the ‘ Beautiful White Devil' !”
S. Aus. has lost two of its best stock-
judges within a week—James Shakes and G. A.
Gebhardt. The former, after gaining experience
of the country from Adelaide right through to
Port Darwin, went into stock-agency, and later
on was, with his partners, floated into a company.
Mr. Gebhardt began as a butcher, like a good
many other of Australia’s biggest squatters, and
died with four S.A. stations and a big reputation
for breeding Lincoln sheep to his credit.
While three or four front rank Westralian
mine-managers have been relieved of their charges,
one —Hainuton, of the Great Boulder—gets £lOOO
added to his salary, whioh makes him second-best
paid manager in Australia. Stewart, of Chi Hague,
still leads.
What a small place the world is ! The
last the present writer saw of “ Banjo ” Paterson
the latter was hunting round Sydney for Vincent
Dowling on some horse business, and there was at
that time no idea of the latter going to Africa.
That’s only about four months ago, and now they
meet at Bloemfontein where Dowling was a
wounded prisoner.
The ex-Australian Faith-healer, J. A.
Dowie, whose success in the business in Chicago
was lately reported, has new enterprises on hand.
The idea is to build “the City of God”—with
Dowie to hold the deeds. The only essential of
citizenship is faith—in Dowie and God. He says
£200,000 has already been subscribed. Also, no
journalists need apply. The site chosen is near
Lake Michigan. What a joke it all reads, to any-
one who knew John Alex. Dowie in Sydney !
Mr. William Hanson, at one time a
partner in the old Empire proprietary, whose 83rd
birthday was lately celebrated, was the fourth
official Govt. Printer of N.S.W. He was ap-
g tinted in 1854; the first Govt. Printer, John
itchen, dated from 1841. In Mr. Hanson’s
term the printing of postage-stamps and book-
binding were taken over by his department.
Since nis retirement in 1859, Messrs. Thomas
Richards, Potter and Gullick, have held the post.
Minister for Customs Carty Salmon (Vic.)
is a Victorian native with an Edinburgh medical
degree. He gives an excellent imitation of the
“Scoatch” dialect, and is supposed to have been
included in the Govt, as interpreter between Pre-
mier M‘Lean and the other members of the
Cabinet. He gave valuable medical assistance in
the accouchment of the new Ministry, made a
whole-souled fight in Vic. and Queensland for
Federation, and is presumed to be a hostage to the
cause given by anti-Federal M‘Lean.
The late Consul Layard, C.M.G., was a
brother of “Nineveh” Layard. In 1873 he was
sent to Fiji as joint commissioner with Commo-
dore Goodenough to enquire into the desir-
ableness of annexing the group. Both com-
missioners hoped to accomplish the
cession, and to be rewarded with
the governorship of the new pro-
vince. The Commodore,
however, declined to
have any truck with
Mr. (afterwards Sir
John) Thurston, who
held King Thakom-
bau in the hollow
of his hand, and
the astute Chief-
secretary of the
local Govt, pre-
vented annexa-
tion at that time,
though, a year
later, Hercules
Robinson pulled
it off by promis-
ing Thurston em-
ployment under
the Imperial
Govt. Soon after-
wards, when Mr.
Layard heard that
a white messenger
who had been in-
strumental in in-
fluencing the sable
monarch under
Thurston’s in-
structions had
been accidentally
drowned, he ex-
claimed, “It is the
hand of Providence ! I
am avenged.” Thurston
scooped the pool, and
eventually got the go-
vernorship, while the
other members of his
govt., including
Chief - Justice
Charles St. Julian
—once a S. M.
Herald reporter,
by the way—were
left in the cold.

Sleath, M.P., of N.S.W., volunteered for
service as a bushman —no doubt on the strength of
his having marshalled a procession or two on
horseback. And now Roberts, more-or-less Labor
M.P. of S.A., has volunteered for the “Imperial
Bushmen.” But he is a fighter, whether he has
much bush-lore or not.
Little Surgeon Fitzgerald, who has gone
to South Africa as a consulting surgeon for six
months, will not do so very badly. In reply to a
question in the Commons lately, Chamberlain said
the British Govt, intended to pay consulting
surgeons on service at the rate of £SOOO a year.
Fitz. is the surgeon who patched up Lady Hope-
toun’s dainty little nose after her accident in Mel-
bourne, and Lord Chamberlain Hopetoun will be
sure to make the other Chamberlain understand
what a workmanlike little fellow he is.
Superintendent Read's retirement on
holiday to be followed by superannuation removes
an ancient figurehead from the Sydney police.
His authority was practically supreme in the
Metropolitan division, as Inspector-General
Fosbery years ago gave that area completely over
to him. With 40 London police he arrived in
Sydney in 1855. He had previously served six
years in the London force, thus counting over
half a century of active service. He will be
entitled to a pension of £470 on retirement.
Minister and Vice-President of N.S.W.
Executive Council Kenneth Mackay, Colonel of
the First Australian Horse, has personally missed
a big thing, and his N.S.W. Ministerial col-
leagues have missed a big thing too, by his
default. Think of the glory had he resigned
Ministerial office to lead a gang into the Transvaal!
Cable in the London papers : “A N.S.W. Min-
ister resigns office to head his regiment against
the Boers ! ” It wasn’t because K. Mackay was
afraid that he didn’t go to the war—there can be
little fear in so gallant a cross-country horseman —
it was simply because he hadn’t eye for a pictur-
esque advt.
N.S.W. Lands Minister Tom Hassall, he
leading cow-shaker, cannot drop the slang of his
old droving days. As an exponent of Jingoism,
Tom came as a surprise. But probably his fervor
was engendered during a trip he made to England,
some years ago, when his fine figure, handsome
face, free-and-easy style, and superb riding caught
the eye of the people of his native English county
on his return there. He did the local fox-hunting,
and thought the low fences of this ennobling sport
a very “soft thing,” while the natives of “them
parts ” looked on astounded at his jumping feats.
Afterwards the county swells fitted him out in
London with horseflesh, and he fairly “ paralysed ”
Rotten-row for a season in company with his
friends, who shone with his reflected light while
he “witched the Row with noble horsemanship.”
And now “ cow-shaking ! ” Ye gods !
Poor “Jack” Edgar, of Melb. Argus,
was originally intended for the R. C. priesthood,
and was a native of Belfast, the hot-bed of faction
troubles. Edgar frequently told a story of his
school days when the chief sport was fighting the
Protestant boys at the rival establishment. One
day he secured a prize in the shape of a lone here-
tic of about his own age, but a trifle shorter,
whom he took prisoner. Holding his captive
against a fence with one hand, and with the other
raised to strike he ordered the heathen to say
“To hell with the Queen ! ” The other refused.
Just as the blow was about to fall a noise caused
Edgar to turn his head, thinking he was assailed
by more heretics in the rear, when his in-
tended victim instantly propped him viciously on
the point of the jaw, and, sending him to grass,
scampered yelling “ To hell with the Pope ! ’
Charles Rasp, discoverer of Broken Hill,
has just left on his first visit to Europe since his
good luck came. And thereupon a reflection on
the German as a colonist. Rasp was a German;
and when he had made his money he settled in
Australia to spend it. But Rasp’s “ boss,” the
man who provided most of the money for leasing
the ground, was a Britisher —and he promptly
left for England, where he has lived ever since.
Still the early Broken Hill men have stuck to
Australia very well. Here, by the way, is the list
of provisional directors printed on the original B.
H. Prop, prospectus: George McCulloch (Mt.
Gipps Station), Bowes Kelly (Silverton), Harvey
Patterson (Burta Station), K. E. Brodribb (Poola-
macca station), Wm. Jamieson (Silverton), S. F.
Hawkins (Mt. Gipps Station), W. R. Wilson
(Silverton), and W. A. Horn and J. W. Bakewell,
of Adelaide. Charles Chappie, of Silverton, was
he first sec. —at £1 a week.
Humor is whispering that Resident Magis-
trate M‘Arthur King, of Norfolk Island,
has had enough of las peculiar subjects
and meditates resignation. ’Twould
surprise few if the rumor materialised.
At Mr. King’s age the effective govern-
ment of a community so outr6 would be
heavy task. Colonel Spalding, the ex-
magistrate, seems to be doing well on
the island, cultivating cocoa, coffee,
and other products. He takes no
part in public affairs.
Curious to note how the pub-
lic nurses the idea that Kit-
chener’s is the strategic brain
of the Britisli Army now in
the field. There is nothing
in Kitchener’s record to jus-
tify the assumption. So far
as the public are concerned
Kitchener’s military reputation
rests mainly on Omdurman, but
—as Churchill and others clearly
show—Omdurman was won not
by Kitchener’s strategy but by
the independent and brilliant
tactics of Hunter and Mac-
donald. And what was true of
Omdurman was, with modifi-
cation, true of the antecedent
battles. Kitchener’s genius lies
less in generalship than in or-
ganization. He is a consum-
mate administrator and viewed
in this light and in connection
with recent Imperial develop-
ments, what are the odds that he
will not be Australian Governor-
General during the first Federal
decade ? Under the coining Im-
perialistic regime the man who will
commend himself to Downing-street
must possess strong personality and
talent qualifying him to guage and
utilise the capability of Australia as a
recruiting-ground for the British Army.
Kang Yew Wai, the Chinese statesman
who lately ranged himself under the flag of re-
form and had, as a result, to hastily board a
British war-ship, wants to travel through Aus-
tralia. All the provinces are willing except Tas-
mania, which stipulates that Kang Yew Wai can
only come “on producing certain credentials from
either the Government of China or the Govern-
ment of Great Britain.” The original Van-
demonians, it will be remembered, nearly all pos-
sessed certain credentials from Great Britain.
N.S.W. Agents-General since a London
resident was fixed upon, in 1863: Edward
Hamilton, Mayne, Cowper, Forster, Alex. Stuart,
Cooper, Saul Samuel, Salomons and Copeland.
Five of these are dead. All of them except
Hamilton have been more or less active politicians.
Three—Cowper, Forster and Stuart —had been ex-
Premiers when appointed. Mayne represented
the Donaldson Ministry in the Upper House.
Cowper was Speaker, and Samuel, Salomons and
Copeland have held various Ministerial offices.
There died in Grafton (N.S.W.) recently
one of the links which bind the Scotch clergyman
of to-day with the fighters who stuck by their
Church in 1843. Rev. J. Mackay was not one of
the ministers who at the time of the disruption
left their parishes because the richer classes
wanted to dominate, but he belonged to the same
school. Ordinarily the most peaceable of men,
when aroused he exhibited a fine old Scotch tem-
perament which brooked no interference. During
a disturbance at the old gentleman’s church in the
eighties, a section of the trustees locked them-
selves in the kirk overnight, and on Sunday
morning when the minister sought admission they
refused it. Nothing daunted, the old gentleman,
then well in the sixties, procured an axe, burst in
the door, and, with the assistance of his wife and
a buxom serving lass, literally threw the trustees
out into the churchyard. And when he had done
this, not without some personal injury, he quietly
walked to the pulpit and, opening the massive
Bible, uttered the usual formula with which the
Scotch service begins.
Dr. O’Hara, over whom the council of Vic.
Medical Society has just resigned in a body, is
a stalwart fellow with a devotion to sport,
especially shooting. He began practice at the
Melb. Brighton suburb about 20 years ago. His
surgery would perhaps have risen to fame more
slowly but for the awful railway accident at Prah-
ran, where a lot of Brighton people were smashed.
O’Hara attended most of them with a prospect of
fat fees paid by Government. His skill was sup-
plemented by nous in the Supreme Court witness-
box over the cases—he is regarded by the barris-
ters as an unrivalled witness. The consequence
was a prodigious advt. O’Hara took the late
surgeon Beaney’s Cromwell House, Collins-street.
There he has got on for a series of years as Fitz-
gerald’s rival. He has a good baritone voice,
and sang the solo, “Ave Maria,” at the
opening of the new church of the Sacred Heart,
Carlton, three months ago, on a Sunday morning.
His name was advertised on the placards. Most
inexplicably, Archbishop Carr did not insist on
his withdrawing from the programme. After
O’Hara’s “Ave Maria,”Rev. Dr. Reville preached.
Archbishop Carr then mounted the pulpit, and
made his attack on Dr. O’Hara, not mentioning
him by name. The Archbishop said that ‘‘ a well-
known Collins-street surgeon ” was concerned in
a syndicate to promote foeticide. It quickly trans-
pired that O’Hara was the man. The modus
operandi was of his invention or adaptation.
The syndicate was burst-up by the Arch-
bishop’s action. The Medical Association dis-
cussed the matter the other night. The council
unanimously recommended O’Hara being struck
off the roll. The association members did not
carry itJby a sufficient majority, consequently the
council has resigned, which means an appeal to
London.

Aboriginalities.
THE LITTLE MATCH-GIRL.
A Sydney street character.
“ 0.P.” : Alby is a grazing cockey of the
always-too-late-to-mend order that seems to get
along in this great baked land of futility about as
well as the other variety. Alby goes out every
morning with a pocket of lunch and bag of
“ Dead woods,” and sticks the old horse in the
whim, and lies down where the water leaks
through the receiving-tank and reads the Dead-
woods aloud. And the old horse goes so slowly
that you can hardly tell whether he is moving or
not. When they’ve “ got him ”in the book, and
Alby is describing the exciting capture to himself
in a loud voice, to preclude any unfortunate mis-
understanding with himself, the old horse stop 3
altogether and listens intently. And after the
“ Prairie Rose ” has rescued “ Cactus Joe ” from
imminent destruction, and is a galloping speck on
the horizon, with her golden hair streaming into
Joe’s mouth, Alby unhooks the old horse and they
part in a happy, ruminative frame of mind. What
Alby and the old horse don’t know about water-
drawing and the terrors of Nuggetty Gulch isn’t
of much account.

White Cliff New South Wales
March 24th, 1895
To the Post Master Jeneril
Dear Sir I have sent a Parsell in the care of you if you
will be so kind to deliver it with your own Hands to my
cousin he has not Herd from me for years he used to Live
at white chappie Newgate His Name is David Hirons. I
forget his Number of the street will you kindly deliver
this Parsell to him it detains 3 Bottles of opel chips I
have told my cousin to give you one Bottle for your kind-
ness. one thing dear Sir the distinchin Between the
letters in the name I forget whether his name was spelt
Hirones or Hirons Please give either of those names,
my cousin has Large Business Places of different sorts I
)>elieve I dont know what the business is I expect dear
Sir you will Know without my trying to explain them to
you I hope you will like the Bottle of opel chips When
I hear from my cousin I will forward some vaulible
Bottles and will not for get you Dear Sir four your Kind-
ness.
I remain Dear
Sir yours truely, R M .
No doubt the Duke of Norfolk, K.G. Earl Mar-
shal and Hereditary Grand Washer-up of the
United Kingdom, &0., &c., duly rushed round
London with three bottles of “ opel chips ” in ono
hand and a Directory in the other.

“ Wayback ” : I have watched a 4ft. 6in.
snake swallow a hen’s egg. It takes a long time.
The jaws are distended to the point of dislocation.
When the egg has got about Gin. into the gullet,
the inner end is pierced by a muscular contraction
and the contents having been sucked out the shell
is rejected. A brown snake once went for a dish
of eggs in a cupboard of my back kitchen. To
reach the dish on the top shelf it had to pass 18in.
of its body through the handle of a cream-jug.
With the jaws closed over the egg, the reptile was
too big to get back the way it went, and all the
crockery came down witli a smash. When I
arrived on the scene the snake was wriggling on
the floor witli the circular handle of the cream-jug
(detached) round it. It was his last wriggle.
• • * *
“ E.D,” : The Bulletin might start a
“ bad-turn ” series with this. It is the experience
of an old settler, and in the veteran’s own language.
“ Worst turn I ever had in my life was when I
shot a man, over 45 years ago, an’ it happened in
what’s now called the Lilydale district. I was out
shootin’ with a new-chum party from my house,
an’ got separated from the rest, who were in charge
o’ my brother, a better bushman than me. I’d
started a fat wallaby, an’ saw him jump from some
scrub, but, as I hadn’t seen him get away, I w’as
expectin’ a pop at him, an’ lookin’ lively for my
chance. Suddenly my dog began barkin’ at some-
thin’, movin’ round in a circle in a wary way, as
if he was afraid o’ the thing. I could see a brown
object propped against a clump o’ scrub, so I took
aim, an’ let him nave a half-ounce slug. Then I
ran up, an’ I saw what I had taken for a wallaby
was a man in a brown coat, sittin’ leanin’ back
against the scrub, an’ buoyed up by it. He hadn’t
moved, an’ I didn’t move for some time. I tood
paralysed—rooted with horror an’ f eelin’ You
never shot a man, did you ? Don’t. It’s the dead-
liest sickest feelin’ when you understand what’3
happened—like mortal terror an’ seasickness com-
bined an’ condensed. Presently I crept up to the
man, an’ put my hand on him to bring him over on
his back, so’s to get a look at him. He was hard
to move, an’ when I did turn him his legs went
up in the air, stiff as poles. He’d been dead about
two days.”

“Jimmie P.”: N.S.W. Railway Depart-
ment has had a futile “ loyalty’’-hunt. Driver
Peppery, with a very heavy load of perishable
goods, found himself signal-blocked at Fivetents
Platform. A boy was in command. The officer
in charge had put the signal up before going to
dinner. He expected to be absent the usual 20
minutes ; but was detained 70 min., and when he
did return Driver P. was “ ropable.” A silly,
solemn, stupendous investigation revealed that
Driver P. had asked the boy to go for his father.
Boy refused to budge. Driver begged, cajoled,
threatened to no purpose, and then used the
following disloyal and seditious language: “Ya
bloomin’ fathers 40 minutes late. He oughta go
an’ make Boer bait. I wish th’ blanky Boers ud
come and tear down his blanky block signal and
let me gitton. I’ll be blocked all along the line
now. Th’ Boers shud blow ’im and ’iz blocks ta ’ell,
bust ’im|!” Severely reprimanded for inciting a
foreign enemy to invade the country and up-end
the constitution, but the charge of being in
treasonable communication with Kruger held to
be not proved.

Dear Bulletin. —ln New Guinea when a
miner “pegs out” (an occurrence not infrequent)
his mate —or at least those of them not too im-
mediately qualified for interment themselves—
dig a hole—depth dependent upon nature of
ground—and then carefully prospect same before
depositing deceased. I have often heard dialogue re
prospects between these amateur sextons. “ Say,
Bill, I got three weights to the dish in that
paddock we planted Tom in.” Bill: “That’s
nothin’! Why, I got a 2oz. slug in Bairbank’s
mosoleem” (mausoleum). —C.M.
* * * •
Bishop Stone-Wigg tells of the New
Guinea custom of burying children with the dead
mother. On one occasion the missionaries saved
twin infants who were being buried beside their
dead parent. The Papuans prophesied all sorts
of trouble, climatic and otherwise. Sure enough,
terrible hurricanes came shortly afterwards—to
the joy of local vaticinators. A clergyman of
Bishop Stone-Wigg’s persuasion recently rejoiced
aloud in his pulpit that British successes in
S’Africa had followed the fervent prayers of him-
self and fellow Anglicans. Papua is a terrible
land for superstition.
* * * *
A certain hard-driving Riverina squatter
was once asked by a traveller for a “ lift ” to a town
towards which both were bound. The swaggie
was accordingly ensconced in the back of the
vehicle, and away they went. Having done the
five miles into town in about a-quarter of an hour,
the squatter pulled up at the first pub., and
handed his turn-out over to the groom. Presently
the latter, who had been instructed to bring the
luggage inside, approached the squatter in the
bar, and asked what about the swag in the buggy.
“ Swag ? ” —the squatter reflected a moment. “By
heavens ! there ought to be a man there, too ! ”
» * 9 9
“ M.Sfc.J.” : Re difference between ’roo
and wallaby (B. 10/3/’00). The wallaroo is easily
distinguishable by color: male, dark-blue or
black ; female, grey. The kangaroo can be told
at once by its long, stately hops without any
apparent exertion, the body being held erect;
whereas the wallaby moves by short, quick hops
and leans over more than ’roo. Wallabies have
certain tracks to run on, and there are often
obstacles on these tracks which, by practice, the
wallabies get over with ease at full speed but
which would hinder a pursuing dog or dingo. The
dingoes know this and a few will lie in wait on the
track, while the others stir the wallabies up in the
scrub. The ambushed dingoes almost always
score a catch, which they divide with those that
hunt.

camp fire at night time. The occurrence is not
uncommon, and not confined to frogs alone. Wild
ducks frequently dart at, and sometimes right
into, a camp fire, which they, like frogs, evidently
mistake for a water-hole, in which the moon or
some bright star is reflected.—H. J. Mac.

“Mutton Bird”: Re crow-poisoning, I
once shot a ’roo inside of which I found a mass of
congealed blood. This gore I poisoned, and two
days afterwards I found 27 dead crows in the
vicinity. Poison easily-digested matter and you’ll
“get” the black rascal every time.

general trend
oemgtoN.E.

were surprised that it was not more generally
known. Further,- the “twist” or “-wind” of a
tree is (in the Southern Hemisphere) from E. to

“ Ken ” : Harlock was a typical new-chuin
getting “colonial experience.” Tree-felling was
his forte, he said. He had taken many a fall out
of the sturdy oak on “Pa’s estates in Staffordshah”
he explained—not for the beastly low purpose of
obtaining firewood (as we irreverently hinted) but
“ faw exahcise.” So Bill and I, after consultation,
introduced him to a bee-tree. “ Splendid ideah,”
said Harlock, “ Ai, myself, will fell the twee and
forwahd a sample of Austwalian wild bees and
honey to Pa by next mail.” “ Right oh, mate,”
said Bill, “cut in.” Chummy clumsily hacked
away, now and again taking a spell to admire the
“busy little creachaws” as they flew in and out
of a hollow limb, and when at last the tree came
down, he advanced gloved, veiled, and billy in
hand, to secure the hard-earned spoil. Suddenly
he began to back, call us ‘ 1 cads, ” and talk
about “escaped conwicts.” The “honey” con-
sisted of three very dead rabbits and the “busy
little creechaws” were, of course, blow-flies.
“ Pa ” has not yet received those samples.
* * * -9
Poor Mildura, though just at the conflu-
ence of Australia’s two best waterways, has now
to send away its fruits and bring its bottled beer
(it is a Prohibition settlement still) by bullock-
dray; and if things get much droughtier,
the camel will have a call. The river
there is Ift. sin. below summer level;
previous record was Ift. 3in., which has
been registered three times since 1860.
Irrigation is now out of the question;
what little can be pumped occasionally
is reserved for the citrus trees, which
want water badly.
* * *
South Africa, even in war-time,
is a paradise compared with some parts
of our own Bakblox. Says a West Q.
paper: —“Our new-married couple from
Winton got as far as Boulia together, and
there the man died from heat-apoplexy
and exhaustion. The Winton track is
enough to kill any man. I knocked up 14
buggy-horses going there and back. As
a passenger from Winton to Boulia I
paid £6 10s. for the privilege of walking
a great part of the distance and belting
the horses until I was prostrate.” No
wonder the Western worker rushes to join a con-
tingent !
# * * »
One unconsidered effect of the drought is
the immense destruction of fish in the rivers of
the West. In these rivers, fish has in any case
been getting scarcer every year, owing to the
numbers of derelicts and nomads who eke out an
existence by fishing with nets, traps and cross-
lines (despite the law); but the present drought
will make cod and bream-hooking a sport of the
good old time.
9 * * *
“R.N.”: Re wild-fowl catching. In
Riverina I have known men who called them-
selves ‘ ‘ sports ” string overnight across a dam a
strong line to which were attached a couple of
dozen fishhooks, baited with frogs. Daylight
would find a dozen or so black duck safely hooked.
Many a good haul was thus brought home after
an hour or so’s “ shooting ” in the morning.
THE IMPROVED JUMBUOK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Have you heard of Peter Olinger’s great invention?
It is bound to be the wonder of the day.
To wipe out the shearing men is Pete’s intention,
And the thing’s as good as done, so get away.
Get away, you tar-boys, rouseabouts and ringers,
Pack your traps, and take a billet in a store.
There’s no clip about this dandy scheme of Olinger’s,
And the “ woolly ” won’t be wanted any more.
Pete Clinger is a scientist, a wonder,
And terrific is the fortune he will reap.
’Tain’t a mere reform in shearing—no, by thunder!
What he aims at’s an improvement in the sheep !
So the day is not far distant when the wether,
And the haughty ram, and ewe of manner meek
Will give up their usual season’s crop together,
With a ready acquiescence, so to speak.
Pete got notions of the methods that he sighed for
From some scurvy ewes on Hittamitta run.
As his patent in the city is applied for,
I may as well explain just how it’s done.
]You vaccinate your flock with his invention—
Which invention is to Clinger only known—
And each year, upon a day that Pete can mention
All the jumbucks shed their fleeces on their own.
Each sheep will be wound up and regulated
To shed, upon a certain day, its crop.
Each one on its hind-quarters will be dated,
You round them up and watch the produce drop.
And Peter says the thud will be appalling,
And this continent will totter in its sleep
Through several million tons of wool all falling
Simultaneously from regulated sheep.

This aboriginal legend tells the genesis o»
that unique mammal known as the Australian
porcupine (Echidna aculQlta), the aboriginal
name of which is Gwinula, or thief, interpit e
also as “ lazy thief ” :
Hundreds and hundreds of moons before the white man
came to Australia walla walla (rain) didn t full foi moj ‘
than 30 moons. The earth was parched and cmcked ,
and out-back, water there was none. The lords dial in
thousands, likewise the kangaroos ; the famished and| njfoes,
which eat only that which they themselves kill (the
blackfellow is correct for once), came howling, around tne
camps, an<l attacked the starving piecamnies. and even
the adult blacks, many being bitten and same few killed.
Desolation reigned, and horrible death front thirst and
hunger stared the tribe in the face. A ghastly attempt
at a corrobboree was made ; and on the following morn-
ing a stampede took place through barren, rugged, water-
less country to the nearest part of the Murrum ndgee, 300
miles or more away, a land abounding in fish and possum.
Many died on the road, and their putrid carcases, as
is the custom, were carried on to the land of promise
swathed in gum-leaves, to preserve them, and encased in
sheets of bark wrapped tightly around with strong vines.
Their retreat was dismal, and their soul-piercing funeral
dirge rang through the sun-parched forest from break of
day till fall of night. , .
Nut a few died through over-eating after the land of
promise was reached. Arrived at their destination, more
trouble met them ; a hostile tribe had "jumped their
land of promise. Attacked and harried on all sides, sick
and weary, cowed, famished, and broken-hearted, they
collected their dead and retreated up the river,
was scarce, and all the men were obliged to hunt and fish
from daybreak until dark. Even the aged, tottering women
(an unusual thing) cheerfully took part in the hunting
and fishing. But one young man, who was short and
plump and fat, would neither hunt nor fish. He stopped
in the camp, stole the juicy ’possum and the rich cod-fish,
cooked and ate them, slept and fed and grew daily fatter.
lie was, in fact, a gwinula —a “lazy thief-.” One night,
however, retribution came. The whole of the tribe
returned empty-handed and mad with hunger. The
larder was empty ; but the gwinula was not. He was
full. He had cooked and eaten all the ’possum and fish in
the camp, and was asleep in his mia mia, gorged, snoring
and happy. Enraged at the gwinula’s heartless gluttony,
each warrior drove his war-spear right through his body,
pinning him to the earth. In every part of the “lazy
thief ” where a spear could be stuck, it was driven. His
whole body 7 —but not his head or limbs —was pierced by
their weapons. He was killed, and for the night they
left him, bristling with spears. He was gone next morn-
ing, spears and all! But soon they found him ;he was
now a porcupine ! He still had the war-spears stuck
everywhere in his body. They had got smaller ; so had
he ; but he was still plump and fat 1 He had repented of
his gluttony. He came to the camp to be eaten —and they
ate him ! They still eat him ; he is still plump and fat .
They love him !
* ♦ • »
Much nonsense is being written on both
sides rc that wonderful “bushman.” Fact is, the
bushman is a first-class horseman, but only a tenth-
class rifle-shot. The bushman who sleeps all the
year round with saddle for pillow and rifle for bed-
mate is a myth nowadays. With the exception of
a few cattle-men, boundary-riders, and kangaroo-
shooters, bushmen seldom take up a rifle, simply
because, in this great land of pre-mortem mum-
mification, there is nothing interesting enough to
shoot at, and not enough available energy to go
and look for it if there was. The man who does
practise with any consistency is the young town-
ship man of the G-or’-bless-yer-berruther order
whose thirst runs to the blood of small harmless
game and sixpences. He’s the man with the
“religious” shave who can tell you all about
calibre, elevation, and make, and knock match-
boxes at 100yds. Queer fact that nearly all these
accomplished young marksmen bear a family
resemblance.
The late H. N. (“NAT') DOUGLAS , an old
Australian actor.
Photo., Charlemont.
Lake George (N.S.W.) had, a week or two
since, almost ceased to exist. Five years ago it
was a splendid sheet of water 17 miles long, 8
miles wide, and from six to 14ft. deep. At a very
recent date a pool 4 miles long, 1$ miles wide, and
2ft. deep was all that remained.

There has been no more news of the
great goat-station, lately talked of in connection
with Centralia; but, in consequence of the very
big rise in the price of mohair, Yankee thoughts
now incline towards the gay Angora. The first
difficulty has arisen through the extreme socia-
bility of the goat; so a cry has come over from
the Pacific Slope to South Australia for something
pure to start with. But, alas ! the goat is a
sociable creature this side too; so South Australia
can’t.

“ Pasty ” : I have discovered another in-
teresting result of linked beers long drawu out.
The subject is a lank alluvial miner in whom
“ D.T.” takes the form of an inordinate desire to
wash his dog. The dog is a plain white-and-tan
fox-terrier without frills or peculiarities, but as
soon as Ben’s drink begins to get back at him he
becomes strangely troubled about that tyke. He
watches its pranks with haggard eyes and
puckered, worried brow for sometime. At last he
takes Pippo in hand and begins to wash him.
During the first few rounds Ben washes the dog
in a casual sort of way. Then he becomes anxious
and distressed at the apparent uselessness of his
efforts to remove the tan part of him, _ and
begins to wash with fury and bad judg-
ment, using great quantities of soap and
soda, till he and his terrier are nearly
smothered in suds. At length, however, he begins
to breathe freely .again, and Pippo issues from a
cataract of suds, clean and in his right dog, and
Ben’s peace of mind is restored. For a few hours
happiness reigns in the sufferer s humpy, but by-
and-bye the old misgivings take possession of him
again, and he washes the dog once more. I have
known him to wash that same dog 18 times in two
days. Ben’s neighbors leave him to. wipe out his
sins in his own way, and the intimation that he is
washing his dog is accepted as an indication that
a protracted and dramatic orgy is nearing con-
clusion.

“Black T.” : Swagging it recently back of
the Darling, I called at a boundary-rider’s hut 30
miles from the nearest water. In the course of
talk I discovered that the man’s freshest item of
news was the Melbourne boot-trunk tragedy, and
as he did not look strong I thought a sudden shock
like the news of the war might break him up, so I
left him in blissful ignorance of absent-minded
beggars and patriotic funds and things. Per-
haps in the course of a year or so he will learn
that he has been sleeping on top of an active vol-
cano, so to speak, This recluse had been there
for 14 years—getting his rations every three
months. What an existe ice !
It had to leak out. A candidate for Vic.’s
next lot of Bushmen wrote when applying, “ I can
ride and shout well.”

If the alleged Benjamin Bridge, now on
his way back from W.A. in custody, is the man he
is supposed to be, the authorities have re-captured
a very fierce and slippery Benjamin. Eight years
ago that youngest son of Jacob was sentenced to a
long term for horse-stealing, but he got out of
Murrurundi gaol the same afternoon and skipped
like a light-hearted child for parts unknown. He
was re-captured in Farthest-North Queensland
and, pending developments, he was put into a
commodious wooden gaol they have up there.
He promptly set fire to the premises, or else they
accidentally took fire while he was in them. The
despairing policeman removed him with great
difficulty, and after a fierce struggle chained his
wild-cat prisoner to the fence while he went to
look for a bucket wherewith to extinguish Her
Majesty’s property. When lie had poured some
water on it a id returned to look at the offender,
the latter was gone; he had taken the fetters with
him and a portion of Her Majesty’s two-rail fence.
The best thing the law can do with Benjamin
now is to drop the old disagreements and send
him away in the Bush Contingent with the office
of Special Cow-Shaker to the N.S.W. Govern-
ment.
The methods of two men who attempted
suicide in Melb. last week were very ““Vf*
a nice attention to detail landed one of them in
Eternity, while the want of it only brought the
other to the lock-up. The successful experimenter
fastened a 91b. stone round his neck, tied his right
arm to his body with a bootlace, stuffed the other
in his waistbelt, and jumped 10ft. into the bay ana
the Hereafter. The other tied a heavy stone
round his neck with a rope, and dived 70ft. into
the Yarra. On the voyage down, the rope broke,
and the disgusted plunger swam ashore, re-
mounted the hill, climbed the bridge parapet, and
again jumped. He landed in the soup all right,
but he couldn’t sink for want of the lost boulder,
and some interfering persons on the bank baulea
him ashore. Astonishing the number of people
who screw themselves up to jumpmg-pomt
and then find that the rope isn t sound, or the
water is too thick, or the razor is too blunt,
something.
The golden head of Mrs. Fraser, the lady
who shot her husband in the head six months ago
at St. Kilda, glinted in the sunlight last week
above the dock of a Melbourne Court. Appar-
ently that golden head dazzled the jurymen from
the first, for the 12 men tottered a good deal on
their mental foundations all through the case.
Mrs. Eraser challenged nine jurors, all elderly
and more or less bald. As a rule, a golden-haired
lady in trouble likes elderly, bald, fatherly gentle-
men to consider her case, but Mrs. Fraser didn t.
The shot-and-paralysed husband was carried into
Court. Just after the original friction, when Hr.
Fraser got that unfortunate foreign substance
embedded in him, there was understood to ha,ve
been a beautiful reconciliation, but the family
promptly burst up again, and it was evidently
still in a burst state. The perforated husband
said that his wife was a hysterical and shnekv
female, whose incessant breaking-out m a fresh
place had made his life a misery. She was the
sort of person who always went about, metaphori-
cally speaking, with a new idea, and a sens®
of injury, and her bonnet tipped frantically
over one ear. The lady in the dock averred,
on the other hand, that she was a bruised
and pounded woman, and that there were many
stripes on her white skin through the doctors
ill-usage. Then she put down her head and
sobbed. The jury probably believed that if it
sent her to prison all that beautiful hair would be
cut off short, and it hesitated at bringing in a
verdict that would lead to such a disfigurement.
The shortest way out seemed to be by finding
that she was insane, but that would mean con-
signing her to an asylum, and the jury wasn t
sure that her hair wouldn’t be cut off short there
also. So it solved the problem with a rush by
finding her not guilty. Judge a’Beckett asked,
with a glassy sneer in his eye, if it meant that
there was no shooting at all, but it replied that it
didn’t—it had merely decided that the shooting
was accidental. Possibly Mrs. Fraser’s acquittal
was partly owing to her wild offer to go to the
Transvaal in boy s clothes as a British spy. _ The
jury was probably so busy thinking how nice she
would look in boy's clothes that its attention was
distracted. And her despairing sob from the
dock that “she didn’t know how to express
her sorrow, because, after all, she was
only a woman, ”| moved the twelve men to
a big lump in the throat at the right moment.
Of course they knew all along that she was a
woman ; but when a woman says that she is
a woman, and says it the right way, it counts for a
lot. As for the doctor, he came in coldly like a
graven image, and went out the same way. He
has posed all through as a cold, impassive, metho-
dical kind of man. If it was true that he pounded
the golden -haired lady, he seemed the sort of man
who wouldn’t do it off-hand just when the trouble
arose, but would make an appointment to wallop
her on the 10th proximo at 1.31$ precisely if she
had no previous engagement, and enter the
appointment in his diary, and take a receipt for
the spanking when it was over. Melbourne can t
quite decide whether the hysterical lady didn’t
really spank herself and imagine that her husband
did it. But it has decided that there is excellent
grazing for a grass-widow in the dock if she is a
nice-looking grass-widow and weeps at the right
moment.

V.K.: That writer alleges that the saving habit is
a' sure portent of race-destruction, and then (joes on to
quote China. He apparently mixes up a mere political
issue with a racial one. The Mongol is by no means
threatened with destruction as a race, hut given a fair
chance, promises to wipe the floor with the Caucasian in-
an industrial war under a Freetrade flag. Also, the two.
best-known exponents of Thrift, Ikey and M’Bawbee,,
don’t look as if anything were radically wrong with their
innards. “ 8.5.” generalises ; and truth never lies wholly
on one side of any question. In fact a great fundamen-
tal truth is about the most awesome rail-straddler ever in-
vented M.C.W. : Tiik Bulletin never, in all its life, did
what you aver. Head the whole article. The heading was.
the text for a sermon ; not the motto for a policy.
....Makoro: The Bulletin never said, or even thought,,
that the Transvaal would not on the whole be a better
country for the average man to live in if brought under
the British flag instead of left under the domination of
Kruger and Co. But that’s not the point. The situation
is, as it were, that Rhodes, Wernher, Beit, Eckstein
and Co. covet a very fine married woman ; and say
to themselves that they can manage that fine married
woman a lot better than her ignorant husband can.
The married woman is unwilling to be managed by
Rhodes and Co., but they say that’s no matter ; it is
necessary for them to take and manage her in the in-
terests of morality, liberty, and so on M. (White
Cliffs) : It would run to two columns. Wants to be put
in one-tenth the space. This is a paper—not a parish

Peoro : “ How does the canting Bulletin justify this
action of its friend, the traitor Schreiner" (in prohibiting
jubilant demonstrations over the British victories unless
specially authorised by Govt.). Well, as there are more
Dutch than English at the Cape, and as Dutch
and English have to live together after the war,
don’t you think it is better, in the Imperial in-
terests, to suppress any public demonstrations calcu-
lated to intensify bitterness of racial feeling? Schreiner’s
conduct has the approval, probably, of a majority of white
British subjects at the Cape. And the British subject at
the Cape has as much right to his own opinion as
the ditto in London, though people who cant like
you don’t see it Misan : The Court did not decide
Gibson’s case “on its merits”; the Court held
that Gibson, simply because he was a prisoner, could not
possibly be allowed right of action against either the
Crown or the person or persons whose negligence might
be directly responsible for the fact that, while in gaol, he
lost an eye through the explosion of the defective gauge-
glass of a steam-boiler near which he was compelled to
work. The Court, in fact, decided that because Gibson
was under sentence of imprisonment for a certain term he
for that term practically lost all his civil rights—in a
word, that a man within the meshes of the law is an out-
law. Truly a Gilbertian paradox. The Court argued on
convenience, expediency, and so o ;-and yet plainly
enough indicated that Gibson ought to be given some
kind of recompense, so long as he did not re-
cover it by law and thus establish a precedent 1....

A Jingo Wants to Know.
“An Old Sydney Resident” with a German-
Hebraic cognomon sends us the following pro-
foundly silly letter from London :
Whether the attitude you take up about the war from
an Australian standpoint be right or wrong, you appear
to overlook one important fact, viz., that the Boers started
this war by the invasion of British territory. What would
you hare us do ? Clear out of our own country without
the show of a fight ? There were only two alternatives—
either fight or funk ; and if we had chosen the latter,
would that policy have received The Bulletin's approba-
tion T If it is possible for Bulletin to give a straight-
forward answer to this question, it would enlighten a good
many of its readers on a subject which has hitherto been
concealed in its editorial bosom.
What could Britain do? Well, the only thing
Britain could do, as The Bulletin has said over
and over again, was what she did— fight. If a
big country has bullied, robbed, and insulted a
small one, has snatched its territory in time
of peace and only given it back under
extreme pressure, has tried to snatch it again,
has picked a quarrel with it by demanding that
the small country will grant conditions to aliens
that the big country never dreams of granting
itself, and the end of all this shabby insult and
injury is that the small country at last rises up and
hits with all its might—well, the big country can
only hit back. If a big man robs a small one,
slanders him, insults him and runs away with bis
wife, and in the end the small man plugs him in
the eye, there is nothing for the big man to do but
plug in return. When Garibaldi rose against
the old oppressors of Italy, and the Greeks
rebelled against the Turks, and the Poles against
Russia, and the Hollanders against Spain, the
old tyrants, however righteously they were biffed,
could only biff back again. There was no other
alternative. But the necessity didn’t make their
previous conduct one whit less sinful and objec-
tionable. The necessity of killing a man because
you have injured him arises very often, but, how-
ever much other people may admit the necessity
of the execution, they aren’t called upon to admire
the executioner.
The Bulletin has reason to know that the fol-
lowing letter (recently in Sydney Evening News)
is strictly in accordance with the brutal and dis-
graceful facts: —
Last night I went to see if I could find my dog at the
Belmore Police Barracks, and to my horror met with a
sight that even now haunts me. Packed in two small
sheds, about 6ft. square, were over a hundred dogs of
every size and breed, from the low-bred mongrel to the
valuable St. Bernard, wedged together—disease-laden
mongrels side by side with dogs that had been
better cared for than some human beings. There
were so many dogs, and the space so limited,
that the large ones were trampling on the small ones,
several of whom—principally little terriers—were lying
apparently unconscious. The choked breathing of the
poor wretches could be heard a hundred yards away,
and the steam was worse than any furnace. Identi-
cation was almost impossible. I mentioned the kind
of dog I had lost, and a man sought for it in a
hopeless sort of way. He did not find it, but
offered me one somewhat resembling mine, which,
after a careful scrutiny under a lamp-post, I could
see was a well-bred animal, too good for there, and
whose owner was probably anxiously looking out for
him. Out of sheer pity I let him go, in hopes that
he might find his way home again, from which he had
probably been enticed. As I was going away, hearing
some horrible howls, I rushed back to see what was the
matter. Three or four large dogs were strung up
together by their necks by means of pulleys—a sort of
hanging process without the drop—and were fighting
death in a most-horrible manner. I was not allowed,
however, much observation, for as soon as I was
discovered looking on I was ordered off in no uncertain
manner. “ You’ve got your dog ; now go ! So I went.
God forbid that my dog should ever fall among
that lot. I always thought till now that there existed
in Sydney a society for the prevention of cruelty
to animals. If a man punishes too severely
a refractory horse, he is promptly brought to court for it,
but this wholesale dispatch of dogs goes on night after
night unnoticed. It is in . the hope that some more
humane method of housing and destroying these poor
dogs will be found that I have been tempted to write.
Surely an animal so noted for his fidelity and usefulness,
and to whom many persons owe even their lives, should
be worthy of a little humane consideration. So long as
men are paid so much for each dog destroyed, will the
temptation to decoy any and every dog—good and bad,
and conceal it as much as possible from the owner—exist.
Trusting something will be done. J.M.
The foregoing is presumably supposed to be an
improvement on the old Sydney system of destroy-
ing, which was to knock the poor animals on the
skilly with an iron bar! That such horrors as the
E. News’ correspondent describes should be pos-
sible is a ghastly satire on Sydney’s alleged civilisa-
tion, and is perfectly of a piece with all the other
hideousness now being laid bare by the plague-
time sanitary inspectors of the pestilence-stricken
city. An ex-policeman who was formerly chief
dog-butqher, by the way, evolved naturally and
appropriately into assistant-hangman. The curri-
culum of cruelty!

Vic. Premier M‘Lean (writes the Argus),
whose position as a poet was established long ere
he entered political life, has been moved to song
by the events of the war. On Sunday, having a
few hours’ leisure, he gave play to his fancy, with
this result :
The will of a lady who had lost the power
to speak or write was duly proved at London Pro-
bate Court last month. Testimony as to the
virtue of this interesting document was given by
the doctor who attended testatrix and devised a
means of ascertaining her wishes. At his sugges-
tion two packs of cards were printed for the
patient, one bearing the names of her individual
relatives, one name on each card, and the other
pack showing the various items of property at her
disposal. The sick woman intimated by signs to
her solicitor which name was to inherit a particu-
lar lot of property, the name cards being shuffled
and dealt out afresh as each “lot ” was “ put up.”
Finally she picked an executor from the pack, and
as it didn’t happen to include the name of some
other person whom she would have also chosen,
the lawyer wrote the word “Sole?” before the
name of the selected party and she nodded assent.
Then the will was carefullv read over to the
speechless, paralysed testatrix, after which she
put her mark in the right place, and the doctor
and solicitor witnessed her hand and deed.

Inspiring item from the seat of war : “A
soldier in the Berks regiment got ‘ spreadeagled,’
that is, strapped to a wheel, four hours a day for
a month, for going to sleep at his post after doing
36 hours’ duty.
Writes H. W. Massingham, the ex-editor
of London Daily Chronicle :
The colonies were swept into the net by the cunning
hands who made this war in order to put money in their
purse. The Outlanders’ agitation was started simulta-
neously at an immense expenditure in Capetown, London,
Australia, and English Canada. The same lies were told
to the colonies as to us. The same fabrication of Boer
atrocities went on there as here, emanating from papers
in the hands of the two or three South African syndicates
who manipulated the war-boom. By-and-by "they will
find out the truth as we are finding it out.
And how the syndicates did pay their hired liars !
One man who edited a Johannesburg paper for the
Gang got £3OOO • a year, besides large fees for
London correspondence.
THE OLD IKTNO BLOOD.
