A Sydneyite lias got a letter from an
American friend who states that a rubber-adul-
terant has been invented there which can be sold
at G cents per lb., and that 40 per cent can be
added to crude rubber without impairing its value.
The American rights have already been sold for
half-a-million sterling, and the British for £150,000.
Re the basket “ lice ” of which Sydney lias
lately heard so much, a Bulletin correspondent
writes:
I once asked a Sydney cane-furniture manufacturer why
he fumigated all his newly-imported material with sul-
phur. Was informed that the cane was full of small
insects which came with it from its place of growth. The
cane-worker did not object to these on hygienic grounds,
hut because they devoured the cane as white ants do
timber. He showed me canes which had been eaten to
'mere shells containing a dry dus . I know a number of
such cane-furniture workers, but never heard of any
personal ill effects caused by these insects.
The empire slumbers peacefully,
Unsullied by the rod
Of despot rule ; servility
Ne’er thrives on British sod—
That glorious realm of liberty,
Whose rule is just, where all are free,
Where, fearless, each may bend the knee
Before his father’s God.
But lo ! what means yon thunder-cloud
Which gathers from afar,
And spreads its dark, portentous shroud,
The nation’s peace to mar ?
The murmur of each sullen crowd
Swells slowly into menace loud,
To lower the British ensign proud,
Or plunge her realms in war.
On Afric’s soil the crafty Boer,
His gage of battle flings ;
And, thundering, to the empire’s core
His stem defiance rings.
Ye Gods ! she wakes ! she sleeps no more ;
At length the baited lion’s roar,
From sea to sea, from shore to shore,
Is borne on lightning wings.
No Briton brooks to be a slave,
They march from England’s plains ;
Bold Erin’s soldiers cross the wave
To rend the tyrant’s chains ;
Stern Scotia’s sons for vengeance crave
A life for each Majuba grave ;
And, ever foremost of the brave,
They join the tartan trains.
From every State a warrior band,
All loyal to the core ;
From Canada, from Maoriland,
From India’s torrid shore ;
Each swears upon his trusty brand
On kopje, donga, veldt, or rand,
To quell the tyrant, hand to hand,
Or see his home no more.
O’er Austral lands the bugles blow,
And rolls the martial drum ;
Their warlike strains like magic flow,
They reach the Bushman’s home.
From hills where snow-fed rivers flow,
From forests deep, from mountain’s brow,
From out each darksome vale below,
The hardy Bushmen come.
With iron thews, the tracker’s eye,
Though somewhat slouching mien,
Those horsemen bold, ’neath southern sky,
Have sun and weather seen.
They heard their cherished country’s cry,
With eager rush to arms they fly,
With stern resolve to do or die,
For Empire and their liueen 1
Whereupon a Bulletin bard :
On Sunday last excited Mac
Found time to tune a lay
Re Britain and those clouds so black
Which quickly pass away.
When, roaring like a winter gale,
The Lion shakes his awful tail—
A signal for the foeman pale
To tremble with dismay.
Mac sang about the Bushmen Oorp3
Who, clad in kharki suit,
Have “ slouched ” away to track the Boer
And teach him how to shoot.
To them the task will seem but soft;
They’ve gazed upon the weather oft,
And by the Flag that floats aloft
They’ve sworn to “ pot the brute.”
O ! Mac, beware of thoughts sublime 1
Restrain the giddy muse
Who tempts you to express in rhyme
Your patriotic views.
In Parliament your Jingo skite
Has proved you fit to be a knight.
No further need of you to fight
The battle of the Jews.


The Kanaka question in Bundaberg (Q.) is
reaching an acute stage. Sudden deaths, chronic
immorality, furious rioting, are the principal
phases of the trouble. T’other day Govt, was
interviewed re the “walkabout” boy grievance
and it was alleged that hundreds of “ free ” boys
loafed about the town making things hideous.
Well —every town (like every man) has the vermin
it deserves.
Arthur Griffith (Fellow Aust. Instit.
Patent Agents, and Member Patent Law Absoc.,
Washington, U.S.A.), 169 Phillip-street, Sydney.
Patents secured and Trade Marks registered
throughout the world. Tel. 2898.*
Tarantulas are being raised in Australia
for the sake of their webs, the filaments of which
are made into thread for balloons. They are lighter
than silk, and, when woven, lighter than canvas.
Each tarantula yields from twenty to forty yards
of filament, of which eight twisted together form
a single thread. —American Paper.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
A ship in the shape of a corkscrew is the
latest Yankee idea. The idea is a cigar-shaped
craft, whose outer skin shall be furnished with
two flanges running in spirals from the bow to the
stern, the entire outer shell revolving by power
supplied from an inner shell which shall retain its
equipoise. Small models are said to have given
remarkable results of speed. The larger model,
now to be built, is expected to show a speed of
fifty miles an hour.

A bill has been introduced into the United
States Congress providing that every company starting
business must have visible assets to represent the whole
amount of its paid-up capital. This means the total
abolition of the “ goodwill ” item which figures in so
many balance-sheets. The idea would knock on tiie
head a stupendous amount of coinpariy-mongering, float-
ing, stock-watering, inflation, first robbery, and other
swindles. It would also prevent the flotation of a good
many honest concerns, but it would abolish so much
fraud that it is an idea well worthy of imitation. Inci-
dentally it would prevent “ preliminary expenses ” being
put in among assets, which wouldn’t he a bad thing by
any means. If shareholders were willing to pay £IOO,OOO
for a concern, £50,000 being for goodwill, they would
simply have to put up the £50,000 for goodwill and
straightway write it off, and start their co. with £50,000
paid-up capital and £50,000 assets.
Melbourne Table Talk remarks of the Sydney
Harbor Colleries that “it is only in N.S.W. that an Aus-
tralian co. can be found to plank down £150,000 on the
chance of making a good thing.” Alas! even in N.S.W.
they don’t plank. The office and directorate of the Har-
bor Collieries is in London, and most of the planking was
done there. The shares held locally are mostly those
which the vendors got in payment for their “concession.”
The “Joint-Stock Companies’ Year-Book” mentions 21
companies that are coal-mining in N.S.W., and of these 12
are N.S.W. companies, one is Victorian, and eight are
British. N.S.W. has a better hold on its coal-industry
than on most of its local industries, but even there the
hold is none too good.
* * • •
A Victorian law court has arrived at a strange
conclusion. A man received a payment of £3 ss. on
account and gave a stamped receipt. Later on he sent a
statement showing the amountjoriginally due, the £3 ss.
paid, and the balance still due. And as the statement
acknowledged that the £3 ss. had been paid the law held
that it was also a receipt, and fined the trader for not
stamping it. At this rate it might be necessary, with a
long-winded customer, to give a dozen stamps for one
small payment, which would seriously damage the profits.
And if the new reading of the law holds good there are
about £7,000,000 or £20,000,000 in fines to be collected in
Victoria for old unstamped monthly statements.
* * • *
A provision in Vic. Income Tax regulations
renders a taxpayer who fails to notify his change of
address within a month liable to the same penalty as if he
neglected to furnish a return. Consequently many people
have been fined £2. Also notices sent to people from
whom special returns are required are apparently intended
to hopelessly muddle the taxpayer’s mind, and it would
almost seem upon the whole that the only way to escape
fining on one hand or another is to send in returns early
and often.

Thanks to the practical stoppage of the Rand
mines during the latter part of the year owing to the war,
Australia is the world’s biggest gold-producer for 1899
-3,777,5590 z. South Africa follows with 3,533,9450 z.,
and the U.S. with 3,506,6790 z. Canada produced 872,227
oz., Russia 1,164,6030 z., British India 411,1060 z., China
321,2930 z., Guiana 185,5110 z., Mexico 448,7830 z., Brazil
122,4810 z., and Columbia 179,0030 z.

Cripple Creek, Colorado, U.S., it is claimed, is
the greatest gold-producing district in the world, area
and age considered. It is probably the richest section of
ground of its size in the known world, looking at
actual accomplishments and results. Production figures
—ln 1891 the district produced only £40,000 worth of gold,
in ’92 £120,000, in ’93 £480,000, in ’94 £720,000, in ’95
£1,450,000, in ’96 £1,600,000, in ’97, £2,500,000, in ’9B
£3,000,000, and in 1899 £4,000,000.

Melb. Argus is quite shocked at the Westralian
slump, and says that if the mines are not differently con-
ducted the public will refuse to have anything to do with
them, and only deal with shares on an investment basis,
such as was the case after the Mysore and other mining
booms. It alludes to the working of the mines in the
Interests of the stock-market, to the picking out the eyes
of rich shoots, thereby bringing about very large yields
one month and comparatively small ones tiie next, anil to
the concealment of tiie truth generally ; hut says not a
word os to the important part the A rocs itself played in
bringing about the boom. Probably it is not aware it lias
done anything wrong, and would he very much shocked
to find it out. Weil, without the Arous and other such
papers to play upon, there would have been no market-
Doom, ana hence no slump. It was by means of news-
papers like the Arhus that the public were gradually led
to believe not only that the mines were greater than they
are, but that the yields when at their highest would con-
■tinue to increase ; so, too, divs. and share-prices. If it
turns up its own file, it will be seen that it declared these
Kalgoorlie mines to be richer than ever when the market
was on the very edge of the slump.

M‘ Arthur, one of the inventors of the cyanide
process, also one of the English directors of North Mt.
Lyell, has examined that mine and been so impressed that
he declares there are more like 270,090 tons of high grade
ore in the mine than 180,000 tons as previously estimated
by somebody, sometime, somewhere. No details are given
as to how this estimate of M'Arthur’s is made up. Sent
from the North mine to the Mt. Lyell smelters to date
upwards of 4000 tons, said to have averaged over 15 per
cent., but no details as to mining and smelting costs are
given.
• • * •
“G.H.H.” : I speak from a personal knowledge
of the field, and without any biaß in favor of North
Lyell. I admit that, as an example of commercial enter-
prise, the Mt. Lyell M. and R. Cos. deserves all the reward
possible. That “the North has no definite lode or de-
posit anywhere,” is indeed news to those who have seen
their main ore body, which, for 80ft. wide, 140 long, and
320 deep, is as solid, rich and consistent an ore-body as
could he met with. It assayed from 25 to 30 per cent,
near surface, and the same at 80ft., 220 ft. and 320 ft.
respectively. This certainly is a large bunch of “ high-
grade ore.” I think it altogether refutes the statement
of “no defined lode anywhere.” It is officially stated
that this lode amounts to 338,000 tons of ore, which will
show a profit of £lO per ton. Treating 200 tons per day
the lode, even at present depths, shows 4} years’ work
and a profit of £730,000 yearly, or about three times the
amount being earned by the parent Lyell Cos. According
to the general manager’s report issued 18 months ago,
Mt. Lyell M. and R. Cos. had onty about 5} years’ work in
sight. Since then anew development has taken place
which may add another six months’ life to it; so the N.
Lyell compares well with the parent co. in longevity on
its main ore body only. Besides this it has a large lode
west of this body which has been cross-cut for about
70ft. and assays 10 per cent. Then it has portions of the
Lyell Tharsis lode, and another 10 per cent, ore body on
the north end, and it is certain to get the Lyell Blocks
lode. Besides all these, it has many thousands of tons of
low grade ore as a casing on the main ore body. As to
the Crown Lyell, it is not deep enough to expect to get
the North Lyell main ore body. The N. Lyell can get
plenty of flux in the Jukes district. It is a magnificent
idea to say “ the parent Lyell holds the key of the posi-
tion,” when in point of fact it is the N. Lyell which, by
virtue of its position and railway, will get the ore from
surrounding mines. It is indeed interesting fiction to
say the only “ salvation for the North is amalgamation ” ;
it is news indeed to suggest that the North wants any
salvation whatever. The alleged “octopus grip” does
not extend to the North, at all events, and when the
North has its own smelters, the “ octupus grip ” will
change ownership, and place the parent mine in a
secondary position in point of revenue produced.
* * • •
The M t. Lyell amalgamati on project latel y reach ed
the hopeless stage and developed into a quarrel between
the founder of the scheme, W. R. Wilson, and the chair-
man of the N. and S. Mt. Lyell, Lonergan. Wilson says
that the other agreed to the idea of amalgamation; not only
was the subject discussed hut the basis on which th e
North and other mines were to come in was tacitly agreed
on. Lonergan says “ nothing of the kind,” that the
hatchet was never buried, that he can never forget the
cruel treatment received by the North at the hands of the
parent co., that the word amalgamation was never men-
tioned, and that the meeting was all on account of Wilson
wanting the North-mine manager to report on the Lyell
Blocks. Wilson is amazed at Lonergan’s denial, and
Lonergan admits that one or other of them is lying.
Miner to The Bulletin from Charters
lowers : A few facts as to how mining law is administered
in British Papua. Warden Campbell, sitting with two
assessors at Woodlark Island, decided that Webster and
another were entitled to he registered for a 10-actc lease
on the I van hoe (or Kulamadan) line of reef. The Wood-
lark Island G.M. Cos. appealed against the decldon.
Warden Campbell, after consulting both parties, fixed the
date of appeal for the third week in January at Samarai
(the principal settlement in East New Guinea). A certified
copy of tiie Warden’s Court depositions was obtained and
placed before the best mining lawyers in N., with the
result that Mr. Jamieson, barrister, of Townsville, and
Crown Prosecutor for N.Q., was retained at a heavy fee to
uphold tiie Warden’s decision in the Appeal Court of
Samarai. The party witli Mr. Jamieson left Townsville
for Samarai Jan. 10th.* Two days later news reached Cook-
town that Judge Winter, of British New Guinea, had
landed at Woodlark Island on Dec. 13th and had heard
and decided the case, upsetting the Warden’s decision
within an hour. When appealed to, the Judge stated
that the decision was final, and declined to re-open the
case, also that the Warden had no power to fix the date
of appeal. The gross injustice of hearing and de-
ciding the case six weeks prior to the date
fixed upon without giving the parties any notice whatever
seemed to be beyond the Judge’s comprehension (at least
that is the only charitable view that can be taken of the
Judge’s conduct). Another case of equal hardship lias
cropped up in connection with dredging areas. An agent
representing a Sydney syndicate interviewed Gov. Le
Hunte as to whether he would entertain applications for
dredging-areas. The answer was such that the co. ex-
pended over £IOOO to test the Mambare River, and ascer-
tain if it would pay for dredging. They found that it
would, and subsequently made applications for several
areas. When the hearing of the applications came on the
Governor stated that he would not entertain applications
for six months until he had framed regulations governing
dredging ; advts. would be put in Southern papers
invitmg the applications of speculators, and if there
were more than one application for any area, it should be
decided by lot. When the agent of the syndicators inquired
if they would be given a prior right to the areas they had
applied for and tested, in consideration of the money they
had expended in experimental work, they were informed
that no preference would be shown them, but that they
would have to take their chance like the rest.
• * • *
The recent British New Guinea ordinance
authorising the issue of miners’ rights to, and representa-
tion of shares by, Papuans is a step towards making
N.G. mining a syndicate-cum-black-labour monopoly.
No objection could justly be made to the Papuan fossick-
ing or mining for wages in his own country, pro-
vided he was paid the current rate of mining wages, but
with white miners’ wages at £4 per week, and “ boys ” at
10s. a month, the latter will he invaluable for “ shepherd-
ing.” Already, it is stated, some owners of sluicing
claims on Woodlark Island, who hold more ground than
they can personally represent, have discharged their
white employes and put on the required number of
“ boys” to hold the ground.

A fierce and ludicrous faction fight is
raging in the heart of the Brisbane Byrnes-statue
committee re the advisability of representing the
deceased politician as attired in a frock-coat or
dressed in an academical gown. Glassey heads
the frock-coat faction and the academical-gown
party is having a bad time. From The Bulletin
point of view it would be a crime to represent
Byrnes in academical gown, seeing how much he
did for the Queensland frock-coat gang.

Be the petition of certain N.S.W. banks
that the final Australian Court of Appeal shall
still be in England, instead of having a Federal
Court of Appeal close at hand in Australia.
When justice is so far off it is all the more
expensive, therefore the rich litigant often wins
by starving the poor litigant out. And when it is
a fight between a bank with, perhaps, a million
capital and ten million deposits, and a private
person who has no deposits
From what one reads in the latest foreign
papers it would appear that the Boer game, on
final disaster looming, is to shut up a sufficiently
big garrison in Pretoria and defend it, if possible,
for a year or so in the hope of a more-or-less-
gt-neral foreign war. A late cable says that Pre-
toria is preparing for “a two years’ siege.”

The legislative consolidation of the Empire
has become an attractive topic of conversation
among the higher “ law ” society of Sydney.
The very difficulties of the subject add to its attrac-
tion. University professor promises to produce the
nucleus of a workable scheme by an early date.
Almost all are convinced that the day of ruling
through mere figurehead Governors nears the end.
S.A. Leg. Council elections take place
May 19, and the Liberal party is hard at work to
defeat such pronounced Tories as Stirling, Ward,
and Basedow. In S.A. one third of the council
retires, periodically, and unless the coming contests
result in at least five out of the eight members
being progressionists the existing Tory majority
will remain supreme. Kingston will be nominated
to oppose Stirling, the Conservative leader, but he
will not be back in time to address the constituency,
and his defeat looks certain. Another probable
difficulty is the clashing of Liberal and Direct-
Labor candidates.

In Westralia, the other day, a prisoner
gave evidence on her own behalf for the first time
in that province. And W.A. made a wise pre-
cedent by making the prisoner the first witness.
Witnesses are ordered out of court till they give
their evidence so that they can’t alter their testi-
mony to fit in with the statements of previous
testifiers on the same side, but the prisoner can’t
be ordered out of court.
M.L. Premier Seddon’s latest dodge for
raising revenue is juggling with the postage-
stamps. . The idea is the simple, inexpensive one
of changing the colors; only in two cases is the
design altered. No Australian province descended
to these, tricks before the famous misspelling
“ Wakitipu.” This brings M.L. in one respect to
the level of such states as Liberia and the third-
rate republics of S. America, who use this juggling
regularly as a source of revenue.
S.A. Labor party was badly beaten in
the contest for W. Adelaide—Kingston’s seat,
which resolved itself into a conflict between
Labor and Denominationalism. Denny, the
successful candidate, is the youthful editor
of the local R.C. paper; he is a fluent spouter,
and claims to be an advanced Liberal. The
Tories refrained from courting certain defeat;
as W. Adelaide is solidly Radical, they did not
nominate a candidate.
In a N.S.W. court the other day the legal
sharks wept on each other’s bosoms and wiped
their eyes with each other’s tails, because lawyers
who. are also members of the Assembly are not
allowed to do Government legal business for hire.
The obvious reason is that it is undesirable to
place it in the power of an unscrupulous Govern-
ment to buy support with legal jobs; but the
sharks were quite grieved at the injury done to
the country because so many dazzling intellects
were thus unable to lend the State a band for a
consideration.
The most admirable thing about the Aus-
tralian Natives’ Association is the hugeness of its
cheek in claiming to be the one powerful factor in
the making of Australian Federation. Really the
A.N.A. has done nothing towards federation
beyond making occasional audacious assertions
about its splendid success in promoting the cause.
Deakin and Quick are A.N.A. members, but they
would have been good apostles of union had the
Society never been heard of. The A.N.A. is com-
posed mainly of self-advertising political quacks,
and its chief idea is a terror of ever being suspected
of doing anything or thinking anything that would
not be acceptable to the Mother Grundies of piety-
politics.
Apropos a singularly manly and
outspoken sermon upon the South
African question, recently delivered by
Presbyterian Rev. John Erwin, at
Cambridge (M.L.), it is worthy of note
that oidy among the Presbyterian clergy
have there been found, so far, men
with backbone enough to publicly stand
up for what they believe to be the
truth of the matter. Some of the R.C.
clergy are doubtless privately of similar
opinion to that of these hard-headed
Scotsmen ; though, in view of the official
attitude of the local Head of their
Church and of the probability that their
motives would be misrepresented and
their utterances misconstrued, they
have not, thus far, given much voice
to their convictions. But this hard,
silent thinking always represents what
comes, in the long run, to be the
opinion of the cooled-down majority.
“ Will the Boers blow up and
flood the Johannesburg mines?” is the
question which now most deeply
agitates the plutocrats who brought
about the war. Kruger and Co.
have been cautioned that for any
wanton damage to property they
will be held personally responsible ; it
remains to be seen whether they will or
will not succumb to the tremendous
temptation of wreaking vengeance not
upon the British nation, which has during
the war fought them fairly, and has all
along been deceived as to the merits of
the South African quarrel, but upon the
gang which for years has been in-
dustriously organising the present grab. If the
desperate Republicans reduce the mines to ruins
and leave not one stone upon another in
Johannesburg, the loss will fall, not upon
England, but upon the Gang ; the dynamite
will explode, not in the British Treasury,
but in the pockets of Messrs. Wemher,
Beit, Eckstein, Rhodes and Co.

The new Victorian Crimes Act is going
to furnish a lot of excitement. One clause dealing
with indecent advertisements prohibits any advt.
treating “of any complaint or condition peculiar
to females.” The statute didn’t exactly mean all
that, but being a shocked old maid in a poke
bonnet it didn’t like to say exactly what it did
mean, so it decided that woman was an indecent
subject all round. In which respect it was some-
what like the curate who fried in his own blushes
as he remarked that Jonah was three days in the
whale’s society.
Apropos the Sydney colliery Occident, cost-
ing five lives : the miner is one of the men whose
oones civilisation grinds to make its bread. His
risks of life and limb are greater than those of
workmen in almost any other trade. In 60 years
in Great Britain, 60,000 miners have been killed,
and over 5,000,000 injured or disabled. The
average death-and-accident rate is 16 per cent,
per annum of miners employed. The collier is
the greatest sufferer. Of all miners he is the one
whose work is most dangerous, and for whom
fewest precautions are taken.
Auckland Observer (M.L.) publishes,
under the heading, “ A Colonial Menace,” a wild
shriek against the danger of allowing a few
Dutchmen to do lumping work on the wharves.
These Outlanders are denied a vote, and it is de-
manded that they be denied a chance to earn a
living as well. Curious how the desire to throw
out our own Outlander on his ear goes along with
such tremendous indignation because some other
fellow doesn’t take his Outlander to his bosom,
and give him all his possessions, and the right to
boss the country and run the whole show.
Apropos of Private Davey’s threatened
action against the Crown for illegal ejection from
the Vic. Bush Contingent, a Melbourne paper
disinters the Chief Justice’s ruling in a somewhat
similar ease four years ago :
Anything- that Her Majesty appears to promise to a
soldier is merely a matter of her bounty to give or with-
hold, as she thinks fit. Her Majesty, being naturally
disposed to do what is right, and to refrain scrupulously
from doing what is wrong, the anticipation is that Her
Majesty will keep her word ; but she cannot be compelled
to do so But a soldier cannot resign of his own motion.
He is bound absolutely ; but Her Majesty is not bound
for a moment. Her Majesty is not compellable to observe
any contract between herself and her soldiers.
Which seems a very pleasant situation for Her
Majesty, anyhow.
The gory speech of Henry Copeland,
N.S.W.’s new Agent-General, in which he im-
plored England to “blood her pups”—tha,t was
before a shot was fired, and when it was still un-
certain whether any would be fired—was not the
hysterical old battler’s first appearance in that
character. It was Copeland who raged loudest
and longest during the Soudan madness; and
when Parkes wrote his “ letter of dissent ” and re-
signed his seat on account of the awful howl with
which it was greeted, it was Copeland who led the
party from Sydney that pumped beer and pat-
riotism into the electors of Argyle, in opposition
to the old man. Also it was Copeland who, even
after Parkes’ re-election, tabled a motion that
Parkes was unfit to sit in Parliament oh account
of his “disloyalty.” But by that time the tide
was turning—the tide always does turn—and
poor Copeland, could get only an undistinguished
person named Coonan to vote with him.

A new semi-sacred song, entitled “ king,
O Ye Angels,” words by H. 0. Ooglilan ; music,
with organ obligato, by Roland Athan, has just
been published by Allan & Co., Melb. Sung re-
cently in Melb. Town Hall by Mrs. Palmer, and
at Ballarat by Miss Kitty Grrindlay, it was warmly
encored.
A distilled novel:
She was a nice girl, and sang in the local choir.
He was a nice fellow, with a tenor voice, who did not
go to church.
They met at a gipsy-tea.
Ultimately she got him to come to her church and Bing
in the choir. That was 12 months ago.
He’s going to marry the girl that plays the organ next
week, and the-other girl doesn’t sing in that choir now.
A distinguished American being inter-
viewed in Wellington, M.L.: “Do I think Wel-
lington windy ? Waal, yes, it’s windy, but it’s
valuable wind if you only had live people_here.
Why if we had this wind in New York city' we’d
generate it by windmills into electricity and light
the earth. Yes, sir, you have a lot of good wind
going to waste.”
An English paper says it is well known
that about a dozen ne’er-do-well English baronets
are knocking about Australia picking up a pre-
carious livelihood as bark-strippers, gum-diggers,
and station rouseabouts. Might also have further
mentioned restaurant waiters, potato peelers,
rabbit trappers, cooks, and cardsharpers. As
a matter of fact some politicians long ago
contemplated imposing a heavy poll-tax on
dead-beat baronets and other stumped sections of
the old nobility.
New York World gives a pen picture of
Joseph Chamberlain: —
He looks unhealthy, and ages rapidly. His temper is
becoming uncontrollable. In addition he has suffered
heavy financial losses through unfortunate speculations
into which he put his sons. Chamberlain has always been
pitilessly selfish in the pursuit of his own personal
ambitions, so his fall evokes no sympathy. He is not
known to have a solitary personal friend on earth outside
his own family.
Yic. Deputy Postmaster-General says he
will severely fine any departmental officer found
resorting to political influence for personal ends.
But since in no other department has more offen-
sive sweating been practised, or have legitimate
claims been more persistently ignored, it is small
wonder that the sufferers should seek redress
through outside channels. Of course the regula-
tions forbid this, but the regulations are practically
dead and buried in all departments long ago, and
if there is to be a resurrection it should be general,
not special. The Postal Department has always
been singled out for sacrificial purposes.

kan Money Kings. It was at a banquet, and one

is said to have sworn to ruin C., and C. is still
chuckling over the fact that his little remark
pierced the great hide of the other saurian.

officers of the British Army raised from the
ranks, was introduced to the Prince of Wales
when he returned from Omdurman. “I don’t think
we have ever met before ” said Tummy, who
reckons he never forgets a face. “Oh, yes,” said
Fighting Mac, “ I was corporal of the guard when
your Highness landed at Bombay.”

SPORTING NOTIONS.
A.Sydney paper states that clever Jack Brewer,
rhe.Graftet and other Australian nags
P* * campaign in England, has “ taken a great
rancy to English racing stewards, because they see less
\y e gods .) than Australian stewards do as a general rule,
and, consequently, are not very inquisitive.” If Brewer
talked in that strain it was possibly his vanity that prompted
. °” er what looks very like an explanation of the
unkind remarks which have been passed upon his jocfyey-
? .JP English horse-reporters. They have credited him
fa m sln £ two or three races through his apparent lack
of skill and judgment. As a rtew-chum he is judged by
his first performances. The stewards and the public have
seen Brewer beaten where they thought a better rider
could have done the trick. Should they presently
discover any improvement in his style they will thence-
forth be quite prepared to “ cop the Brewer,” no doubt.
* * * *
“Filbert ” : Saturday will see Rosehill Cup run
over 6£ furlongs, but, at time of writing, it is hard to
decide what will start for this or be reserved for Rand-
wick. Old Whakawatea won it last year with 9.3, so that
Sequence, who is undoubtedly the best . sprinter now
racing in N.S.W., should hold a big mortgage on this
year’s Oup with 9.5 (which includes 71b. penalty), being
only 51b. more than he successfully carried in Rosehill
Shorts Handicap, 6 furlongs, last Saturday. This fine son
of imported Far Niente may, however, decline the en-
gagement in order to escape a penalty for Doncaster
Handicap, in which his weight is only 8.5. Despite her
defeat in Rosehill High weight Handicap, 6 k fur., Alice
Mostyn, who carried 9st., no doubt benefited by that
gallop, as it was her first appearance in public since a two-
year-old. She is doing great track-work, and will be
better suited in the Cup with the light impost of 7st.
than in a welter. In the absence of Sequence, however,
writer inclines to the chance of that smart filly, Sweet-
heart, who has to her credit ’99 Moonee Valley Cup, 7 fur.,
when she downed the mile record-breaker, Djin-Djin.
Sweetheart is sprinting well at Randwick just now, and a
further recommendation is the fact that she is in the re-
doubtable Kelso stable, which is frequently successful at
Rosehill.
* * • *
Rosehill (Sydney) form was more closely
watched than usual by the public in view of many of the
performers being engaged in the important events at
A. J.C. Autumn meeting. People were just beginning to
think that Sequence, who is engaged in Doncaster Han-
dicap and Sydney Cup, was not the don he was “ cracked
up to be,” but he set all doubts at rest in the Shorts,
when he started a warm favorite and cantered home from
a good field. Idle Boy, another Doncaster horse, won the
Stewards’ Mile, beating several Doncaster and Sydney Cup
candidates. Hi-Hi, the mare which used to run for J.
M‘Sweeney under the name of Nuka, won Rosehill March
Handicap, when she beat some eight Sydney Cup horses,
a performance which immediately shortened her price for
that event.
• • • *
At a recent race-meeting 61b. over-weight was
inadvertently written by one of the officials on the black-
board opposite a competitor’s name, when, as a matter of
fact, there was no over-weight. This little “ up-to-date-
ism ” was the means of causing Ikey Mo to stretch the
price of this well-performed pony, with the result that it
won easily.
* * * *
“Ancient : Saw a most amusing' piece of by-
play at a Sydney suburban course. A well-known “ com-
missioner,” after a mysterious parley with a “ book,”
took 100 to 10 about a certain horse. Bookie immediately
passed it on in two lots to two other books, who passed it
along ad infinitum. Public took the cue, and quite a
respectable sum was put on in half-so vs. and so vs., in the
way of “ savers.” Had an enquiry been held on the
horse’s running, the books would have shown quite a
respectable backing by the horse’s people, but writer
would like to wager there was not a penny on in a bona
fide bet. And so the game goes on.
* * » »
The latest horror in sire’s-name-reversed horse-
nomenclature is Rotagivan, now running on the Sydney
courses. Rotagivan is an aged mare by Navigator from
Hope, and will probably win a race before long.
• * » *
At a M.L. sports’ meeting-place, one morning
recently, a dog that had evidently lost its master took up
quarters and refused to budge for anybody. Among those
who tried and failed to shift it was a well-known bookie.
The more he thought about the dog, the more certain he
felt that its conduct had some meaning, and suddenly it
dawned on him. The day was Newmarket Saturday, and
there was the straightest of straight tips—Watch-dog.
He took a wager on his own account on Watch-dog, and
fielded heavily against the others—and now he is looking
for that dog to apologise.
• * • «
Noble Lady, winner of Granville Stakes at
Rosehill (Sydney) on Saturday, is the first of imported
Nobleman’s stock to win a race. The filly is very much
on the small side, and looked a veritable pony as she
walked away from a good field at her back coming up the
straight.
• • • «
South Australian jumping record was beaten
at Mt. Pleasant show this month, when Brooklass, leaving
the other competitors at sft. llin., was put to a 6ft. jump,
and left a clear couple of inches of daylight between hoof
and hurdle.
• • • •
The Bulletin forgot to mention that Yol. 9 of
Miller’s Australian Racing Chronicle was published a few
weeks back. Vol. 9, covering the space between Aug. Ist,
1899, and Jan. 31st, 1900, provides much study of the
awful foolishness of backing “ form,” or putting faith in
favorites during that period. The price of Miller’s biennial
moral lesson is only five “ bob,” and no horsey home
should be without it.
* * • *
The impregnable Collingwood tote still merrily
defies the local d’s. and pays prices which should bring
the blush of shame even to the boiler-plated cheek of
Ikey Mo. Owing to its having been the scene of several
savage battles the tote is now known in Collingwood by
the name of “ Wrensburgh.”
♦ * * *
At a north-western N.S.W. town recently, when
a selection of horses for South Africa was being made, a
noted “fly” cocky offered an 8-year-old, which was ap-
parently fit enough, but was rejected as “ too old.” The
horse was then taken away, had his tail and mane docked,
and had a new rider “ put up,” with orders to canter
round town and make him lively. After the spurt the
horse was again offered, and bought for 17 guineas.
• * * *
In Sydney premier cricket on Saturday Central
Cumberland kept its place at the head of the competition,
beating E. Sydney by 190 runs. Pye made top score
of the day—l 33. Farquhar’s score of 70 was a gallery per-
formance, as he hit 9 tourers, 2 fives, and 1 sixer. Cricket
kept up its glorious uncertainty by the easy defeat of
Paddington by S. Sydney—Marsh and Mcßeath simply
mowed down the strong Paddington bats. Central Cum-
berland now holds the lead with 10 points. Waverley is
next with S, and then N. Sydney and S. Sydney with 6
points each.
* » • «
The present manner of placing the teams in
N.S.W. electorate cricket is : a win counts two points, a
draw one point, while two points are deducted for each
match lost. The unsatisfactory results of this method
are plainly shown in the fact that Central Cumberland
must now win the Premiership, bar accidents, although
Paddington inflicted on them their only defeat, and are
themselves undefeated. Present method does not en-
courage what the public want to see—free cricket. One
side goes in and makes a big score for a few wickets and
“ declares,” the other, hopeless of victory, plays a stone-
wall game to rob the other side of the extra point, unless
they happen, as has occurred, to want that particular
team to pass some other in the averages. The only fair
method of calculation is by averages—W2., the number of
runs made and wickets lost for and against each club.
* * * *
Clem Hill was 23 on March 18. He has now
been playing in flrat-class oricket for six years. His first
great score (160, not out, against Stoddart’s team in
[arch, ’96) was made when he was a schoolboy of 17.
* * * *
Major Poore, the great batsman who would
have been one of the M.C.O. team in the trip now aban-
doned, is with Buller in S’Africa. The Major is 30, and
was unheard-of before his sudden jump to the top of last
year’s batting averages. He made 1551 runs, averaging
91 per innings. He is nearly as tall as the giant Bonnor,
and is one of the finest swordsmen in the British army.
* * * *
Old-Timer ”: Golden Gate A.C. (Sydney)
billed and boomed Paddy Harden (“ of Balmain ”) and
Jimmy Dempsey (“of Waterloo”) as if they were cham-
pions, and a packed house resulted on Saturday night.
Before half the first round had expired Dempsey got a
comparatively light punch on the eye (the first blow that
landed), and sat down till counted out. He walked out
of the ring with his hand over the wrong optic (he had
apparently forgotten which was hit) like a milksop school-
boy, while the crowd vented its disgust in jeers. Manage-
ment tried to make amends by matching Harden against
a pick-up, but the extra became very uncertain on his
feet, and had to be led outside the ropes inside 30sec.
1 was marvellous that the crowd behaved so well in the
circumstances, for never was such a frost seen in a boxing-
hall. Writer tips that the present pug. boom will collapse
inside a few months if these greed-prompted affairs are
persisted in. Promoters have a notion that anything will
go down, hence the prevalence of cheap and nasty novice
tournaments j but there should be no draw in these now
as most of the dirty-legged, beer-sodden, broken-limbed’
foul-mouthed competitors come up from the city’s filthiest
areas, and people take a big plague risk in getting within
coo-ee of them.
* ♦ * *
Very nearly a record house for Sydney rolled up
to see Vic. Tot Higgins and Maorilander Otto Cribb fight
the best of 20 rounds at Metropolitan A.C. (Sydney) on
Monday night. Some were there before 6, and by 8 the
doors had to be closed ; then there was not a nian less
than 2300 in the hall, and as each of these paid 10s., ss.
or 2s. 6d. for admission the pugs, must have netted a big
sum, which they whacked at the rate of 60 and 40 per
cent, to winner and loser respectively. It was a clawing
scrambling, clinching fight, as a rule, with Cribb very
much in evidence the whole time. Tot displayed unusual
sprinting abilities, and had Cribb chasing him round the
square frequently. Higgins often operated well with both
hands, and once or twice had Cribb swaying in his tracks,
but the hard-headed, tough-hided, huge-fisted Maori-
lander could have endured twice as much and lived. Tot’s
comparative cleverness stood out, but Cribb’s crude
methods—many of which proved more effective than
. n the case with the most skilful—sorely
tried the Victorian at times. Tot laughed and so did the
onlookers at Otto’s efforts to catch his man when Higgins
took it into his head to “do a Botany.” They would get
together and bang away right and left vimfully and
viciously, and the crowd would get up on its feet and yell
again with a mighty yell that must have been heard a
mile off. Both were in good trim, but Cribb’s condition
was much the better. They went palpably weak several
tunes and came again, though in the last few rounds
of the 13 fought it could be plainly seen that Higgins had
shot Ins bolt, and it was only a matter of time when he
would reach the end of his tether. Clean knock-down
blows were conspicuous by their absence. Higgins scored
one in the first term, and, though he himself was on the
floor at least eight times afterwards, it was never through
a fair punch ; there was more push or shove in the cause
that produced the effect. In one round, Tot tripped him-
self and went sprawling, and twice, as the gong clanged
for corners, he walked over to his opponent’s chair. The
roughing and the pace told on Higgins, also his hands,
particularly the left, went back on him. After the tenth
round, which was one of the most willing, Tot got weaker
and Cribb became correspondingly stronger. While in
his corner resting, before the 14th round, Higgins’seconds
threw up the sponge, thus terminating a most exciting
though not too clever struggle.
* * * *
“Old Timer”: Watched Cribb and Higgins at
it on Monday night, and got badly excited often. Never
saw two men put ’em up in such good physical trim
especially Cribb, who could have stayed there a week.
The way he improves every fight is surprising ; his leg-
work occasionally would have brought credit to the
cleverest. No veteran was ever cooler. Both were
exceptionally plucky and resourceful. Neither shirked
his work a moment. Higgins occasionally gave us a dis-
play which recalled the Tom Lees—Peter Jackson fl«-ht
for the Australasian Championship when old Joe Harper
coming away disgusted, told the crowd outside that Lees
was four laps ahead and he was afraid Jackson hadn’t foot
enough to catch him. Cribb dodged and ducked wonder-
fully well in his own peculiar fashion, and frequently
brought those ham-like hands into violent contact with
Higgins’ head, neck, or ribs. Otto used the left really well
now and again, and amply demonstrated that if he knew
as much about the game as Tot he’d be champion of the
world. There’s more of the real, right-down fighting
instinct in him than in any man we’ve seen since Joe God-
dard- The hitting didn’t look too stiff, and neither
showed much punishment about the face, but these two
can stand it better than most others. That Cribb can
Eunch, and punch hard, there’s no doubt; he and cyclist
arry Corbett had a quiet turn-up at Seale’s gymnasium
before Corbett met Wylie last Monday week, and Larry
caught one that left him in doubt for three davs as to
whether his ribs were broken or not. Otto has engage-
ments with heavyweights Broughton and M'Coll coming
off, and more to follow—bald-patch Jim Barron being
among the number; meanwhile other pugs are growling
at Cribb’s monopoly of the game. He’s a popular idol on
a very high pedestal just now, and writer tips him to
stay there a long time if he is judiciously' managed and
doesn’t get big head.
* • * *
Much-boomed pug-match between “the Poet”
( p ete McCoy), of Sydney, and Melb. McAuliffe at Metrop.
A.C. (Sydney) last week turned out a fiasco. McAuliffe
as far as appearances went, hadn’t trained an hour ■ he
was hog-fat. Four rounds of make-believe on the Cabbage
Garden man’s part, and real right-down serious but not
too effective graft by the whilom owner of the flowing
tresses, saw McAuliffe find the floor somehow —certainly
not in the orthodox fashion, per medium of a punch—and
stay there on his haunches, looking round complacently,
until the referee counted 10. Then he got up in a hurry
but was disqualified for being a palpable non-trier. The
inevitable barney followed—Mac’s seconds and a section
of the push taking a hand. ‘ 1 Fight on ! ” said the referee
ghe win, tie or wrangle mob had bested him). “ The
Poet made things hot in the next 3min. and once sent
the chicken-hearted Victorian through the ropes whence
his seconds carried him to his chair as the gong sounded
corners. That settled Mac, he wasn’t taking any more.
The loser’s whack (less £4 paid for ex.’s) went into the
club coffers. McAuliffe is now labelled “ not wanted ”bv
boxing promoters.
* * * *
Australian lovers of the noble art will be pleased
to hear that good old Peter Jackson indignantly denies
that he is starving in the States, as was stated in a
Yankee par. which went the rounds of the local press.
Jackson says he foolishly allowed the many thousands
which he handled in his palmy days to slip through his
fingers, but he is not so bad in either health or pocket as
the paragraphist would make out.
* * * *
At Philadelphia (U.S.A.), Feb. 13, that whilom
terror, Australian Joe Goddard, now a physical wreck
stood up against Sailor Sharkey, a man who would have
supplied a tough task for Barrier champion Joe in his
very prime. Goddard stood a fearful bashing and was in
sorry plight when his seconds skied the rag about the
middle of the fourth round. But Joe, as big a man-eater
as ever at heart, insisted upon continuing and made a
direful-looking dig at Sharkey. They mixed things
fiercely. Thousands of onlookers fell over each other with
excitement and created a pandemonium—yelling, arguing
and roughing. The fighters went to the floor together
and rolled about punching like men possessed the whole
time. The referee could do nothing. Eventually they
were separated. Joe is said to be as full of lire as of yore,
but his years and badly-broken-up physique rendered him
a cinch for the young, strong, agile sailor.
News from the States tells that Maorilander
Fitzsimmons is very much on the job : tie has posted
£lOOO and offers to fight the present world’s champion,
Jeffries, and, tailing him, sailor Sharkey, Gus. Ruhlin, or
Kid M‘Ooy in that order. “ 1 don’t care when the fight
takes place, and I don’t care who faces me,” says the
business-like man from these parts, “and if none of those
heavyweights care for the job, then the rest of the middle-
weights may come along and I’ll undertake to toe the
mark at middleweight limit.” This is as straight as that
auburn-haired freak’s disaster-bringing left.
* * * *
EkPremier N.S.W. middleweight Mick Dunn and
Vic. heavyweight Fred Preston, who has been yearning
for a cut at alleged champion Felix, are to meet at
Metrop. A.C. (Sydney) April 17—catch weights. Burly
Fred is no wonder in his division, while Mick occupies
the top rung of the 11.4 class—hence, Dunn should very
nearly come out a-top.
• * • *
Re Bulletin pars (24/3/’00) anent Australian
Mr. Mackintosh’s performance at Monte Carlo last
month. We have another here at least as good, if not
better, in Mr. A. E. Smith (“ Oashmore.”) lie beat
Mackintosh and others for the interprovincial champion-
ship two years ago, and still owns the honor.
Recently at N. Brighton (Vic.) he put up a performance
that will probably stand the assaults of years to come.
Shooting from the outside distance of 34yds. “Cashmore”
triumphed over 25 other competitors with three noms.
each and gathered in first money having 28 kills in front
of his name. Losing his third nom. early he survived 6
rounds on the second and carried the first right through,
grassing 38 birds of a possible 40. Mr. Snuth is a Vic.
native. As a wing shot he’s head and shoulders above
any other man in the provinces now, and might be
reckoned a “ cert.” to beat Mr. Mackintosh any time.

Plunging champion of Bananaland, W. H.
Beattie, stayed under water lm. 6sec. after winning that

couldn’t remain submerged 2jmins. Beattie won. ’Tis

* • * *
As it has been for years past, Balmain Swimming
Club’s two teams (A and B) fought out the final of N.S.W.
Amateur Swimming Association Water Polo Competition
on Saturday. “A ” team won (it invariably does) by 6
goals to 2. Other clubs take little or no interest in the
game; they never practise, and forfeits are plentiful.
The standard of play is much below that of 15 years back,
which suggests the question—why is this fixture tolerated
by the ruling bodj r ? The public won’t stand water-polo
as served up now, and all swimmers, bar Balmain men,
are full to the brim of it.
• » • •
Two out-of-the-way sprint performances were
put up at Sydney Cleveland-street Public School Swim-
ming Carnival on Saturday. “Mud” Bishop covered
90yds. in 55|sec., and got a special prize for the feat,
while Otto Kong Sing, erst schoolboy champion of the
province, easily threw same distance behind in 61, and
looked as if he could have squelched Mud’s time had it
been called for.

‘‘ Q.” : Re the Marquis of Queensberry’s death
and the doubt expressed about him being the author of
the famous boxing-rules bearing his name. The recently
deceased Marquis knew very little about the game, but
his father, Lord Drumlanrig, was the best amateur of his
time. The late Mr. John Chambers, well - known as a
sculler, amateur boxer, editor of Land and Water (he
was the journalist who accompanied the late Capt. Webb
m his famous ’cross the Channel swim), was the real
°f the M.Q. rules. Chambers submitted them to
the Marquis, and requested the privilege of naming the
code after his title. They were brought into existence in
connection with the Queensberry Cup Competition at
Lime Bridge running-grounds.

Streak ”: Never had an exalted opinion of

Inter-association bowling - match—Metrop. v.
Northern—at Newcastle (N.S.W.), proved a big take-down
for the Sydney section, who looked upon the task as easy.
Home trundlers had the surplus points in three out of
four links ; Hannigan’s Redfern quartet was the only
one on the visiting side to score a win, and it beat
Silcock s four by 30 to 16. A particularly strong rink
(Reeder’s) from Balmain finished up worse than any other
having 43 to 13 notched against it. Totals—Northern’
118 ; Metrop., 84. Such a genuine surprise has rarely
been sprung in N.S.W. bowling.
“Bias”: There’s trouble over the personnel of
N.S.W. team for Interprovincial bowling match against
' ictoria. Champion City players, though having one
more rep. than any other of 13 clubs in the 32 chosen
and three more than some, have kicked over the traces
and withdrawn all their men who would allow themselves
to be withdrawn (three out of four) from the combina-
tion. The grievance is that they have been improperly
placed in the teams, and their green should be one of the
three favored. Committee responsible doesn’t see this.
Other clubs have just as good men, individually—and
there are several better greens. Besides, City has had a
lot of the “ fat ’ hitherto. This uncalled-for action will
hurt bowling generally, and tend to still further widen
the breach existing between City and most of the other
13 bodies forming N.S.W. B.A.

Sydney Metrop. A.C. owns a capital piece of
clock-work mechanism that does away altogether with
the time-keeper at fig’llts, and lets the crowd see the cor-
rectness of this important part of the biz. for itself A
big bell operated by electricity clangs noisily at the end
of each round (3min.) and at the close of th® minute’s
space, while a large hand on a plainly visible dial tells off
the number of secs, a man is down. Writer has an idea
that this is the famous old York-street Club’s timing
clock in a new dress.

Extract from letter from the Hielands :
Macpherson’s two sons and Sandy Chisholm’s son are
going with Lovat’s scouts to South Africa. They have
been at Beauly for the last fortnight learning to ride and
shoot. Alec. M. is aide to his father the merchant, and
his experience ranges from tracking the red herring to
its lair to wrestling with the wild pen’orth of goodies.
His brother Tonal has had actual experience of cauld
steel, being the legally-bound apprentice of Sandy Beg
the tailor, while Chisholm’s son has for years past been
professionally engaged in punching the murderous 21b.
loaf.
Be these the “ trained deer-stalkers and gillies,
unerring shots, and accustomed from infancy to
the hardy little ponies of their native hills ” of
which we read in the Jingo press ?
The most cheeky feature of Vic. postal
service is the 2d. letter-card, which is about
equal for purposes of correspondence to a
quarter of a sheet of note paper. The letter
card has a limited sale among citizens who can’t
find time for putting a sheet of paper into an
envelope, but inasmuch as it needs more licking
than an envelope it saves very little trouble. It
weighs about the sixth of an oz., and states in red
tape—“lf anything be enclosed in this letter-
card it will be treated as an insufficiently paid
letter” which is to say that being twice the
area and only half the thickness of an ordinary
penny post card the Postal Dept, charges the extra
penny for gumming and perforating it at the
edges.
London Daily News war-item :
Cooper, of the 19th Hussars, was on outpost duty, and
was wounded in the arm while shooting at the enemy
from behind a large rock. He remained at his post until
dark, and then rose with the intention of walking into
the camp, but in the darkness he mistook the road, and,
walking down the wrong side of the hill, was taken
prisoner. He was detained until the morning. His
wound was dressed and he was well looked after, and he
was sent back with a tin of jam and a box of chocolate.
The Australian papers print dozens of items like
this—in fact, the evidence supplied by British
soldiers in the field is practically all like this. And
then in the next paragraph the papers generally
get back to the old blood and butchery lie, and
say that the brutish inhumanity of the Boers puts
them outside the pale of civilised races.
“ Snarleyow ” : Premier Lyne and his col-
leagues, in quarantining several blocks of more-or-
less-plague-infected Sydney city buildings and
employing 1000 men to clean them up, made the
proviso that the 1000 were to be at once inoculated
with plague prophylactic, thus ensuring the men
being unable to do anything for one, or two, or
more, days after the operation. The 1000, too,
were to be lodged and fed in one of the
“ clean ” wharf-sheds in the dirty, infected
area, pigging together on the floor amidst
the mephitic vapors of,filthy Darling Harbor, ayid
with the foulness of the loathsome places they
have to clean clinging about their’ nevermhanged
clothes. It will be a miracle if an epidemic of
some sort does not break out among that 1000 men
selected, it is said, from the unemployed attending
the local Labor Bureau—men who are ill-nourished
and therefore ready to fall victims to any disease
that comes their way.

««Curryface ” : The percentage of deaths
from bubonic plague in India appears to be all
against the total-abstainer. The pnestly and
sacred Brahmins, non-alcoholic and vegetarians,
had a larger percentage of deaths among them
than the low-caste chuckler or mochee bootmaker,
who boozed and ate carrion; and the Mahomme-
dan who esohewed alcohol in favor of coffee and
water, went under more quickly than the Parsee
who tippled.

“ David G.” on certain decaying substances :
John Norton’s recent indictment of Sydney Corporation
tip and the tip-fed cows is an old story well-known to all
who live near the Sydney common. It’s a pity John, who
has been an alderman for over a year, never before found
out the abomination. Better late than never, however,
though the denunciation would have been more effective
had it come from someone else —say from that medical
Alderman and M.L.A. who interjected that the hospital
poultices are “ supposed to be burned,” and then made a
rambling speech for the defence. Nothing could better
show the imbecility and helplessness of the whole
aldermanic gang than the way it went about trying
to find a substitute for its beloved dirt-heap,
and at last hit on the idea of lightering the
rubbish to sea, if it could find a lighterman to take the
job. The aldermen have had the whole question before
them for years, during which they have been coquetting
with various “destructors." They had no difficulty in
making up their minds about buying an organ, hiring an
organist with whom they can’t agree, and building
£600,000 worth of markets. All these things are
luxuries with which Sydney could very well dispense for
years to come; but the crying need for a furnace or
furnaces in which the rubbish of Sydney could be burned in
a civilised fashion is ignored. The truth is that though
Sydney wears clothes it isn’t civilised. Itdoesn’t put an un-
duly high value on beads and brass wire, but it loves the
gaudy and meretricious in art and architecture. It will run
miles after a brass band, not caring a straw whether it is
playing in tune or not. It went mad over the distinction of
having the biggest organ, and now its people are just as
wild as a tribe of Solomon Island head-hunters at the idea
of sending out their braves to fight for laurels, and
bring back dead Boers’ teeth as trophies. Now we see in
this affair of the corporation tip what a very thin veneer
(or is it varnish T) of civilisation has been laid over the
corporation, which is supposed to represent the wealth,
intelligence, and science of Sydney—dirty, diseased, ill-
built, and ill-paved Sydney.

“ Sidelight ” : Clay Clements should dress his hair dif-
ferently if he wishes to impress Sydney audiences. The
geometrical precision of that scalp-line up the centre of his
head recalls the hidden-away portraits of our mothers’
school-friends. Clay’s hair is always in the way. Thus
in his denunciation of Leah—his great chance—one’s
attention, which should be riveted upon the actor’s words,
is really centred on his painful efforts to keep that hair
out of his eyes. Clay should “ git ’is 'air cut f”

Dear Bulletin. —That talented Australian citizen, late
G. B. Allen, did not die “ neglected and unknown ”
(B. “ Red Page," 10/2/’OO). His reputation as a composer
was made before the Gilbert and Sullivan operas were
heard of. His “Castle Grim,” book by R. Reece, is, in
fact, the archetype of modern comic opera —nonsense
without burlesque. Another composition of his was “The
Galway Rose,” a rollieking, Irish domestic opera, first
staged, I believe, in Manchester. In his younger days,
Allen organised and ran an opera co. of his own through
Australia and the East. Loveday, Irving’s well-known stage-
manager, was his first violin, and “Private Secretary”
Thornton first faced the footlights in a thinking part in the
co. ■ Allen’s last work for the stage was an operetta in con-
junction with a Queensland writer. This was promptly
accepted by a backblock co., whieh repaired to its native
fastnesses without leaving its address, and its doings
the press recordeth not. Allen’s songs have often been
sung by Sims Reeves, and other leading singers, and an
anthem of his is rendered weekly in St. Paul’s. His
“ Little Bird so sweetly Singing ” is a favorite with Amy
Sherwin ; and when the prima-donna was last in Bris-
bane the composer and singer appeared, hand in hand,
on the platform in response to a vociferous call. Allen
used to say that for musical purposes a song depended on
the first two lines, and that without touching words it
was impossible to compose touching music. An exception
he made in the case of Balfe, “ to whom it was indifferent
whether he worked upon the Newgate Calendar or the
Psalms of David.” I have known Allen decline to hear his
own songs sung, with the remark, “ I don’t want to hear
the things murdered.”— Ciiaput.

“ Skull ” : The passing of “ Widow O’Brien ” at Melb.
Royal was a welcome event, for the reason that the latter
end of the show was about the least ingeniously-devised
variety-entertainment yet dished up to Melbourne. There
was absolutely no fun. on the Bristol, the humor of the
comedy-farce being exhausted in Mrs. O’Brien’s villa, and
the whole thing contracting n fearful sickness imme-
diately it touched ship-board. But stay, that after-part
was fiendishly comical in vagrant spots whilst Sheridan
in a hermaphroditic disguise recited serious poetry at a
series of living pictures. Since haggard man with un-
clipt talons first began to comb rhymes out of his long
jj a j r never has worse poetry been made than the patriotic
s | o ps with which Sheridan introduced those unfortunate
scen es. The poetry was longer than the pictures, and
sounded like the desolate maunderings of lunacy induced
by over-indulgence in “ Absent-Minded Beggar ” and
squalid patter-rotic songs of local origin.

“ F.R.”: The Sunday-school catechism is a necessary
text-book for the creed problemists of the stage. In
“ ihe Jewess,” at Sydney Royal, a portion of the Lord’s
Prayer is dragged into the Angelus. The Angelus was
instituted 30 years before the Lord’s Prayer was ever
heard of. That was when Jerusalem was golden. It
might interest the Royal stage-manager to know that the
Angelus is tolled in nine strokes with pauses after every
three, and then fifteen strokes continuously. All of this
has its significance, whereas the irregular ding-dong that
goes on at the Royal savors strongly of the ludicrous.

when that horror was recently produced at Ipswich (Q.) j

has not the slightest resemblance to any piece of the
same name which may have been played here in a tent.
The Saturday-night gods at Melbourne Alexandra are
probably the most youthful lot of 500 playgoers to be
found in any theatre-gallery in the wide world. The
average age of their cheerful laughter doesn’t sound a day
older than 14. And they do laugh. When Hamlet affects
to mistake Polonius for a fishmonger those boys simply
roar. Heaven knows what would happen if the gloomy
Prince were to accuse the old weariness of keeping a tripe-
shop.
Extract re the madness of Hamlet from Dampier’s
theatre-programme: “Shakespeare was too great an
artist and too earnest a teacher to express his loftiest
thoughts and his subtlest speculations through the mouth
of an irresponsible being.” Nonsense ! Thoughts as
lofty and speculations as subtle have been given to the
world by avowed madmen. Shakespeare knew of, and
noted, the near alliance of genius and insanity, a.n
alliance so near that it is often difficult to say which is
which. Hamlet-may have been a deliberate artistic ex-
emplification of a condition so common that Lombroso
has almost established it as a law.

Aram ” goes for anything.

effectually destroy them —fleas and all.

College, Hawthorn, Melbourne, Victoria.
The Bulletin is glad to be able to
announce that Mr. S. T. Whidden, M.L.A. for
Cook Division, Sydney city, is now out of danger.
Mr. Whiddon was suffering from bleeding of the
retina, and after 13 weeks of exquisite agony the
affected optic had to be removed to save the sight
of the other and obviate the imminent risk of fatal
results.
Richard Thomas Hooker, of Singleton
(N.S.W.), celebrated his 100th birthday bjr a
gatliering in the local church, the other day. Five
generations were present, and the ancient “ re-
cited a set of verses he had learnt in his twelfth
year.” When a man nears the century he remem-
bers much more clearly the things that happened
in his childhood than those which occurred the
month before last.
Curious that that ghastly story of the
Queensland woman who wanted to get rid of her
newly-born infant, and persuaded her 13-year-old
daughter to burn it alive, should have made hardly
a noticeable ripple on the mental surface of the
community. Australia is profoundly shocked at
very , much smaller atrocities some thousands of
miles away.
The shooting at Harrismith of the Scotch-
man, M‘Lachlan, for refusing to serve against
Britain when commandeered, has been made the
theme of Jingo diatribes innumerable, and several
Australian bards have written stirring poems
thereon. And now it turns out that Chamberlain
publicly stated in the Commons that he could
find no foundation for the story. And he ought
to know.
Henry Dodds, director of Broken Hill
Block 10, B.H. North, Silverton Tram, and other
gilded co.’s, who died lately in Melb., had just
such a fatal illness as scores of miners have. It
dated from Gov. Beauchamp’s visit to 8.H., when
Dodds was among the banqueters. Coming out
of the hot room at midnight he struck the same
dry, cold, piercing air which cuts into the miner
every night as he comes out of the shaft from hot
and often hot-and-wet workings. For every miner
killed by lead at Broken Hill, at least half -a-dozen
go under before pneumonia and other rapid lung-
diseases.
Benalla (Vic.) Shire Councillors put up a
record for honesty by deciding to apply the shire
funds only to those purposes for which the rates
are collected, and resolved that any Absent-
minded Beggarism must be done by private
subscription. But the relief of Ladysmith came
about, and Brownjones hoisted the only flag in
the place, and Smithjones, not to be outdone, hung
out nis wife’s gaudiest petticoat, and the fire-bell
clanked and the church-bell clanged, and to keep
up the “dignity” of the town the twelve wise
men broke their good resolve, and sent Roberts a
£5 congratulatory cable. It would be interesting
to know what a dog-tired general said about
Benalla when he got the message.
H. C. Lingham, of Melbourne, who dedi-
cated a rather awful “Threnody” to Lord
Kitchener, has been made happy by the receipt of
an autograph letter from that square-jawed war-
rior. Also the Queen has, by deputy, graciously
returned thanks for a copy of the little volume.
Mr. Lingliam’s Threnody relates to the “ Last
Hours of General Gordon’s Lion Heart at Khar-
toum,” of f& Kitchener gets the dedication for being
the Resurrector of British Supremacy thereabouts.
All things considered, The Bulletin would
advise Lingham to lay a poem re the Australian
Contingents upon the altar of Joe Chamberlain,
and apply by letter for a C.M.G.-ship.

La Grippe’s Antics.
VIOLENT PALPITATION.
Two Days’ Treatment Effects
A PERFECT CURE.
The Case of
Mrs. C. CARTER and FAMILY,
(by our special.)
Every springtime, for several year past,
when the parks are yellow with dandelions,
large numbers of Adelaide residents are
attacked with a distressing malady known
locally by the varied terms, “influenza,”
“ dandelion fever,” and “hay fever.” With
the utmost impartiality it attacks both high
and low, emptying both the factory and the
halls of Parliament. Medical science strug-
gles vainly with the disease ; in fact, the
doctor and his family are often amongst the
first victims. We were, therefore, very
pleased to hear that Mrs. Arthur Carter, of
Pulsford-road, Prospect, Adelaide, had found
a successful remedy for this dire complaint.
On being interviewed by a reporter, this lady
very kindly related her experience :
“ It was last spring, just about this time ;
the fields were all yellow with flowers, when
I had my four children all laid up at the
same time with influenza.”
“Can you tell me their symptoms, Mrs.
Carter ? ”
“ They were very bilious, with that sort
of feeling as though they wanted to be
sick and retch up, and yet could not do so.
They had no appetite at all —I could not
tempt them to eat with any dish I set before
them. Their mouths were nasty, with a
white coat over the tongues. There was a
croupy cough from the chest, and phlegm
always coming up. A drawing-in pain in
the stomach, like a fainting coming over
you. Round under their jaws their necks
were all swollen up as though they had the
mumps. Of course, I had them all in bed,
and the youngest, my little girl, was in such
a state of fever that she became quite de-
lirious. And I noticed 'hat they all turned
yellow in the face —quite a greenish yellow.”
“Did you send for a doctor, Mrs. Carter ?”
“ No. I have always tried to avoid doc-
tors ; and besides, I had been strongly ad-
vised by a friend of mine to try a remedy
she had the utmost faith in : and that, I
may tell you, was Clements Tonic. I gave
all my children a course of that remedy.”
“ What was its effect ? ”
“ First, it sent the children off into a good,
sound sleep ; and when they awoke, it was
with a good appetite and a much-improved
appearance. After that their cure was so
rapid that in two days they were quite well.
There was no sign of biliousness, white-
coated tongues, croupy cough, or swollen
necks ; the skin was the natural colour-, and
the feverishness had quite gone. You can
imagine my feeling of joy, and my thankful-
ness to Clements 'I onic.”
“ But I hear, Mrs. Carter, that you have
personally derived signal benefit from Cle-
ments Tonic. Will you kindly tell me about
that ? ”
“ That is true ; but I wished to tell you
about the children first. My own trouble
was chiefly a violent palpitation of the heart.
I made sure I was gelting heart disease ; it
would thump, thump, thump, like a wheel
in an engine with a missing cog. The pain
of it would seize my chest, and almost choke
me for want of breath.”
“ What were your other symptoms ? ”
“Indigestion, with a heavy pain above
the stomach. I have often thought this
arose from my drinking so much tea, and
for that reason I now make my tea much
weaker in strength, and drink less. I had
a sharp, piercing pain between the shoulders.
There were times when this pain came to me
Buddenly, without warning, like a dagger
being driven into my back It would cause
me to scream out, and be for the time quite
helpless. Another trouble was my chest;
there a pain like bronchitis racked and tore
me with a burning soreness all over my
lungs. I would cough and cough as though
striving to bring up all the flesh and blood
in my inside. As you may have guessed,
proper rest was quite out of the question.
No position would give me comfort —it was
move and turn all the time, and I was fright-
fully irritable and nervous. My mind was
in a constant fear of some trouble happening,
and restless with a dread that could not be
allayed. As nearly as I can describe it, I
felt as I think a criminal would feel who
was in momentary expectation of being ar-
rested by the police, knowing at the same
time all escape was impossible.”
As the reporter listened to Mrs. Carter’s
vivid description of one of the severest symp-
toms of nervous disorder, and noted her
present ample and matronly figure, hearty
with vigour and good health, her experience
appeared even more extraordinary than it
does when set out in cold type.
“D d you consult a medical man on your
own behalf, Mrs. Carter 1 ” enquired the
reporter.
“ Luckily, I did not,” replied Mrs. Carter;
“my troubl s, though severe, were of short
duration ; for, knowing the virtues of
Clements Tonic, I was quick to try that
remedy, and, as in the case of my children,
the effect on my sy s em was rapid and magical.
My heart ceased to trouble me by throbbing,
my indigestion gave place to good appetite, I
slept well, losing the fearful mind worry ;
and my chest and lungs, that had been sore
to suffocation with the r morseless cough, be-
came eased and quiet; and the stabbing
pain in the back between the shoulders never
came again.”
“ And how long was Clements Tonic in
effecting this great change ?"
“ Only a few days. You will remember
I took it very quickly after my trouble
began, and no doubt this hastened my cure,
saving me much pain and expense.”
“ There is a great moral lesson to be
drawn from what you have told me, Mrs.
Carter—a lesson that should go to the heart
of every mother and suffering woman in
the land. May I tell them ?”
“ If you really think so, and wish to pub-
lish what I have said,” replied Mrs. Carter,
“ you are quite welcome to do so, and in
any way you may deem best.”

Fishin’.
(Beino the true story of a valued Bulletin bard
at present laid up.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
T am writing this in bed,
With a bandage round my head,
A leg in splints, a dislocated toe ;
And my face together tacked,
1 11 a style not too exact—
And all because a-fishin 5 1 would go.
I am living in Balmain,
Where they blast out street and lane,
And houses roost on ledges where they wish ;
And I thought it would be fine
To go forth and throw a line,
And show the local anglers how to fish.
I was standing on the edge
Of an old, brown sandstone ledge,
And flung the line with such enthusiasm
That I flung myself also
From the ledge—a mile or so
I think it was in depth, that awful chasm.
That’s why I write in bed,
With a bandage round my h ad,
A leg in splints, a dislocated toe;
And my face together tacked,
In a style not too exact —
And all because a-fishin’ I would go.
I’ve had warning, I must own,
Just to let this sport alone :
It’s often brought me close to Kingdom Come.
Once I went with Pat Magee ;
We inverted in the sea
And nearly drowned—and lost a jar of rum.
I was wrecked at George’s Head,
And they gave me up for dead,
Until they poured some whisky down my t’ roat.
Then, of course, I woke and swore
I would ne’er go fishin’ more—
And next day I was fishin’ from a boat!
I have choked beneath the wave—
Where I thought to find a grave—
And felt the ocean seething in my brain.
I have seen the bubbles rise,
Many-hued like shining eyes—
And after all have come to life again.
I have lost a fortune, too,
For the lady loved me true,
And said she’d make me rich when we were wed;
And I promised I would meet
Her one evening fair and sweet,
But joined a fishin’ company instead.
I suppose it’s in the blood ;
For I used to grope in mud
For worms, and get most shockingly defiler l ,
And my father, so ’tis said,
Used to take his bait to bed
When he, too, was a young and guileless child.
There are men who charge their Fall,
And their going to the wall,
To drink, or cards, or evil company—
But I’ve no such fine excuse
For my journey to the deuce :
’Twas fishin’—simply fishin’—ruined me.

corro ‘tly that the late YV. T. Campbell, of the Bega Free
1 rkss, died in Bega, Hospital. Mr. Campbell died at the

A revolting tale from Liverpool, N.S.W. :
Editor Bulletin.— Re the rapid increase in cancer and
other horrors. Up here we have emptying into George’s
iuver the drainage from the woolwash, from the Sydney
paper-mills, and from the railway station latrines; and
from the Benevolent Asylum wash-house, where are
washed the clothes from the consumptive ward, and were
was.led, until a few weeks back, the clothes from the can-
cer ward. Also, there are at present floating about a
couple of dead pigs, and some time back there was taken
u , r ' ver wpse of a suicided cancer patient,
whi h had been for days in the water. No wonder that
the sand has 6in. of black slime on it, and that the water
would almost pass for ink. Yet right from this part of
the river they are every day carting away the alleged
sand for use, I ani informed, upon or adjacent to the
works of the Sydney water-supply ? As for the Benevo-
' en f' Asylum itself, there is no urinal for the cancer-ward,
and the water from the baths flows into a tank, and is
thence pumped over ground accessible to cattle—a dairy
being close by. The nightsoil also is thrown into a pit
and left uncovered, and the cotton-lint used by cancer
patients is left lying around for the sparrows to use for
nesting purposes. That they do so use it, I have had
ocular demonstration.—Locus.
“0.P.” discourseth of public liars :
No sensible nation ever expected itself to think to any
great extent, because a thinking animal can’t possibly be
a working animal, and vice versa , and it takes so much
work to hunt up the necessary food and rent and things
that most people have no time left for anything else. To
meet the case was invented the thing called dailv-journal-
lsni, which undertook to do the most of the public’s
thinking and supply the result on paper at a low figure.
But it soon started ham-shops, importing-houses, and
other things and began to tell the poor old public that
ham-pies were good for indigestion, and an open port
good for the country, and that the old public himself was
the backbone of the country—all because the poor old
chap was soft, and it wanted him to buy its things at a
high figure and look pleasant. And the fool-public
believed it and did what it told him, and it got
fatter and wealthier and he got thinner and poorer,
an i- . wenfc on for 80 many years telling lies and
getting fatter and wealthier and developing a dislike for
poor thin persons, that untruthfulness and fat and a dis-
like for p.t. persons became constitutional with it. So
the small splinter of conscience that had pricked it occa-
sionally in the older and thinner days vanished so utterly
* P?°l' le now believe it ever had such a thing.
And the things that daily journalism has got to telling
poor thin persons now are so false, and mean, and mis-
leading, and the cause of so much poverty and ignorance
a.nd crime, that d has become necessary to suppress the
said daily journalism if only for the sake of the children.
1 consequently propose that any person making in print
or in a public place a statement of any kind which is sub-
sequently proven to be' false shall be imprisoned for not
less than six months. This would apply also to the plat-
™™ ancator ’ e “ guinea-pig and his prospectus,
lb® Madame Palnuste ” (alias Murphy, of Surry Hills)
the our reporter” who procures the “ Remarkable
Kouser testimonial from the woman in the “picturesque”
house, the huckster whose goods are “ absolutely below
cost, and other frauds too numerous to mention. But
worst of all is the affluent “great daily” that urges the
toiler to vote for the wrong man, misrepresents and libels
tne toflers best friends, and persuades him to desert the
missus and kids and lay down his life for his worst enemies.
