CHEAP AND FASHIONABLE TAILORING.
PJ G. HATTE, of King-street, Newtown (near Sydney,
N.S. W.), has recently made great additions and im-
provements to his Tailoring Department, which is now one
of the features of his extensive business. He receives regu-
lar shipments, from his Home buyers, of all the newest and
most fashionable goods, and has one of the smartest cutters
in the colony. Everything is made to measure on the pre-
mises; a perfect fit is guaranteed, and patrons may rely on
every satisfaction being given. Self-measurement forms
and patterns sent free by post. Hatte’ssuitsof Indigoserge,
all-wool tweeds, and sergerettes (with extra pair of trousers
given in) for 425. are the marvel of tailoring and the talk of
Australasia. For 60s. Hatte makes a suit of tweed or serge
fit for a prince. Inspect or send for patterns of his new
woollens, comprising vicunas, West of England, Scotch and
Blarney tweeds, at 5/s. 6d, and 055.; the great value offered.
Hatte makes a smart Chesterfield overcoat from meltons,
vicunas, and Venetians from 455., and the new paddock
coat, with full back, side vents, crescent pockets, silk-faced
and velvet collar for 665. Hatte’s speciality is in dress-
suits, starting at £3 35., made of purple dye elastic coating
with silk-facing. For excellence of material, perfect fit,
latest fashion, and superior workmanship, Hatte is un-
equalled, whilst his charges are at least 30 per cent, under
those of ordinary city tailors.

A NYTHING which causes a drain on the nerves helps to
ft- make a SUSPENSORY necessary. Excessive mental
effort, prolonged and exhaustive physical exercise, over-
indulgence in any way, dissipation, irregularity of diet,
loss of sleep, anything which produces fatigue has its
first effect on the vital organs. A sick man, or a tired
man, has not much vita! vigor, the strain tells on the
most delicate nerves first, fatigue there produces fatigue
everywhere. Fatigue of the vital organs leaves a man
with little energy, with little strength to work or think.
The connection between the brain and the vital organs
is most direct. Excessive mental work affects the vital
power, and abuse of the vital power affects the brain —a
strain on one is a strain on both. A SUSPENSORY stops
one strain on one. It is not a big strain, but it is there
all the time : it is. one little strain multiplied by the
number of seconds in the day. It is a continual drag,
which empties the store of vitality just as a reservoir may
be emptied drop by drop.

really mean is an

Getting Directions.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Directions, as given by the average bushman, so
befog the traveller generally that he has rnoie
trouble in keeping the road by their aid than he
would have bad without them. Bushy describes
the road as he last saw it, which might bo last
week or last year, giving a rigmarole of details
concerning the turns and hollows, the big tree, the
dog-leg fence, and the black stump; and while
he’3 telling all this he’s sketching out the map of
it in the dust with a stick. Loaded with this infor-
mation you start, and perhaps you’ll sight some-
thing that he missed, and that uncomfortable lost
feeling of the lost one comes over you at once. If
you don’t see the bogged cow, or the dead tree
(which has probably fallen down), the feeling
increases. Presently all the little details get
mixed up till you don’t know anything, and you’re
in perpetual misery till you get to the end of those
directions —and get a fresh batch to lie on your
mind like a soddened doughboy.
A bushman forgets that nothing should be
mentioned that is not absolutely necessary
for the traveller to know, such as well-traversed
branch roads and striking land marks. Bogged
cows and dead horses are not land-marks, except
for the time being. Bushy often overlooks the
fact that many objects that most impress him
change with the effluxion of time. Old Micky, of
Crowland, was one of these. He was working on
a road when an acquaintance happened along.
“Good day, Peter,” said he, “an’ where are ye
goin’ ? ”
“Well, Micky, I want to go to Bindelby, but I
don’t quite know the way.”
“Nuthin’ easier. Keep straight along the road
and yer won’t miss it.”
“ Is there only one road, Micky ? ”
“ Ye’ll find one enough to take ye to Bindelby.
Maybe ye’ll want another to bring yer back, seein’
as how it takes some people a mint o’ time to go to
even a little place. They wear the road out goin’
there.”
“But, Micky, I’m in a hurry. There’s no turn-
offs?”
“Well, yes, a few. But you stick to this road,
till ye see it run into a gate. Go through that—
you’d better open it first, though. It swings to
yer right when ye’re pullin’ it to yer. Shut it.
Then turn yer horse round an’ keep after the road
agin. It wriggles a lot, but you stick to it, an’
yer’ll find it’s a road to be depended on. I’ve
known it a good many years now, an’ I’ve never
seen it take anybody anywhere yet but to Bindelby
an’ away from it. Nevermind the branch roads—
keep off ’em, or they’ll take yer astray.”
Well, are there any particular landmarks ? ”
“ Lemme see. Ye’ll cross a gully runnin’ like a
stranded eel. There’s a spotted bullock feedin’
alongside it. I'hat’s fiije mile. Next, ye’ll see a
kangaroo sittin’ bolt upright on yer left. That’s
eiyht mile. Then ye’ll see a big hole on yer right.
But don’t give yer horse a drink there. There’s
no water in it. . . . That’s halfway. Next, ye’ll
come to a lot of trees on a hill. There’s some
parrots on the outside one.”
“ When were you along there, Micky ?”
“Three years ago.” Micky spoke quite seri-
ously. “Byan’bye, ye’ll strike a big flat, with a
dead dog on it. Three miles from that, there’s a
house with two chimneys, an’ smoke cornin’ out of
’em. That’s Bindelby .
* * • •
The mileage of some unpegged roads varies in a
perplexing manner. Tell a traveller the distance
to Busters pub., for instance, and he’ll ask if
they’re horse-miles or foot-miles. I asked a
how far it was to Xainhounno. Oil a
station-horse, five miles; on a traveller s horse,
seven miles ; on foot, it’s 10 miles ; and, if you re
hungry and tired, it’ll be about 15. I’ve known it
to be about 17, but that doesn’t happen often.’ ”
* • * *
One day I was going to McSweeney’s, and
asked a inintenance-man where I must turn off
the road to reach it.
“D’yer know that track that turns off t other
side o’ the tea-tree swamp ? ”
“ Yes.”
“ That’s not it. There’s a red hill bit further
along with a stump on it. The stump’s got a
piece knocked off the side where a waggon hit it.
I think it was Dooley’s waggon. 1 did hear
there used to be a stone just beyond it, but I
b’lieve Dooley picked that up last time he was
along to shy at Finnegan’s bull. He’s an ugly
brute, is Finnegan’s bull, an’ he’s nearly always
about that red hill. I remember ”
“ Yes ; but about the road to McSweeney’s.”
“ There’s a track turns off just over the red hill.
That goes to Denehy’s selection.”
“ And where does McSweeney’s turn off ? ”
“Two miles past Denehy’s road you’ll see
where Nolan was bogged with a load of timber
last week. Nolan keeps a pub in Coraki. I’ve
seen the time when Nolan ”
“ Oh, never mind Nolan! McSweeney’s, man! ”
“ I’m cornin’ to McSweeney’s. When you pass
the mud-hole you’ll see a road runnin’ like this.”
He squatted down and commenced to perplex
me with the bushman’s inevitable mud-map,
drawing a line with his finger to represent the
main road, others to represent branch roads,
parallel and intersecting lines for fences, dots for
trees and houses—some three or four miles off the
road, and all hopelessly out of sight. Then he
told me to turn off at an ironbark tree that had a
limb sticking out like this (another map), and pass
a log lying like this (another map), when the road
takes a turn and runs like that (more map).
“You’ll have all straight sailin’ then.”
I rode off. It was 27 miles. I found the place
and delivered my message to the “missus,” as
“ Mac. was away workin’ on the roads.” I re-
turned next day, and as I passed the maintenance-
man he asked, “ Did ye find McSweeney’s ? ”
“I did. Mac. wasn’t at home, though.”
“ Did ye want to see him ? ”
“I did. I had an important letter for him.
Left it with the missus.”
“ Damn it,” said he. “Tm McSv/eeney ! ”
EDWARD S. SORENSON.
Raskin’s kindness and simple honesty are
. ~ , . . , ,
shown in a letter he wrote long ago to his coach-
man, who, by the way, states that all the old ser-
V ants of the family have been amply provided for :
“Dear David,—Here is your cheque at last. I never
g- e t half my day’s letters answered, and they get pushed

has come at last, and I begin to get through my business.

Elsewhere!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“So you’ve come to take my place,” said the old
man, shaking off a Sunday afternoon doze. “ Well,
my time ain’t up till the end of the month, but
you’re welcome to the job, and I hope you’ll like
it.”
“Anything is better than the road,” said the
stranger, dropping his swag on the spare bunk.
“ I’m sick and tired of that. I want rest.”
“ And because you wanted a rest you accepted a
job from a cockatoo ; you might as well jump into
a river to avoid a shower of rain,” said the old
man. “ These cockatoos are just budding into
landed aristocrats. They are self-made men, and
have felt what graft is ; get a man like that, and
he’s the hardest boss on the face of God’s earth.”
The stranger swore wearily, and brushed away
some road dust.
“ Now, take this place; I’ve tried a few, and
it’s a fair sample. I won’t go through the whole
programme —I haven’t time, but you start work
with lighting the fire for the slavey in the morn-
ing. They send you out fencing and mustering
during the day, and the country about here has
been set on edge so that cockatoos could count
their acres on both sides of it. I suppose when
you heard about shepherding you thought you
were going to ride about and whistle to a dog. If
I know anything about it you’ll be going on all-
fours yourself, and doing most of your own bark-
ing. If you doubt it look at that hill.”
The stranger paused in unbuckling his swag,
rose, and went to the door. The hill was steep—
so steep that in places the earth and stones had
slipped away and left nothing but bare rock.
“ And the boss, he is as self-opinionated as the
rest of his kind. He thinks you can’t set a post in
straight, or put a mob of sheep through a gate
without him. If he’s seen a dead sheep or lost a
cow it will start him off; if you can’t see through
a hill he’ll call you blind ; if you can’t hear a mile
against the wind you’re deaf ; he’ll sit in the shade
and watch you struggle up hill with an eight-foot
strainer and call you short-winded, and tell you of
the bigger loads he’s carried. And like the rest of
them he’s a schemer, he is. He’ll have a boundary
fence down at knock-off time and keep you till
dark to finish it. He’s never satisfied; if you
shifted that hill in a day, he’d want to know why
you hadn’t shifted the other one.”
“ They’re all the same way,” said the stranger.
“ All cockatoos are,” answered the old man,
“ but it’s different among the stations. There the
boss gives you the orders in the morning, perhaps
to mend a fence or shift sheep, and you’re done by
three o’clock. Then it’s too late to go for other
orders, so you camp around until five and go home.
I’d sooner fifteen bob from a station than thirty
from a cockatoo. I wonder now you didn’t try
down there, and this is just the time. A young
fellow like you is sure of a job. And there’s a
home for life.”
“Think so?” said the stranger. His face was
turned down the valley now, and he was looking
at the last, distant windings of the road.
“ Think so ! ” answered the old man. “ Why,
ain’t it just where I’m going; but come on, it’s
cow-time. I’ll show you round.” ;
“ You needn’t trouble,” said the stranger,
coming back from the door and buckling-uphis
swag-straps. “You can tell your boss I’ve
changed my mind. Solong ! ”
» * * *
“No, there’s not much chance for an outsider,”
said the station-hand sympathetically. “ You’ve
got to be known, or have a friend who is known,
or is second cousin by marriage to the owners in
town before you can get on here, and there’s not
much in it anyway. Fifteen shillings a week and
find your own horses and dogs isn’t much fori
shepherding, and the tucker they give you is star-
vation—no milk, no butter, only bread, mutton,
potatoes and tea, and, if you’re lucky, a dead
weight duff on Sundays, mostly stale fat, raisin-
stones, and grit. No, it’s up among the cockatoos
you’ll strike the good things now. A mate of
mine is up there. He says he has cows to milk,
but he knocks off early for that, and he gets after-
noon tea and cake when he’s in the sheep-yards.
He’s never expected to work on a holiday, if he
does he’s thanked for it. And the missus is very
good to liim, too, he says—does his washing, gets
up his white shirts and all, and at the end of the
year the boss gives him a five-pound bonus over
his regular wages. I’d try up there if I were
you.”
But the stranger didn’t. He had been there
already.
J. WEST.
A Railway Episode.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Picture a railway yard at the top of a steep slope,
a siding crammed with trucks, an engine shunting.
An extra-heavy bump, and an unbraked truck at
the far end flies out the end of the siding and off
down hill. A fall of 10 miles, the passenger train
coming up and no means of warning it. The truck
must be caught. The shunter j umps on the engine-
buffers, lengthens the screw-coupling and sits ready
while the driver throws the valve open and with a
rush they are after the runaway. The engine
catches up and the shunter reaches out to drop the
coupling over the hook as the buffers touch. The
buffers press together, then the engine seems to
stop, while with added force and speed the truck
flies on. He has missed and now with white scared
face is holding to the couplings with energy bom
of fear.
In dropping the coupling he has overbalanced
and has only been saved from a fearful death by a
blind, instinctive clutch. A moment he watches
the sleepers that seem to grin at him as they fly
under, then a rousing curse from the driver brings
him to himself. Again he is ready and the engine,
with every pound of steam on, is at top speed.
The passenger-train cannot now be far away, and
this time there must be no mistake. Again they
draw near the truck, the shunter holds out the
coupling, and with careful eye watches his time.
The buffers touch and down goes the coupling.
This time there has been no mistake. “ All
right! ”he yells. On go the brakes with a screech
and as they pull up, the shunter lifts himself and
looks ahead. There, rounding the curve a quarter
of a mile away is the “ passenger.”

The Game of Hoodman Blind.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
For them no blatant brasses swell,
Their deeds no sculptures show ;
Their stirring stories none oan tell,
Their names no singers know;
The men who fought the wars and foil.
And never struck a blow.
Three tiers of oars, with steady stroke
They swept through Salamis ;
They shortened sail through cannons’ croak
And biting bullets’ hiss ;
Black, blistered, blind, to-day they stoke
Through battle as it is.
They shared the pride of Grecian days,
The laurels Caesar wore,
The brilliance of the Viking frays—
The boast of British lore—
Who paved the widest water-ways
And battled with the oar.
With straightened backs and bended knees—
Fierce force in every form—
They learned to flout the Arctic breeze,
The sand-blast whirling warm ;
To slip the ship through silky seas
And drag her through the storm.
They heard the stamping overhead,
The shrieks of those who fell ;
They felt the bumping of the dead
Upon the rythmic swell;
But •> -ked in silence that she sped
Alon 0 the water well.
Though decks had slimy grown with gore,
And frenzied blows fell wide ;
Though gleaming beaks through timbers tore,
And bleeding blades were plied ;
They swung their oar and pulled their oar.
And cleared their oar—and died 1
They manned the yards in Nelson’s fleet
To furl the flapping sail;
They scorned the shell-burst ’neath their feet,
The spinning chain-shot’s wail;
Their thoughts to make the reef-points meet
And fight throughout the gale.
They looked not on the tops ablaze
With volleys from the foe ;
They stirred not from their task to gaze
Where blood bsgan to flow ;
Nor paused when through the smoky haze
Their comrades dropped below.
To-day in grime and grease they bide,
Bereft of sound and sight;
The flush of victory denied,
The tremor of the flight,
Until the green sea bursts th e side
To whelm them in its might.
They know not how the battles go
Within their steel recess.
Their post and power they may not know ;
Their fate they may not guess;
Their duty but to wait below
Anear the battle’s stress.
No pomp and panoply have they,
No cheering crowds they find;
But, fixed as targets through the fray,
Their ordered work they mind
Salute the hearts unseen that play
The Game of Hoodman Blind !
BONDINGIE.
Only Five!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In the raw and sullen light of dawn he stood at
last outside the portal of the Lion and the Key at
Darlinghurst. Was it indeed but five years since
he had entered there t —or was it fifty ? No ! —her
words—the last he had heard from her —came
clear and fresh to him again from the phonograph
of a devoted memory.
“Five years, Jim —only five! And when you
come out I’ll marry you ! So, keep up your heart
and remember .”
The rest of the sentence had not been for official
ears, but Maitland had been at no loss to supply
it for himself. “ Remember it was to shield
her ! ” he muttered, as he waved aside a Prison-
Gate Salvationist. “Brother!” the man had
called him, but he had now no brotherhood,
he told himself, with men ungaoled and unde-
graded. Still, it was for her sake ; and, though he
called himself no martyr, he could not help feel-
ing, by the hungry relish with which he plunged
into the already fast-pulsing life of Oxford-street,
that he had bitterly earned the promised reward.
The vile racket of the trams, even, made melody
to his prison-starved ear; to his stone-surfeited
eye came with delightful easement she small, far
glimpse of the Park’s verdure that closed the vista
of the bustling morning street; his own very voice,
as he hailed a cab, rang strangely—thus raised—
upon his silence-sated hearing. Well, once more,
it had been ail for her sake ; and, though she had
never come to see him, she had, of course, her
relatives to consider. That was what she would
be telling him in another ten minutes, for was he
not speeding towards her now !
• • * *
“Miss Brackenbury?” The crustaceous old
lady took off her glasses, the more narrowly to
inspect this unduly impatient young man. “ It is
nearly three years since Miss Brackenbury left
here. She did not, I am sorry to say, conduct
herself towards me with that—er—consideration
which—”
“Three years! Where did she go? Where’s
she now f Pardon me, madam ’’—noting the
dowager’s astonished glare—“ but, you see, I am
so very anxious ! ”
“ So I should be disposed to imagine. And, as
to where the young person —sniff ! —is at present,
I should say—sniff, sniff !—she is, mo3t likely,
wherever her husband is ! ”
For a moment, Maitland’s pale face took on an
additional whiteness ; but then came a flush, and
ne laughed. “ Husband ?—Nellie Braokenbury’s
husband ! Oh, that don’t ‘go ’! Exouse mo—but
surely ”
In his nervous eagerness he forgot to choose his
words ; for,' though he assured himself mentally
that the thing was perfectly ridiculous, the mere
possibility had closed like a cold hand upon his
heart.
“An acquaintance with vulgar slang is not
amongst my accomplishments”—the exasperated
ancient pointed to the door —“ but if, as I surmise,
you intend to convey that I am in error, let me
recommend you to make a call at 53, Denmark-
street, Darlinghurst! ”
. “ Darlinghurst ? ” repeated Maitland, after a
vicious door-slam had nearly shot him down the
steps. “Then she was closer at hand than I
thought. She wanted to be as near me as she
could—God bless her ! ” and he laughed so hys-
terically, as he got into his vehicle again, that the
cabman, who had himself “done time,” took a
curious survey of him through the trap.
“Bit balmy, I reckon ! —an’ no wonder, if lie’s
just out o’ chokey. Seen him cornin’ across from
the Jug. It’s a ’ell of a thing, is ‘solitry,’ for
shovin’ a bloke off his bloomin’ chump ! ”
* * * *
“ Miss Brackenbury ? No ; she don’t live ’ere,”
the pert little serving-wench piped up at Mait-
land. “But there’s a lydy of that nyme comes
’ere sometimes ; Mrs. Manderson’s sister, she is.
Kin you see Mrs. Manderson ? Gome inside, an’
I’ll ask ’er.”
A couple of minutes’ wait, and then, at the
rustle of a dress, Maitland turned.
“ I beg your—why, Nellie, it’s you ! The girl
said you were not here. I’ve only just this morn-
ing got ”
With a warning hand she stopped both his
speech and the glad advance he was making
towards where she stood, with something in her
face that struck him now like a blow.
She was a plump young woman ; blonde and
creamy, after the sweetly lymphatic type. But
there’s nothing like lymph for an awkward situa-
tion, and her voice was musically equable as she
said that she had expected him, one of these days
—and wouldn’t he sit down ? In a half-stunned
kind of way the man took a chair, and waited
while the canary at the window executed a short
ear-piercing mockery of jubilation.
“ Five years, Jim, is a long time.”
“Isit ? ” He looked stupidly up at her. “Five
years ago you didn’t think so. ‘ only five, Jim ! ’
was what you said.”
“ Five years ago I was a fool! ”
Her left hand went up to her cheek, and Mait-
land’s eye, as she intended, caught the gleam of
gold upon the fourth finger. He drew himself
together as one who had received a bullet ; then
got up and stood gripping the chair-back till it
creaked. But his voice was quiet.
“ The Miss Brackenbury the girl spoke of ? ”
“ Is my sister.”
“ I see. And you are ”
“ Mrs. Manderson.” She looked him steadily
in the face. “ Reproaches will be useless, Jim ! ”
“Oh, yes,” he assented in a lifeless kind of way.
“Of course ; quite useless. Mrs. Tom Mander-
son, may I ask.”
“ Yes ; you know my—husband ? ”
“ I know your husband —yes ! And he knows
me. But the question is ”—his voice recovered its
tone as suddenly as from the releasing of a spring
—does he know you ? Does he know, to begin
with, that you had promised to be my wife as soon
as I came out of—that place ? ”
“ Yes ! ” She was deadly pale, but her mouth
was set like steel and her eyes were narrowed
almost to slits as she fronted him unflinchingly.
“He knows all about that; but he knows also, of
course, that such promises are often broken.”
“Under such conditions? ”
She settled herself as for a final effort. “To
what conditions do you allude ? ” she said.
Maitland’s jaw actually dropped with the
extremity of his amaze. Then, with the reaction,
it snapp 'd sharply up again. “ I see !” he said—
“l see! Well —good morning to you, Mrs.
Manderson ! ”
She passed suddenly between him and the door.
“Wait!” she said. “Wait, Mr. Maitland—
Jim ! —what are you going to do ? ”
“ Mr. Maitland is going to do nothing but get
as far away from such a woman as possible. And
there isn’t any ‘ Jim ! ” There was a Jim, once; ”
he stared as if at a picture of the past in the
crown of his hat—“a miserable, God-forgotten
idiot of a Jim, who confessed to a theft he had
never committed, because the thief was—the girl
he loved. The girl who said she loved him / The
girl who was to wait ” —he broke into a hoarsely
horrible shout of laughter that made the woman
start back and set the canary wildly dashing round
its cage—“the girl who was to wait till he had
got through with her punishment! ‘ Only five
years, Jim ! —only five years ! ’ ” —and he laughed
again till the chandelier trembled above him.
Shaken at last out of her bowelless stedfastness,
the woman laid a hasty hand upon his arm
“ Hush ! Take care ! People will think you arc
mad ! ” Mad ! —the word gave her a suggestion.
“You are mad, to say such things! Ah !”
forcing back her equanimity as the door opened—
“ here is my husband. You know Mr. Maitland
Tom, don’t you ? ”
The big, rough-bearded man in the doorway was
in no hurry to acknowledge the acquaintance.
“Hum—yes!—l did know him a little; but 1
scarcely expected the honor of a call. And, ii
that was his laughing I heard as I came down
stairs, why, all I can say is that he is in astonish-
ingly good spirits, considering ”
“That he’s just come out of gaol! ” Maitland pul
in. “ Yes, Tom Manderson, you needn’t remind
me ! I’m a pariah now, I know—and there’s not
much danger of my being allowed to forget it—in
a Christian community ! That’s why L was laugh-
ing at myself—for being such a drivelling imbecile
as to have expected the former Nellie Bracken
bury, here, to keep a certain promise about whic
she says she has told you.”
“Oh, yes ! ” Manderson sat down and put two
magisterial thumbs into the arm-holes of his waist
coat. “ I don’t wish to be hard on you, of course ;
but, as you very properly admit, the idea wa
simply preposterous. And—though, to be sure,
it’s never too late to mend— ”
“ Don’t!—for God’s sake ! ” Maitland winced
at the platitude, which seemed to seal his thence
forth social status. “Anything but that sort o
stuff! I’ll have no pious slobbering over Jim
Maitland, yet! ”
Manderson’s scanty and mechanical human!
tarianism gave out. His face reddened furiously
as he jumped up and flung open the door. “Oh .
—if that’s the way you talk ! ” —a wave of hi.-
hand said the rest.
Into Maitland’s pallid face came the intense
whiteness of sudden rage. “Fool!” he began,
struggling desperately with the words. “ Self-
righteous ass ! —if you only knew ”
Mrs. Manderson, with a swift meeting of the
apprehended situation, got quickly behind her hus-
band. “Don’t take any notice of what he may
say, Tom ! I see what’s the matter now ; the
poor fellow’s head’s affected 1 He’s perfectly
crazy ! ”
She thought she was lying—but she wasn’t! for,
when Maitland had stopped speaking, something
had seemed to give way in his brain. In a dazed
kind of way, now, he quietly took his chair again,
put his hand to the top of his head, and sat there,
quietly smiling, until a constable was brought to
take him away.
* • * •
To furnish for this yam a regulation ending,
there ought to have been in Callan Park or
Gladesville a much-demented person who should
for years have oonfined his utterances to the
mysterious observation “ Only five ! ” and should,
upon his death-bed, have regained his reason and
told his tale. Maitland, however, as a matter of
cold fact, recovered his sanity inside twelve
mouths, and now relates, as here set forth, a story
which it is only fair to say no man of the world

A Northern Territory trooper heard the
other day that a certain “ wanted ” nigger was at
a Chinese camp about 13 miles away, so he
despatched two of his own blackboys to catch the
darkey aforesaid. Next morning accordingly they
trotted up to tho station with a Chinaman in tow.
“ What’s this?” asked the trooper,_ “where’s the
darkie ? ” “ Oh,” said the boys, “ him no more sit
down alonga that one place; so we bin bringum
this one Chinaman.” _ “But what did you bring
him for?” “Oh, him bin sellum opium longa
blackfellow.” “ Did you see him sell the opium ?”
asked the trooper. “No; no bin see_ um, but
mine plenty savee him all day sellum opium longa
blackfellow.” The trooper had his doubts and let
the Chow go. John didn’t even growl, but con-
tcmtedly walked the 13 miles home again. The
Chinaman is the only practical philosopher.
